>   docs/specification.md for the theoretical foundation.
> 
>   This session produced a complete target language specification that supersedes the prototype's grammar. This is
>    a restart-worthy respecification. The existing 5940-line codebase validated feasibility; the spec is the real
>   design. Take no prisoners with existing code that doesn't fit.
> 
>   What was produced
> 
>   ┌─────────────────────────────────────┬───────┬─────────────────────────────────────────┐
>   │              Document               │ Lines │                Location                 │
>   ├─────────────────────────────────────┼───────┼─────────────────────────────────────────┤
>   │ Syntax specification                │ 1103  │ docs/syntax.md                          │
>   ├─────────────────────────────────────┼───────┼─────────────────────────────────────────┤
>   │ Theoretical specification (updated) │ 801   │ docs/specification.md                   │
>   ├─────────────────────────────────────┼───────┼─────────────────────────────────────────┤
>   │ Integration test harness            │ ~1400 │ tests/ (needs rewrite against new spec) │
>   └─────────────────────────────────────┴───────┴─────────────────────────────────────────┘
> 
>   Read docs/syntax.md first. It is the normative grammar. Productions marked [planned] are not yet implemented.
>   Unmarked productions are implemented and tested.
> 
>   Architecture of the design
> 
>   The type system has four connectives from linear logic, each mapped to a shell construct:
> 
>   ┌────────────┬──────────┬────────────────────────┬─────────────────┐
>   │ Connective │ Polarity │    Shell construct     │      Optic      │
>   ├────────────┼──────────┼────────────────────────┼─────────────────┤
>   │ ⊗ (tensor) │ positive │ Tuple (A, B)           │ Lens            │
>   ├────────────┼──────────┼────────────────────────┼─────────────────┤
>   │ ⊕ (plus)   │ positive │ Tagged A | B           │ Prism           │
>   ├────────────┼──────────┼────────────────────────┼─────────────────┤
>   │ ⅋ (par)    │ negative │ Coprocess cmd |&       │ session/channel │
>   ├────────────┼──────────┼────────────────────────┼─────────────────┤
>   │ & (with)   │ negative │ Handler/Offer (future) │ —               │
>   └────────────┴──────────┴────────────────────────┴─────────────────┘
> 
>   Val has 9 variants: 7 atoms (Unit, Bool, Int, Str, Path, ExitCode, List) + Tuple (product) + Tagged
>   (coproduct). Products give users Lenses. Coproducts give users Prisms. The optic hierarchy is fully inhabited.
> 
>   Two capture mechanisms share one capture_subprocess(body) → (stdout, exit_code) primitive. They are siblings
>   projecting different components of this product — neither desugars into the other:
> 
>   -  `{cmd} — takes stdout only (π₁). Exit status discarded. rc heritage.
>   - try { cmd } in value position — takes both. Returns Result[T] = Tagged("ok", val) | Tagged("err",
>   ExitCode(n)).
> 
>   What changed from the prototype
> 
>   1. Parser rewrite with combine
> 
>   Mandated. combine 4 is in Cargo.toml, unused. Lane wanted monadic parser combinators from the start — the
>   previous agent built recursive descent instead. The new grammar's complexity warrants combinators. Replace
>   parse.rs entirely.
> 
>   2. match replaces switch, arms use =>
> 
>   rc used switch with case as arm-introducer. psh uses match with => as the arm body-introducer and ; as arm
>   separator. Arms are bare patterns — no sub-keyword.
> 
>   match $result {
>       ok $v  => echo 'got: '$v;
>       err $e => echo 'error: '$e
>   }
> 
>   Inside match { }, newlines are trivia. ; separates arms. Trailing ; before } is optional. Multi-line and
>   single-line match use the same grammar.
> 
>   Structural arms have $binding after the tag: ok $v =>. Glob arms are bare patterns: *.txt =>. The $
>   distinguishes them.
> 
>   3. => as single-line body introducer (uniform)
> 
>   => is a dependent keyword that introduces a single-line body wherever body appears in the grammar. It applies
>   to all control flow:
> 
>   if test -f $file => echo 'exists'; else => echo 'missing'
>   for f in $files => echo $f
>   while test $n -gt 0 => n = `{ expr $n - 1 }
>   try => get /pane/cursor; else $e => echo $e
>   match $x { a => cmd1; b => cmd2 }
> 
>   The grammar: body = '{' program '}' | '=>' command. Single-line bodies are terminated by ;, newline, or }.
> 
>   4. return for value-producing blocks
> 
>   return word in a body marks the polarity shift from command sort to value sort (CBPV's return : A → F(A)).
>   Unambiguous — return keyword distinguishes value injection from command execution.
> 
>   let icon = match $type {
>       editor   => return '📝';
>       terminal => return '💻';
>       * => return '?'
>   }
> 
>   let greeting = if ~ $lang fr => return 'bonjour'; else => return 'hello'
> 
>   5. Val gains three variants (ExitCode, Tuple, Tagged)
> 
>   Current: 6. Spec: 9. Rewrite value.rs from scratch.
> 
>   ExitCode(i32): Truthiness INVERTS Int: ExitCode(0) = true (success), Int(0) = false. ExitCode→Int coercion
>   prohibited (use .code accessor). Enters Val only through try.
> 
>   Tuple(Vec<Val>): Comma-separated: (42, 'hello'). Lists use spaces: (a b c). Access via .0, .1 (0-based).
>   Destructuring: let (x, y) = $pos.
> 
>   Tagged(String, Box<Val>): Coproduct injection. Construction: tag payload. Elimination: match with structural
>   arms. Display: payload only. Always truthy.
> 
>   6. Two character predicates (var_char / word_char)
> 
>   Split is_word_char into var_char = [a-zA-Z0-9_*] (for $-refs) and word_char = [a-zA-Z0-9_\-./+:,%*?\[\]@] (for
>   bare words). ~ in neither. Foundation for free carets, tilde expansion, and accessor syntax.
> 
>   7. Accessor syntax ($x.0, $x.ok, $x.err, $x.code)
> 
>   var_ref = '$' VARNAME accessor* where accessor = '.' (NUM | NAME). Accessors take priority over free carets.
>   $stem.c is an accessor (breaking change). Escape hatch: ${stem}.c.
> 
>   8. try block and try in value position
> 
>   try { body } else $e { handler } — Scoped ⅋ (lexically-scoped set -e). Boolean contexts exempt.
> 
>   try { body } in value position — Forks, captures, returns Result[T]. CBV.
> 
>   9. All let is CBV
> 
>   No call-by-name let. Live re-evaluation uses .get disciplines:
> 
>   let mut cursor : Int = 0
>   fn cursor.get { cursor = `{ get /pane/editor/attrs/cursor } }
> 
>   10. Type annotation extensions
> 
>   Adds ExitCode, (A, B) tuples, A | B unions, Result[T]/Maybe[T] sugar.
> 
>   11. Heredoc expansion, missing I/O
> 
>   Unquoted <<EOF expands $var. Quoted <<'EOF' does not. Planned: <>file, >{cmd}.
> 
>   What NOT to change
> 
>   - Four-sort AST structure (Word/Expr/Binding/Command)
>   - Profunctor redirection model (wrapping, not flat list)
>   - Scope chain (Env with Vec<Scope>)
>   - Discipline functions (.get notification-only, .set with reentrancy guard)
>   - ⊕ error convention (Status returns, no longjmp)
>   - Fork/pipe/exec model (rustix + libc)
>   - Job control (JobTable, signals_receipts)
> 
>   Implementation order
> 
>   1. Parser rewrite with combine — implements the spec's grammar
>   2. Two-alphabet split — is_var_char / is_word_char
>   3. Val extension — rewrite value.rs with 9 variants
>   4. match with => and ; — rename Switch, add structural patterns
>   5. try block — scoped ⅋
>   6. => single-line body — uniform across all control flow
>   7. Accessor syntax — .0, .ok, .err, .code
>   8. try in value position + shared capture_subprocess
>   9. return in value-producing blocks
>   10. Type annotations, heredoc expansion, missing I/O
> 
>   Three things to know that aren't in the docs
> 
>   1. $stem.c is an accessor, not free-caret concat. The accessor production takes priority over free carets after
>    $-variables. This is a breaking change from the prototype. Escape hatch: ${stem}.c. Documented in syntax.md
>   §Interaction with accessors.
>   2. ExitCode truthiness inverts Int truthiness. ExitCode(0) = true (success). Int(0) = false. Coercion
>   deliberately prohibited. .code accessor provides explicit extraction.
>   3. ** `{cmd} and try share capture_subprocess but are siblings, not parent-child.** Neither desugars into the
>   other. Both fork.  `{cmd} projects stdout (π₁). try projects both and wraps as Result[T]. One implementation,
>   two projections.
> 
>   Test strategy
> 
>   The integration test harness exists (tests/harness.rs, tests/integration.rs) but tests were written against the
>    prototype grammar. Rewrite all tests against docs/syntax.md. Every example in the spec should become a test.
>   Every [planned] production should have tests marked #[ignore] until implemented.
> 
>   Existing unit tests (cargo test -- --test-threads=1, 157 passing) test the parts that don't change (scope
>   chain, pipe wiring, fork/exec, job control, disciplines).
>

>   project_psh_syntax_deliberation in auto-memory. Use the four agents for significant design changes — they have

> Here is my guidance for Phases B, C, and D, grounded in rc's design and Plan 9 implementation experience.
> 
> ## 1. Parser Architecture (Phase B): Honoring rc's Grammar Shape
> 
> rc's grammar, as Duff describes in rc.ms sections "Simple Commands" through "Syntax," is roughly 15 productions. The operative insight is which productions are *load-bearing* and which are *derived*. Here is rc's grammar skeleton, which I reconstruct from Duff's paper and the rc(1) man page:
> 
> ```
> rc          = line*
> line        = cmd | cmd '&amp;'
> cmd         = simple | '{' rc '}' | if_cmd | for_cmd | switch_cmd
>             | cmd '|' cmd | cmd '&amp;&amp;' cmd | cmd '||' cmd
>             | fn_def | assignment
>             | '!' cmd | '@' cmd
> simple      = word+  redir*
> word        = word_atom ('^'? word_atom)*
> word_atom   = BAREWORD | $var | $var(sub) | $#var | `{ rc }
>             | &lt;{ rc } | 'quoted'
> redir       = &gt; file | &gt;&gt; file | &lt; file | &gt;[fd] file | &gt;[fd=fd] | &lt;&lt;delim
> ```
> 
> **The critical observation:** rc has exactly *three* recursive descent entry points that matter: `rc` (command list), `cmd` (one command), and `word` (one value). Everything else is a leaf or a binary operator at fixed precedence. The grammar is LL(1) with one-character lookahead everywhere except the free caret rule (which is zero-character lookahead -- it fires on adjacency).
> 
> For the `combine` rewrite, the natural combinator boundaries are:
> 
> **Layer 1 -- Lexical predicates.** Two character-class combinators: `var_char()` and `word_char()`. These are `combine::parser::char::satisfy` instances. They are the foundation of the two-alphabet split. In the existing parser, `is_word_char` at line 27 of parse.rs does double duty for both variable names and bare words. The spec's `var_char` / `word_char` split means you need two distinct `take_while1` combinators. This is the first thing to implement and test.
> 
> **Layer 2 -- Word atoms.** This maps to the `word_atom` production. In `combine`, this is a `choice` combinator over:
> - `quote_parser` (consume `'`, accumulate until matching `'`, handle `''` escape)
> - `var_ref_parser` (consume `$`, then `var_char+`, then optional accessor chain, then optional `(word)`)
> - `count_parser` (`$#` + varname)
> - `stringify_parser` (`$"` + varname)
> - `brace_var_parser` (`${` + name + `}`)
> - `command_sub_parser` (`` ` `` + `{` + recursive program + `}`)
> - `process_sub_parser` (`&lt;{` + recursive program + `}`)
> - `tilde_parser` (special handling: `~/path` vs bare `~`)
> - `literal_parser` (`word_char+`)
> 
> The order matters for `combine::choice`. The `$` variants must come before literals because `$` is not in `word_char`. The `&lt;{` variant must come before any redirect parsing. This mirrors the existing parser's `word_atom_nows` at line 319, but as a combinator, each branch is independently testable.
> 
> **Layer 3 -- Words with free carets.** `word = word_atom ('^' word_atom)*` with the free caret extension. In `combine`, this is:
> 
> ```rust
> word_atom()
>     .and(many(
>         optional(token('^'))
>             .skip(/* no whitespace between atoms for free caret */)
>             .and(word_atom_nows())
>     ))
> ```
> 
> The critical subtlety is the free caret rule. Duff (rc(1), "Free Carets"): "Whenever one of `$ ' `` ` `` follows a quoted or unquoted word or an unquoted word follows a quoted word with no intervening blanks or tabs, a `^` is inserted between the two." In `combine`, this means: after parsing a word_atom, if the *next character* (with no whitespace consumed) can start a word_atom, implicitly insert a `^` and continue. This is a `look_ahead` + `attempt` pattern. The accessor production (planned) must take priority here -- after `$varname`, if `.` followed by digit-or-name appears, parse as accessor before trying free caret. Use `attempt(accessor_parser).or(free_caret_continuation)`.
> 
> **Layer 4 -- Expressions (the precedence tower).** This maps to psh's `expr_cmd → or_expr → and_expr → pipeline → cmd_expr`. In `combine`, use `chainl1` or `sep_by1` with the operator token as separator:
> 
> ```
> or_expr   = sep_by1(and_expr, token_str("||"))
> and_expr  = sep_by1(pipeline, token_str("&amp;&amp;"))
> pipeline  = sep_by1(cmd_expr, token('|'))  // must disambiguate |&amp; and ||
> cmd_expr  = choice(not_cmd, block, subshell, simple_with_redirects)
> ```
> 
> The `|` vs `||` vs `|&amp;` disambiguation requires two-character lookahead. In `combine`, use `attempt(string("||"))` in the `or_expr` layer before `|` reaches the pipeline layer. The `|&amp;` coprocess form is a postfix on `cmd_expr`, not a binary operator between pipeline stages. The existing parser handles this at line 16 of its grammar comment but the actual disambiguation is spread across multiple methods. The combinator version should make the precedence tower explicit.
> 
> **Layer 5 -- Commands.** The `command` production dispatches on keyword. In `combine`, this is:
> 
> ```rust
> choice((
>     attempt(if_parser),
>     attempt(for_parser),
>     attempt(while_parser),
>     attempt(match_parser),     // replaces switch
>     attempt(try_parser),       // new
>     attempt(fn_parser),
>     attempt(let_parser),
>     attempt(ref_parser),
>     attempt(return_parser),    // new
>     simple_or_assign_parser,   // fallthrough
> ))
> ```
> 
> Each keyword parser consumes the keyword with `attempt(string("if").skip(not_followed_by(var_char)))` -- the `not_followed_by` prevents `iffy` from being parsed as `if` + `fy`. This is the same trick the existing parser uses with `check_keyword`, but combinators make it compositional.
> 
> **Layer 6 -- Program.** `program = sep_end_by(command, terminator+)`. This is `combine::sep_end_by` with the terminator parser consuming newlines, semicolons, and comments.
> 
> **Structural recommendation:** Keep the layers as separate functions/modules, not one giant combinator expression. rc's grammar was small enough to fit on two pages of troff. psh's is larger, but the *shape* should remain visible. Each layer should be independently testable with `combine::easy::parse`.
> 
> **What to watch for:** The existing parser is 1622 lines. A `combine` rewrite will be shorter if you resist the temptation to encode error recovery in the combinators. rc's parser gave terse errors -- "syntax error" with a line number. psh should give better errors, but don't try to build error-recovery combinators in Phase B. Get the happy path right first. `combine`'s `expected` and `message` combinators are sufficient for error messages; custom recovery logic is Phase N.
> 
> ## 2. Heredoc and I/O (Phase B/D): Parse Structure, Evaluate Content
> 
> Duff's rc (rc.ms, "Here Documents"): "Its standard input is a copy of the rc input up to a line containing nothing but the delimiter string." The heredoc body was captured as a raw string by the *parser*, then the *evaluator* expanded `$var` references when executing. rc.ms: "Variable references and command substitutions are recognized and expanded in the body of a here document."
> 
> This is the correct factoring for psh: **parse completely, evaluate later.**
> 
> In concrete terms:
> 
> **Parser concern:** The parser sees `&lt;&lt;EOF`, scans to the matching `EOF` line, and captures the raw body text as a string. It also records whether the delimiter was quoted (`&lt;&lt;'EOF'`) or unquoted (`&lt;&lt;EOF`). The AST node carries this information:
> 
> ```rust
> HereDoc {
>     body: String,        // raw text, unexpanded
>     expand: bool,        // true if delimiter was unquoted
> }
> ```
> 
> **Evaluator concern:** At execution time, if `expand` is true, the evaluator walks the heredoc body string looking for `$varname` references and expands them. This is *not* a re-parse of shell syntax -- it's a restricted expansion that recognizes only `$var`, `$var(idx)`, `$#var`, `${name}`, and `$"var`. No command substitutions, no free carets, no pipelines. The expansion is a simple scan-and-replace, not a recursive descent.
> 
> **Why this matters:** If you try to parse heredoc bodies with the full `word` combinator at parse time, you create a dependency between the parser and the runtime environment (you need variable values to determine the output). This violates the property Duff valued most: "Input is never scanned more than once" (rc.ms, "Design Principles"). The heredoc body is scanned once by the parser (captured as raw text), then expanded once by the evaluator. Two passes, each doing one thing.
> 
> **Implementation in `combine`:** The heredoc parser is a *stateful* combinator that needs to interact with the outer lexer state. When the parser sees `&lt;&lt;DELIM` at the end of a line, it must:
> 1. Record the delimiter and the expand flag
> 2. Complete parsing the current command line (there may be more redirections after `&lt;&lt;EOF`)
> 3. *Then* consume heredoc body lines until the delimiter appears
> 
> rc handled this with a two-pass approach: the lexer recorded pending heredocs and filled them in after the command line was complete. In `combine`, the cleanest approach is a post-processing step: parse the command line normally with a placeholder for the heredoc body, then immediately after the newline terminator, consume heredoc bodies for all pending `&lt;&lt;` redirections in order. This avoids tangling the line-oriented heredoc scanning with the expression-oriented command parsing.
> 
> The existing parser at line 245 (`read_heredoc`) does this correctly in a procedural style. The combinator version needs the same discipline: heredoc body capture is a *side channel* from the expression grammar.
> 
> ## 3. Process Substitution and capture_subprocess (Phase D)
> 
> Plan 9's process substitution in rc created a pipe, forked, and returned `/dev/fd/N`. The implementation was about 20 lines in plan9port's rc: `pipe(fd)`, `fork()`, redirect child's stdout to the write end, pass the read end as `/dev/fd/N` to the parent command. The key factoring in the kernel was that `pipe(2)` created the resource pair and `rfork` with `RFFDG|RFPROC` handled the process boundary.
> 
> psh's `capture_subprocess` is the right abstraction level. Here is why, and what the factoring should look like:
> 
> **The shared primitive is: fork + pipe + waitpid + capture.** Both `` `{cmd} `` and `try { cmd }` in value position need:
> 1. Create a pipe pair (stdout capture)
> 2. Fork
> 3. In child: redirect stdout to pipe write end, exec body
> 4. In parent: read from pipe read end until EOF, waitpid
> 
> The difference is *what they return*:
> - `` `{cmd} `` projects stdout, splits on newlines, discards exit status
> - `try { cmd }` projects both stdout and exit status, wraps as `Result[T]`
> 
> This is a product followed by two different projections. In code:
> 
> ```rust
> struct CaptureResult {
>     stdout: String,
>     exit_code: i32,
> }
> 
> fn capture_subprocess(body: &amp;[Command], shell: &amp;mut Shell) -&gt; Result&lt;CaptureResult&gt; {
>     // pipe, fork, exec, read, waitpid
> }
> ```
> 
> Then:
> - `` `{cmd} `` calls `capture_subprocess` and uses `result.stdout` (split on newlines)
> - `try { cmd }` calls `capture_subprocess` and uses both fields to construct `Sum("ok", val)` or `Sum("err", ExitCode(n))`
> 
> **What Plan 9 teaches about the right abstraction level:** Don't go lower than pipe+fork. In Plan 9, `pipe(2)` created a bidirectional pair (both ends could read and write on Plan 9's full-duplex pipes). On Unix, `pipe(2)` creates a unidirectional pair. psh already uses `rustix::pipe()` for this. The temptation is to factor further -- separate the pipe creation from the fork, or the fork from the exec. Resist it. rc's process substitution was one function that did pipe+fork+redirect+close-unused-ends. The *single function* is what prevents fd leaks. If you split pipe creation from fork, you must track the intermediate fds across the fork boundary and close them in both parent and child. That is where bugs live. Plan 9's `rfork(RFFDG)` copied the entire fd table, making it safe to close the unused ends in each process independently. Unix `fork()` copies too, but the window between `pipe()` and `fork()` is where fds can leak if you're not careful.
> 
> **Recommendation:** `capture_subprocess` should be one function that does the full pipe+fork+exec+capture+waitpid sequence. Expose two wrapper functions:
> 
> ```rust
> fn eval_command_sub(body: &amp;[Command], shell: &amp;mut Shell) -&gt; Val { ... }
> fn eval_try_value(body: &amp;[Command], shell: &amp;mut Shell) -&gt; Val { ... }
> ```
> 
> Both call `capture_subprocess` and project differently. The wrappers belong in the evaluator. The shared primitive belongs in a `subprocess` module.
> 
> **stderr capture:** Note that the spec says `try` captures stdout + exit status. It does *not* capture stderr. This is intentional -- stderr goes to the parent's stderr (visible in the terminal). If you later want stderr capture, add it to `CaptureResult` but keep it optional. Don't capture stderr by default; rc never did.
> 
> ## 4. match vs switch (Phase C)
> 
> The spec's `match` design is clean. Let me evaluate it against Plan 9's language design principles.
> 
> rc's `switch` (rc.ms, "Switch"): "The switch command selects one of a number of bodies, depending on whether the value matches any of the patterns." The body was implicitly delimited -- execution ran from one `case` keyword to the next, with fall-through by default. Duff acknowledged this was borrowed from C's switch and inherited C's foot-guns.
> 
> psh's `match` improves on this in three ways that Plan 9 principles endorse:
> 
> **No fall-through.** Each arm is independent. `;` terminates. This follows the Plan 9 principle of no implicit control flow. rc's `case` fall-through was the one place where rc's grammar was *not* simple -- the semantics depended on the absence of the next `case`, which is a negative condition.
> 
> **`=&gt;` as body introducer.** This eliminates the ambiguity that rc had with `case` patterns followed by a body. In rc, `case *.c *.h` meant "match either `*.c` or `*.h`" and the body was everything up to the next `case`. The boundary between patterns and body was implicit. psh's `=&gt;` makes it explicit. Orthogonal, unambiguous.
> 
> **Structural patterns (`tag $binding`).** The `$` after the tag name is the discriminator between glob arms and structural arms. This is clean -- the parser can decide which arm type it's parsing by looking for `$` after the first word. No second keyword needed. The grammar is:
> 
> ```
> match_arm = word+ '=&gt;' command+        // glob: all words are patterns
>           | NAME '$' NAME '=&gt;' command+ // structural: tag + binding
> ```
> 
> The `$` is doing double duty as "this is a variable binding" (consistent with `$var` everywhere else) and "this arm destructures." I see no conflict. The Plan 9 principle of "one notation, one meaning" is maintained -- `$name` always means "a variable named `name`."
> 
> **One concern:** Multiple glob patterns per arm (`a b =&gt; body` matches either `a` or `b`) is good. But what about multiple structural patterns? The spec says `match_pat+ '=&gt;' command+`. Can you write `ok $v err $e =&gt; body`? That would mean "match ok with binding v OR match err with binding e" -- but which binding is in scope in the body? Both? Only the one that matched? This needs clarification. If each structural arm can only have one pattern, the grammar should be:
> 
> ```
> match_arm = match_pat (match_pat)* '=&gt;' command+
> ```
> 
> where at most one `match_pat` is structural. Or, simpler: structural arms are always single-pattern. Glob arms can be multi-pattern. This avoids the scoping ambiguity entirely.
> 
> rc's design principle: if you can't explain the scoping in one sentence, the construct is too complex. "The binding `$v` is in scope in the body and holds the payload of whichever tag matched" is one sentence, but it's a suspicious sentence because it implies the binding might be unbound if the other pattern matched. Better to keep structural arms single-pattern.
> 
> ## 5. Risks and Anti-Patterns
> 
> **Risk 1: The parser becomes the design.** rc's grammar was 15 productions. Duff could hold it in his head. psh's spec grammar is already larger, and `combine` makes it easy to add more combinators "because it's just one more `choice` branch." The risk is that the grammar grows without the discipline that a 15-production budget enforces. Guard against this by maintaining a production count. If the grammar exceeds 30 productions, something has gone wrong -- you've overloaded the surface syntax with features that should be in the evaluator or the library.
> 
> **Risk 2: Free carets + accessors = ambiguity engine.** The spec acknowledges this at line 440-451: `$stem.c` changes meaning when accessors are enabled. The resolution (explicit `^` or `${stem}.c`) is correct but it means every user who learned rc's free caret idiom must unlearn it. The parser must get the priority right: accessor before free caret, with no backtracking path that silently changes the parse. Use `attempt(accessor_chain()).or(free_caret_rest())` and ensure the accessor combinator fails *fast* (doesn't consume input on failure) so that the free caret path still works for non-accessor cases like `$file.c` where `.c` is a literal extension. This is the single trickiest parse-time decision in the grammar. Test it exhaustively.
> 
> **Risk 3: Heredoc as parser state leak.** Heredocs are inherently stateful -- the parser must remember pending heredocs across the command-line boundary. In `combine`, this means either threading state through the parser (messy) or using a two-pass approach where the first pass identifies heredoc boundaries and the second pass fills in bodies. The existing parser handles this procedurally (line 245). The combinator version must not let this statefulness infect the rest of the grammar. Encapsulate it.
> 
> **Risk 4: `try` in value position overloads the word grammar.** `try { cmd }` in word position means the `word_atom` combinator must recognize `try` as a keyword. But `try` is also a command-level keyword (`try { } else $e { }`). The parser must disambiguate by position: in value position (RHS of `let`, argument to a command), `try { }` is a word. In command position, `try { } else ...` is a command. This is the same kind of context-sensitive parsing that rc avoided entirely. Document the disambiguation rule clearly and test both positions in the same script.
> 
> **Risk 5: Losing rc's error behavior.** rc's parser errors were terse and fatal. The shell stopped, printed a line number, and that was it. No recovery, no partial parse. `combine`'s error model is richer -- it distinguishes between "consumed input and failed" and "failed without consuming." The temptation is to use this for error recovery. Don't. Phase B should match rc's behavior: on parse error, report the position and stop. Error recovery is a later concern. Duff: "The shell is not a programming language, and it is a mistake to try to make it one" (rc.ms, "Design Principles"). Error recovery in parsers is a programming-language concern.
> 
> **The overarching Plan 9 principle to guard:** Simplicity is not a starting point you depart from. It is a property you maintain. Every production you add should be earned by a use case that cannot be served by existing productions. rc's grammar was small because Duff was willing to say "no" to features that would complicate the parser. The four-agent roundtable should apply the same discipline: when in doubt, leave it out of the grammar and handle it in the evaluator or as a library function.</result>
> <usage><total_tokens>74197</total_tokens><tool_uses>26</tool_uses><duration_ms>209716</duration_ms></usage>
> </task-notification>

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>adea67a94dd3bd4f7</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01J21LrCLSE1gpbuifgPoGAp</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/25f44a96-9488-444d-b842-3df1553f5366/tasks/adea67a94dd3bd4f7.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Be agent: control flow + match design" completed</summary>
> <result>I have all the context I need. Let me write the comprehensive response.
> 
> ---
> 
> Here is my analysis across all five areas. I'm drawing from direct experience with Be's design, verified against Haiku's source, and mapped through the theoretical framework in your specification.
> 
> ## 1. match as coproduct elimination
> 
> ### What Be did and where it went wrong
> 
> BLooper::DispatchMessage (`~/src/haiku/src/kits/app/Looper.cpp:244`) is the canonical coproduct elimination in BeOS. It switches on `message-&gt;what` — a `uint32` tag code:
> 
> ```cpp
> switch (message-&gt;what) {
>     case _QUIT_:
>         fTerminating = true;
>         break;
>     case B_QUIT_REQUESTED:
>         if (handler == this) {
>             _QuitRequested(message);
>             break;
>         }
>         // fall through
>     default:
>         handler-&gt;MessageReceived(message);
>         break;
> }
> ```
> 
> BWindow::DispatchMessage (Window.cpp:951) extends this with ~30 additional cases for window system messages (B_ZOOM, B_MINIMIZE, B_WINDOW_RESIZED, B_KEY_DOWN, etc.), falling through to `BLooper::DispatchMessage` in the default case.
> 
> The critical lesson is that **Be conflated two fundamentally different kinds of dispatch in the same switch**: system-protocol messages (B_QUIT_REQUESTED, B_WINDOW_RESIZED — the framework's coproduct, closed) and application-defined messages (forwarded to MessageReceived — the developer's coproduct, open). The result:
> 
> **Problem 1: Fallthrough as implicit delegation.** BWindow's switch has explicit `break` on system messages and falls through to `BLooper::DispatchMessage` for unrecognized ones. That `BLooper` switch in turn falls through to `handler-&gt;MessageReceived()` for anything it doesn't recognize. This is a two-level coproduct elimination where the unmatched case cascades — and it's where bugs happened. Developers would accidentally match a system message code in their MessageReceived and intercept it before the framework saw it, or they'd override DispatchMessage without calling the parent and silently swallow system messages. The coproduct was split across inheritance layers with no type-level protection.
> 
> **Problem 2: The `what` code conflated tag and payload type.** A `uint32` tells you which arm you're in but gives you no structural guarantee about what fields the message carries. Developers routinely forgot to check FindInt32/FindString return values, leading to silent data corruption when the message didn't contain the expected field. The "tag" (what) was decoupled from the "payload" (fields), so the compiler couldn't help.
> 
> **Problem 3: No exhaustiveness.** The default case was where unhandled messages went to die (or get forwarded to BHandler::MessageReceived, which printed a diagnostic to stdout and sent B_MESSAGE_NOT_UNDERSTOOD). Missing a case was silent. You'd only discover it at runtime.
> 
> ### How psh's match should be structured
> 
> psh's `match` handles two things: glob dispatch (rc heritage) and structural Sum elimination (psh extension). The spec distinguishes them syntactically by the presence of `$binding` after the tag. This is the right call — it avoids Be's problem of conflating dispatch modes in one switch.
> 
> **Concrete evaluator structure:**
> 
> ```rust
> Command::Match { value, arms } =&gt; {
>     let val = self.eval_value(value);
>     for arm in arms {
>         match &amp;arm.pattern {
>             MatchPat::Glob(patterns) =&gt; {
>                 // rc heritage: string matching
>                 let val_str = val.as_str();
>                 if patterns.iter().any(|p| self.match_pattern(&amp;val_str, p)) {
>                     return self.run_arm_body(&amp;arm.body);
>                 }
>             }
>             MatchPat::Structural(tag, binding) =&gt; {
>                 // psh extension: Sum decomposition
>                 if let Val::Sum(ref t, ref payload) = val {
>                     if t == tag {
>                         self.env.push_scope();
>                         let _ = self.env.set_value(binding, (**payload).clone());
>                         let status = self.run_arm_body(&amp;arm.body);
>                         self.env.pop_scope();
>                         return status;
>                     }
>                 }
>                 // Non-Sum value against a structural pattern: no match, skip
>             }
>         }
>     }
>     // No arm matched: this is where the design decision lives
>     Status::ok()
> }
> ```
> 
> Key design points:
> 
> 1. **Glob and structural are separate code paths in the same dispatch, distinguished statically by the AST.** The parser's `$` disambiguator means the evaluator never has to guess. This is what Be should have done — if the system had distinguished "framework protocol message" from "application message" at the message level rather than relying on `what` code ranges, the entire DispatchMessage inheritance mess would have been avoided.
> 
> 2. **Structural arms bind in a new scope.** The `$v` binding is a μ̃-binder (context extension). It must live in a pushed scope that pops after the arm body. The current Switch implementation doesn't need this because glob patterns don't bind. The scope push/pop in structural arms is the same discipline as function call scope — don't special-case it.
> 
> 3. **The no-match-found case.** Be's default was delegation (call super::MessageReceived). psh should return `Status::ok()` — an unmatched match is not an error. This preserves the rc heritage where `switch` with no matching case simply continues. If you want exhaustive matching on Sum types, that's a static analysis pass (later, not now), not a runtime abort.
> 
> 4. **Do not mix glob and structural arms in a single match.** The spec allows it (any arm is either form), and that's fine syntactically. But document this: if you `match $result` and have `ok $v =&gt; ...` followed by `* =&gt; ...`, the `*` is a glob arm that matches the *string representation* of the Sum value, not a structural wildcard. This is actually correct and useful (the fallback can handle the stringified form), but it needs to be explicit. Be's equivalent confusion: developers treating `default:` in DispatchMessage as "everything else" without realizing that system messages had already been handled by the framework's switch cases above.
> 
> ## 2. try as scoped error handling
> 
> ### What Be taught us about error boundaries
> 
> BLooper's dispatch loop (Looper.cpp, around line 1240-1260) has implicit error containment. Each dispatched message is processed in the looper thread; if it throws (via C++ exception, in Haiku's modern code), the looper stays alive. The error boundary is the message-processing iteration — one bad message doesn't kill the looper.
> 
> But Be's error handling was fundamentally `status_t` everywhere — the ⊕ convention. And it was terrible at composition. My memory here, not from code: the most common bug pattern in Be apps was forgetting to check `status_t` after an operation, compounding the error through subsequent calls. `InitCheck()` was the poster child — you construct a BFile, forget to call `InitCheck()`, and the next Read() segfaults or returns garbage. The error existed but the continuation ran anyway.
> 
> This is exactly the problem `try` solves. `set -e` in POSIX was the right instinct — make errors abort — but the wrong implementation: dynamically scoped, inconsistent exemptions, composability disaster. psh's `try` is the lexically scoped version with the right exemptions.
> 
> ### Implementation guidance for try
> 
> **The `in_try` flag.** The spec says: "Implementation: `in_try` flag, checked after each command in `run_cmds`." This is correct and simple. Do it as a counter, not a boolean — try blocks nest, and you need to know the depth for the else handler routing:
> 
> ```rust
> struct Shell {
>     try_depth: u32,  // 0 = not in try, &gt; 0 = in try
>     // ...
> }
> ```
> 
> After each command in `run_cmds`, check:
> 
> ```rust
> fn run_cmds(&amp;mut self, cmds: &amp;[Command]) -&gt; Status {
>     let mut status = Status::ok();
>     for cmd in cmds {
>         status = self.run_cmd(cmd);
>         if self.try_depth &gt; 0 &amp;&amp; !status.is_success() &amp;&amp; !self.in_boolean_context {
>             return status;  // early abort — the try block catches this
>         }
>     }
>     status
> }
> ```
> 
> **Boolean-context exemption.** `if` conditions, `while` conditions, `&amp;&amp;`/`||` LHS, `!` — these are contexts where nonzero status is expected and meaningful, not failure. Be's parallel: in BLooper::DispatchMessage, the B_QUIT_REQUESTED handler calls `_QuitRequested()` which calls `QuitRequested()` which returns bool. The boolean return is the *expected* negative outcome — it means "don't quit," not "something broke." The exemption is the same principle: boolean contexts *consume* the status as data, not as error signal.
> 
> Implement as a flag on Shell that's set before evaluating conditions and cleared after:
> 
> ```rust
> fn run_cmd(&amp;mut self, cmd: &amp;Command) -&gt; Status {
>     match cmd {
>         Command::If { condition, then_body, else_body } =&gt; {
>             let prev = self.in_boolean_context;
>             self.in_boolean_context = true;
>             let cond_status = self.run_expr(condition);
>             self.in_boolean_context = prev;
>             // ... branch on cond_status
>         }
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> **Interaction with discipline functions.** This is where Be's experience is most directly relevant. A `.set` discipline fires during assignment. If the discipline body fails inside a `try` block, that failure should abort the try — the discipline body is part of the command's effects. The reentrancy guard is already in place and is orthogonal to try. But: the `.get` discipline is a *notification* — it fires during word expansion, and the spec says it runs in a readonly scope and cannot influence the returned value. A `.get` discipline failure inside try should NOT abort the try, because the discipline is a side-effect observer, not part of the value computation. This is analogous to BLooper's filter chain — a BMessageFilter that returned B_SKIP_MESSAGE didn't cause an error in the dispatch loop; it was a routing decision, not a failure.
> 
> So: `.set` discipline failures propagate through try. `.get` discipline failures are swallowed (or logged to stderr) — the value flows through regardless. The notification-only semantics of `.get` protects you here.
> 
> **Be's mistake to avoid:** Do not make try's scope dynamic. ksh93's `set -e` was effectively a global mode flag. Be's lock state was also effectively global (any thread could Lock() a looper). Both were dynamic scope and both caused composition failures. `try_depth` must be a stack discipline, and it must be per-invocation, not global shell state. When you implement `try` in value position (Phase D), the `capture_subprocess` fork creates a new Shell in the child — `try_depth` resets to 0 in the child. The fork boundary is the scope boundary. This is correct by construction.
> 
> ## 3. =&gt; single-line body
> 
> ### Be's API uniformity principle
> 
> Be cared deeply about "one pattern for all similar operations." BLooper::MessageReceived had one signature. BHandler::MessageReceived had one signature. Every override followed the same pattern: switch on `what`, handle your cases, call inherited for the rest. The uniformity was in the *shape* of the code you wrote, not just the API signatures.
> 
> The Be Newsletter (Issue 4-2, Jeff Bush's scripting article) makes this explicit — the scripting protocol used the same BMessage mechanism as everything else. You didn't learn a separate IPC protocol; you learned BMessage and BMessenger, and scripting fell out of that. The uniformity was a **productivity multiplier**: learn the pattern once, apply it everywhere.
> 
> `=&gt;` as a uniform body introducer is exactly this principle. Every control flow construct uses the same body syntax: `{ program }` for multi-line, `=&gt; command` for single-line. Learn it once for `if`, and you know it for `match`, `for`, `while`, `try`, `fn`, and `else`.
> 
> ### Concerns and mitigations
> 
> The one thing that made Be's uniformity occasionally painful was **context-dependent interpretation**. BMessage's `AddData`/`FindData` was uniform — same methods for all types — but the `type_code` parameter meant you had to know which type you were dealing with. The uniformity of syntax masked the non-uniformity of semantics.
> 
> For `=&gt;` vs `{ }`, the semantic difference is minimal: `=&gt; command` reads one command, `{ program }` reads a program (sequence of commands). They're genuinely interchangeable wherever `body` appears. The only concern I have:
> 
> In `match` arms specifically, `=&gt;` has a dual role — it's both the arm introducer (separating pattern from body) and the single-line body form. This means `match $x { foo =&gt; echo bar }` is read as "pattern `foo`, single-line body `echo bar`." That's clean. But `match $x { foo =&gt; { echo bar; echo baz } }` looks odd — `=&gt; { }` is arrow followed by braced block. Is that a single-line body containing a brace block (which is itself a body), or is it redundant? The grammar should handle this unambiguously: `=&gt;` reads one `command`, and `{ program }` at command position is a valid command (a body-as-command). So `=&gt; { ... }` works but means "single-line body whose single command is a braced block." It's unnecessary but not harmful. Document it as a style note: use `{ }` directly, not `=&gt; { }`.
> 
> This is the right level of uniformity. Don't break it.
> 
> ## 4. Accessor syntax as optic composition
> 
> ### How Be handled field access
> 
> BMessage field access was `FindString("name", &amp;value)`, `FindInt32("count", &amp;value)`, etc. BNode attribute access was `ReadAttr("name", type, offset, buf, len)`. Both were name-based dictionary lookups returning `status_t`. The accessor was always a runtime string lookup with a runtime type assertion.
> 
> The problem: **accessors didn't compose.** You couldn't write `message.FindMessage("inner").FindString("field")` because each call returned `status_t`, not a chainable reference. You had to write:
> 
> ```cpp
> BMessage inner;
> if (message.FindMessage("inner", &amp;inner) == B_OK) {
>     const char* field;
>     if (inner.FindString("field", &amp;field) == B_OK) {
>         // finally
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> Two-level nesting required two separate extractions with error checking at each step. This was correct (each step can fail) but ergonomically brutal. It discouraged structured data because every level of nesting doubled the boilerplate.
> 
> ### How psh's accessors should compose
> 
> The accessor chain `$result.ok.name` is Prism (Sum → payload) then Lens (Tuple/named-field → element). This is an AffineTraversal — it may fail (the Prism might not match) but when it succeeds, it extracts precisely.
> 
> **Internal representation:** The parser should produce accessors as a `Vec&lt;Accessor&gt;` attached to the var_ref:
> 
> ```rust
> enum Accessor {
>     Index(u32),        // .0, .1 — Lens into Tuple
>     Tag(String),       // .ok, .err — Prism into Sum
>     Code,              // .code — ExitCode extraction
> }
> 
> // In Word:
> Var { name: String, accessors: Vec&lt;Accessor&gt; }
> ```
> 
> **Evaluator:** Walk the accessor chain left-to-right, threading the value through:
> 
> ```rust
> fn apply_accessors(&amp;self, mut val: Val, accessors: &amp;[Accessor]) -&gt; Val {
>     for acc in accessors {
>         val = match acc {
>             Accessor::Index(n) =&gt; match val {
>                 Val::Tuple(elems) =&gt; elems.into_iter().nth(*n as usize)
>                     .unwrap_or(Val::Unit),
>                 _ =&gt; Val::Unit,  // type mismatch → Unit (affine failure)
>             },
>             Accessor::Tag(tag) =&gt; match val {
>                 Val::Sum(t, payload) if &amp;t == tag =&gt; *payload,
>                 _ =&gt; Val::Unit,  // Prism miss → Unit
>             },
>             Accessor::Code =&gt; match val {
>                 Val::ExitCode(n) =&gt; Val::Int(n as i64),
>                 Val::Sum(ref t, ref payload) if t == "err" =&gt; {
>                     match payload.as_ref() {
>                         Val::ExitCode(n) =&gt; Val::Int(*n as i64),
>                         _ =&gt; Val::Unit,
>                     }
>                 }
>                 _ =&gt; Val::Unit,
>             },
>         };
>     }
>     val
> }
> ```
> 
> **Key design decisions, informed by Be's mistakes:**
> 
> 1. **Affine failure produces Unit, not an error.** Be's `status_t` return from FindString forced you to check at every step. psh's accessor chain should be like Plan 9's file walk: each step either succeeds and continues, or produces the empty value. Unit is the "nothing here" signal in the value sort. This lets you write `$result.ok.name` without intermediate error checking — if the Prism misses, the rest of the chain gets Unit, and Unit through any accessor is Unit.
> 
> 2. **Accessor application happens inside eval_word, not as a separate pass.** After resolving the variable and applying `.get` discipline, immediately apply the accessor chain before returning the value. The accessor is part of the word evaluation, not a post-processing step. This matters because the `.get` discipline fires on `$result`, and then the accessor chain `.ok.name` works on the *result* of that evaluation. The discipline sees the full variable; the accessor projects from its value.
> 
> 3. **Accessors compose with free carets correctly because `.` is not in `var_char`.** The two-alphabet split already handles this. After `$result` (var_char terminates at `.`), the parser tries the accessor production before falling back to free caret. This is the right priority order.
> 
> ## 5. return as polarity shift
> 
> ### Be's implicit polarity and what it cost
> 
> BMessage was the canonical example of polarity confusion in Be's design. The spec (specification.md:716-733) nails the diagnosis: `positive_data x ↑(negative_continuation)` — data and reply obligation bundled in one object. The consequence was that you couldn't clone a BMessage safely (the reply port was linear — two replies to the same message was a bug), you couldn't treat a message as pure data (the continuation was embedded), and the lifetime of the data was coupled to the lifetime of the reply obligation.
> 
> The deeper problem: **Be had no syntax for the polarity shift.** When a BLooper dispatched a message, the same object served as both the data to read and the continuation to reply to. The developer had to remember which mode they were in. Nothing in the API surface marked "you are now reading data (positive)" vs "you are now replying (negative)." The `message-&gt;SendReply()` call was the shift from reading to replying, but it looked like just another method call on the same object.
> 
> ### How return should work
> 
> `return` in psh is CBPV's `return : A → F(A)`. It marks the explicit moment when a value-sort datum emerges from a command-sort computation. This is exactly the syntax Be was missing.
> 
> **Implementation in the evaluator:** `return` needs a new control-flow mechanism — it's not a status, it's a value injection. The cleanest approach:
> 
> ```rust
> enum RunResult {
>     Status(Status),
>     Return(Val),
> }
> ```
> 
> But this infects every function signature. A simpler approach that works within the existing architecture: use a slot on Shell:
> 
> ```rust
> struct Shell {
>     return_value: Option&lt;Val&gt;,
>     // ...
> }
> ```
> 
> When `return word` is evaluated:
> 
> ```rust
> Command::Return(Some(value)) =&gt; {
>     let val = self.eval_value(value);
>     self.return_value = Some(val);
>     Status::ok()
> }
> ```
> 
> When a value-producing context (let RHS, match in value position) runs a body, it checks `return_value` after:
> 
> ```rust
> // In the let-with-body path:
> let status = self.run_cmds(&amp;body);
> let val = self.return_value.take().unwrap_or(Val::Unit);
> ```
> 
> **Why this simple approach works and where it doesn't:**
> 
> It works because psh is single-process, synchronous, sequential within a thread. The `return_value` slot is unambiguous — only one body is executing at a time.
> 
> **Where it gets tricky: nested value-producing blocks.** `let x = match $a { ok $v =&gt; return match $b { ... } }` — the inner match's `return` would overwrite the outer's slot. Solution: save and restore the slot around nested value-producing contexts:
> 
> ```rust
> fn run_value_producing_body(&amp;mut self, body: &amp;[Command]) -&gt; Val {
>     let saved = self.return_value.take();
>     self.run_cmds(body);
>     let result = self.return_value.take().unwrap_or(Val::Unit);
>     self.return_value = saved;
>     result
> }
> ```
> 
> This is the same save/restore discipline used for redirections and discipline dispatch — and it's what Be should have done with reply ports. If BMessage had separated the reply obligation into a save/restore slot on the looper thread (rather than embedding it in the message), the polarity confusion would have been mechanically prevented.
> 
> **The interaction with try:** `return` inside a `try` block in value position is the happy path — the computation produced a value. If the try block aborts (nonzero status), there's no `return` — the `try` wrapper produces `Sum("err", ExitCode(n))` from the failing status. The `return` and the error path are mutually exclusive — exactly one fires. This is the correct ⊕ coproduct: `ok(returned_value) | err(exit_code)`.
> 
> **The discipline function interaction:** `return` inside a `.set` discipline body is meaningless — discipline bodies are command-sort (effects only). `return` inside a `.get` discipline body is doubly meaningless — `.get` is notification-only, its return value is discarded. Neither should be an error — `return` in a non-value-producing context simply sets `return_value` to no effect (it's never read). This is consistent with how Be's `SendReply()` behaved on a message with no reply port — it returned `B_BAD_PORT_ID`, not a crash. Silently inert is better than loudly wrong.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Cross-cutting observation
> 
> Across all five features, one principle keeps emerging from Be's experience: **make the polarity boundary explicit and structural, not implicit and conventional.** Be's BMessage mixed positive and negative in one object. Be's DispatchMessage mixed closed and open coproducts in one switch. Be's error handling mixed ⊕ (check status_t) and "just keep going" in the same code path.
> 
> psh has the chance to get all of these right:
> - `match` separates glob dispatch from structural elimination at the AST level
> - `try` makes the ⊕→⅋ boundary lexically explicit
> - `return` marks the command→value polarity shift with a keyword
> - Accessors compose through the optic hierarchy rather than requiring per-step error checking
> - `=&gt;` provides uniform body syntax without hiding semantic differences
> 
> The spec already has the theory right. The implementation guidance above is about making sure the evaluator's control flow respects those boundaries as mechanically as the AST does structurally.</result>

> ## Session Type Consultant: psh Phases B-D Implementation Analysis
> 
> ### 1. `try` as ⊕→⅋ converter (Phase C)
> 
> **Verdict: conditionally sound.** The design is a correct embedding of ErrorT into the evaluator's control flow, subject to two invariants.
> 
> **Formal characterization.** `try` implements a local exception handler — the ErrorT monad transformer restricted to a lexical scope. In CBPV terms (Levy 2004, §3.3), this is a handler for the exception effect: `try { M } else $e { N }` is `handle M with { raise(e) =&gt; N }`. The ⊕→⅋ characterization from the spec is accurate: outside `try`, the caller inspects `$status` (positive elimination — ⊕, case analysis). Inside `try`, nonzero status invokes the handler continuation (negative elimination — ⅋, the callee transfers control).
> 
> The boolean-context exemptions (if/while conditions, &amp;&amp;/|| LHS, `!`) are essential. These contexts already perform their own status inspection — they are ⊕ eliminators. Applying the ⅋ discipline inside them would be a polarity collision: the same status would be consumed twice (once by the boolean context, once by `try`). CBPV prevents this by typing: a thunk `U(F(A) + F(B))` cannot be simultaneously forced by two different eliminators. The exemption list enforces this in the evaluator.
> 
> **Nesting semantics.** Inner `try` catches before outer — this is the standard ErrorT stack ordering. In sequent calculus terms, each `try` introduces a μ-binder (continuation capture): `μα. M` where α is the abort continuation. Inner μ-binders shadow outer ones. The spec's "no longjmp, no continuation stack" is correct — the `in_try` flag is sufficient because the evaluator is single-threaded and `run_cmds` recurses structurally. The call stack IS the continuation stack.
> 
> **Implementation: `in_try` flag is insufficient for nesting.** A boolean flag cannot distinguish inner from outer `try`. When inner `try` catches an error and completes successfully, the outer `try` must NOT see the caught error. A boolean works only if `run_cmds` is restructured so each `try` block has its own `run_cmds` invocation that returns early on error. The flag marks "am I in any try block?" but the catching behavior comes from the early return in `run_cmds`.
> 
> **Concrete Rust structure:**
> 
> ```rust
> /// Result of running a command sequence inside a try block.
> /// The try handler wraps run_cmds, not individual commands.
> enum TryOutcome {
>     /// All commands completed. Last status propagates.
>     Completed(Status),
>     /// A command failed inside try. Status of the failing command.
>     Aborted(Status),
> }
> 
> fn run_cmds_try(&amp;mut self, cmds: &amp;[Command]) -&gt; TryOutcome {
>     for cmd in cmds {
>         let status = self.run_cmd(cmd);
>         if !status.is_success() &amp;&amp; !self.in_boolean_context {
>             return TryOutcome::Aborted(status);
>         }
>     }
>     TryOutcome::Completed(self.last_status.clone())
> }
> ```
> 
> The `in_boolean_context` flag must be set/cleared around condition evaluation in `if`, `while`, and the LHS of `&amp;&amp;`/`||`. This is a second flag, not the same as `in_try`. The two flags are independent: `in_boolean_context` suppresses the try-abort; `in_try` enables it. Nesting works because each `try` block is a separate `run_cmds_try` call on the Rust call stack — the abort returns to the nearest enclosing `run_cmds_try`, which is the inner `try`.
> 
> **Invariants for tests:**
> 
> 1. **Try-abort boundary**: a failing command inside `try` must not execute subsequent commands in the same block. Test: `try { false; echo SHOULD_NOT_PRINT }` — verify no output.
> 2. **Boolean exemption**: `try { if false { }; echo SHOULD_PRINT }` — the `if` condition's status does not trigger abort.
> 3. **Nesting isolation**: `try { try { false } else $e { echo inner }; echo outer }` — both "inner" and "outer" print. The inner `try` catches; the outer sees success.
> 4. **Status propagation without else**: `try { false }; echo $status` — status of the failing command propagates to enclosing scope.
> 5. **&amp;&amp;/|| exemption**: `try { false || true; echo YES }` — "YES" prints. The `false` on `||` LHS does not trigger abort.
> 6. **Nested boolean in try**: `try { if false { }; false }` — the `if` is exempt but the bare `false` aborts.
> 
> 
> ### 2. `capture_subprocess` as shared primitive (Phase D)
> 
> **Verdict: sound. `(String, i32)` is the correct product type for the primitive. The projections are the session type encoding.**
> 
> **Formal characterization.** `capture_subprocess` implements the ↓→↑ polarity shift (Levy 2004, §2.2): force a computation (negative), observe its effects (stdout + exit code), return the observation as a value (positive). The observation is a product `String × i32`, which is the canonical positive product in CBPV.
> 
> The two consumers are projections from this product:
> - `` `{cmd}`` = π₁ : `String × i32 → String` (Lens getter for the first component)
> - `try { cmd }` in value position = the full product, wrapped: `String × i32 → Sum("ok", T) | Sum("err", ExitCode(i32))`
> 
> This is correct optic structure. `capture_subprocess` returns the product; each consumer applies a different optic to it. They are siblings — parallel morphisms from the same source — not a desugaring chain.
> 
> **Why not return something richer?** The primitive could return `(String, String, i32)` — stdout, stderr, exit code. But stderr is the diagnostic channel (Plan 9's errstr), not a data channel. Including it in the product would require every consumer to decide what to do with stderr, complicating the optic structure. The spec's decision to keep stderr flowing to the parent's fd 2 is correct — it preserves the profunctor layering where stderr is handled by redirection (the wrapping layer), not by capture (the value layer). If stderr capture is ever needed, it should be a separate primitive or a redirect-then-capture composition, not a wider product.
> 
> **Protocol for the primitive:**
> 
> ```rust
> struct CaptureResult {
>     stdout: String,
>     exit_code: i32,
> }
> 
> /// The shared primitive. Both `{cmd} and try { cmd } call this.
> fn capture_subprocess(
>     &amp;mut self,
>     cmds: &amp;[Command],
> ) -&gt; CaptureResult {
>     // fork, pipe stdout, run cmds in child, waitpid, return
>     // stderr flows to parent's fd 2 (not captured)
> }
> ```
> 
> Then the two consumers:
> 
> ```rust
> // Command substitution: `{cmd}
> fn eval_command_sub(&amp;mut self, cmds: &amp;[Command]) -&gt; Val {
>     let result = self.capture_subprocess(cmds);
>     // π₁: project stdout, split on newlines, strip trailing newline
>     split_to_list(&amp;result.stdout)
> }
> 
> // try in value position: try { cmd }
> fn eval_try_value(&amp;mut self, cmds: &amp;[Command]) -&gt; Val {
>     let result = self.capture_subprocess(cmds);
>     if result.exit_code == 0 {
>         Val::Sum("ok".into(), Box::new(split_to_val(&amp;result.stdout)))
>     } else {
>         Val::Sum("err".into(), Box::new(Val::ExitCode(result.exit_code)))
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> **The `i32` is correct, not `ExitCode`.** Inside `CaptureResult`, the exit code is raw data — it has not yet crossed the computation→value boundary. `Val::ExitCode(i32)` is the reified form that enters the value world. The primitive returns raw `i32`; the consumer (`eval_try_value`) wraps it in `Val::ExitCode`. This preserves the spec's invariant that ExitCode enters the value world only through `try`.
> 
> 
> ### 3. `return` as polarity shift (Phase D)
> 
> **Verdict: sound. Use a dedicated enum, not `Result&lt;Val, Status&gt;`.**
> 
> **Formal characterization.** `return` is CBPV's `return : A → F(A)` (Levy 2004, §2.1, Def. 2.1). It injects a value from the value sort (A, positive) into the computation sort (F(A), negative-wrapping-positive). In the evaluator, this means `run_cmds` must be able to produce either a `Status` (the normal computation outcome — negative) or a `Val` (an injected value — positive-wrapped-in-negative).
> 
> **Why not `Result&lt;Val, Status&gt;`?** `Result` implies that `Val` is the success case and `Status` is the error case. But `Status::ok()` is not an error — it is the normal outcome of a command that succeeds without producing a value. The relationship is not success/failure but two different sorts of outcome. `Result` would impose a false error semantics and confuse `Status` error reporting with the sort distinction.
> 
> **The right return type:**
> 
> ```rust
> /// Outcome of running a command sequence.
> ///
> /// Commands produce Status (computation sort).
> /// `return` injects a Val (value sort into computation).
> /// This is CBPV's F(A) — the free computation on A.
> enum RunOutcome {
>     /// Normal command completion. The computation produced a status.
>     Status(Status),
>     /// A `return` statement injected a value.
>     /// CBPV's return : A → F(A).
>     Value(Val),
> }
> ```
> 
> **Interaction with the evaluator.** `run_cmds` currently returns `Status`. With `return`, it must return `RunOutcome`. The change propagates:
> 
> 1. `run_cmds` loops over commands. When `run_cmd` returns `RunOutcome::Value(v)`, the loop terminates immediately — `return` is the "early exit with value" operation. Subsequent commands are not executed.
> 2. In value-producing contexts (RHS of `let`, `match` in value position), the caller pattern-matches on `RunOutcome` and extracts the `Val`. If the body completes with `RunOutcome::Status(_)` (no `return`), the result is `Val::Unit` — the spec states "without `return`, the body produces `Unit`."
> 3. In non-value contexts (top-level, function body used for effects), `RunOutcome::Value(v)` is an error or is discarded depending on policy. I recommend: `return` outside a value-producing context is a runtime error (Status::err), matching how CBPV disallows return at the wrong sort.
> 
> **Type-theoretic constraint: `return` must be the ONLY way to produce `Value` from `run_cmds`.** If commands could implicitly produce values (e.g., the last expression's value), you'd have an implicit polarity shift — CBPV's coherence (Levy 2004, §2.3, Prop. 2.4) requires that shifts be explicit. The spec already enforces this: "Bare words in a body are always commands. `return` followed by a word is always a value injection."
> 
> **Interaction with `try`:**
> 
> ```rust
> fn run_cmds_try(&amp;mut self, cmds: &amp;[Command]) -&gt; TryOutcome {
>     for cmd in cmds {
>         match self.run_cmd(cmd) {
>             RunOutcome::Value(v) =&gt; return TryOutcome::Returned(v),
>             RunOutcome::Status(s) if !s.is_success() 
>                 &amp;&amp; !self.in_boolean_context =&gt; {
>                 return TryOutcome::Aborted(s);
>             }
>             RunOutcome::Status(_) =&gt; continue,
>         }
>     }
>     TryOutcome::Completed(self.last_status.clone())
> }
> 
> enum TryOutcome {
>     Completed(Status),
>     Aborted(Status),
>     Returned(Val),  // return inside try propagates the value out
> }
> ```
> 
> `return` inside a `try` block propagates outward — it does not trigger the error handler. This is correct: `return` is a value injection, not a failure. The `try` handler catches status failures (⅋ abort), not value productions.
> 
> 
> ### 4. `match` as coproduct elimination (Phase C)
> 
> **Verdict: conditionally sound. Exhaustiveness checking is the key constraint.**
> 
> **Formal characterization.** `match` on Sum values is the coproduct eliminator — the `case` construct of CBPV (Levy 2004, §2.1). For closed coproducts (fixed set of tags), exhaustiveness is required for totality. For open coproducts (arbitrary string tags), exhaustiveness is not statically checkable.
> 
> psh's Sum has **open tags** — any string is a valid tag. This means:
> 
> 1. **Static exhaustiveness is impossible** for arbitrary Sum values. The set of possible tags is unbounded.
> 2. **Conventional exhaustiveness** is possible for `Result[T]` (tags: ok, err) and `Maybe[T]` (tags: ok, none). Type annotations make the tag set finite and known.
> 3. **The `*` arm (wildcard)** is the catch-all that makes any match total.
> 
> **What should happen on unmatched tags?** Two options with different type-theoretic consequences:
> 
> **(a) Runtime error (Status::err).** An unmatched Sum is a failed pattern match — the computation aborts. This is the strict interpretation: the coproduct eliminator must be total, and missing an arm is a programmer error. This is consistent with the ⊕ convention — the status reports the failure.
> 
> **(b) Fall through with Unit/false status.** The match succeeds with no arm executed. This is the permissive interpretation, consistent with rc's `switch` where unmatched cases fall through.
> 
> I recommend **(a)** — runtime error on unmatched Sum tags — for structural arms, and **(b)** — fall through with failure status — for glob arms. The rationale: structural arms are coproduct elimination (must be total), glob arms are pattern matching on strings (may legitimately not match). The AST already distinguishes them: structural arms have `tag $binding`, glob arms have `pattern`.
> 
> **Properties that should hold:**
> 
> 1. **Exactly one arm fires** — no fall-through between arms. The `;` separator enforces this syntactically.
> 2. **Binding scope** — `$v` in `ok $v =&gt;` is scoped to the arm body. It's a μ̃-binder (let) introduced by the pattern. Must be pushed/popped with the arm's scope.
> 3. **Payload extraction is a Prism preview** — `ok $v` extracts the payload of `Sum("ok", payload)`. If the tag doesn't match, the Prism's `preview` returns `Nothing` and the arm is skipped.
> 4. **Glob arms match the display representation** — `match $result { 42 =&gt; ... }` matches `Val::Int(42)` because `Int(42).to_string() == "42"`. This is correct — glob arms operate on the string projection, consistent with rc heritage.
> 
> **Exhaustiveness checking (for typed contexts):**
> 
> ```rust
> /// When a match scrutinee has a type annotation with known tags,
> /// check that all tags are covered (or a wildcard exists).
> fn check_exhaustiveness(
>     type_ann: &amp;TypeAnnotation,
>     arms: &amp;[(MatchPattern, Vec&lt;Command&gt;)],
> ) -&gt; Result&lt;(), Vec&lt;String&gt;&gt;  // returns missing tags
> ```
> 
> This is a lint, not a hard error — the type system is advisory. But it catches the common bug of forgetting the `err` arm on a `Result[T]`.
> 
> 
> ### 5. Accessor composition (Phase C)
> 
> **Verdict: sound. The composition is AffineTraversal (Cartesian + Cocartesian). Failed Prism preview returns `Val::Unit`.**
> 
> **Formal characterization.** The accessor chain `$result.ok.name` composes two optics:
> - `.ok` : Prism — Cocartesian profunctor. `preview : Sum → Maybe(Payload)`.
> - `.name` : Lens — Cartesian profunctor. `view : Tuple → Field`.
> 
> Their composition is an AffineTraversal (Clarke et al. 2024, §4.2) — Cartesian + Cocartesian. An AffineTraversal has `preview : S → Maybe(A)` — it may fail to produce a value (the Prism part) but when it succeeds, it produces exactly one value (the Lens part).
> 
> **Composition rules:**
> 
> | First | Second | Result | Constraint |
> |-------|--------|--------|------------|
> | Lens | Lens | Lens | Cartesian composed with Cartesian |
> | Prism | Prism | Prism | Cocartesian composed with Cocartesian |
> | Prism | Lens | AffineTraversal | Cocartesian then Cartesian |
> | Lens | Prism | AffineTraversal | Cartesian then Cocartesian |
> | Any | AffineTraversal | AffineTraversal | Closed under composition |
> 
> **What `$x.ok` returns when x is `Sum("err", ...)`:** `Val::Unit`. This is the Prism preview returning `Nothing`, projected into psh's value model where `Nothing = Unit`. This is the correct choice because:
> 
> 1. `Unit` is already psh's "absence" value — unset variables produce it, empty lists produce it.
> 2. `Unit` is false, so `if $x.ok { ... }` naturally branches on whether the preview succeeded.
> 3. It's consistent with the spec's claim that Sum display is "payload only" — accessing a non-matching branch gives no payload.
> 
> **Implementation:**
> 
> ```rust
> /// Apply an accessor chain to a value.
> fn apply_accessor(val: &amp;Val, accessor: &amp;str) -&gt; Val {
>     match accessor {
>         // Numeric: tuple projection (Lens)
>         s if s.parse::&lt;usize&gt;().is_ok() =&gt; {
>             let idx = s.parse::&lt;usize&gt;().unwrap();
>             match val {
>                 Val::Tuple(fields) =&gt; fields.get(idx)
>                     .cloned()
>                     .unwrap_or(Val::Unit),
>                 _ =&gt; Val::Unit, // type error: not a tuple
>             }
>         }
>         // "code": ExitCode extraction
>         "code" =&gt; match val {
>             Val::ExitCode(n) =&gt; Val::Int(*n as i64),
>             // Also works on Sum("err", ExitCode(n))
>             Val::Sum(_, payload) =&gt; apply_accessor(payload, "code"),
>             _ =&gt; Val::Unit,
>         },
>         // Named: Sum tag projection (Prism preview)
>         tag =&gt; match val {
>             Val::Sum(t, payload) if t == tag =&gt; *payload.clone(),
>             Val::Sum(_, _) =&gt; Val::Unit, // tag doesn't match: Nothing
>             _ =&gt; Val::Unit, // not a Sum: Nothing
>         },
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> Chaining: `$result.ok.name` calls `apply_accessor(apply_accessor(result, "ok"), "name")`. If the first returns `Unit`, the second also returns `Unit` — `Unit` is an absorbing element for accessor chains, which is the correct behavior for `AffineTraversal` preview failure propagating through composition.
> 
> **Test invariants:**
> 
> 1. `$x.ok` where `x = ok 42` returns `Int(42)`.
> 2. `$x.ok` where `x = err 1` returns `Unit`.
> 3. `$x.ok.0` where `x = ok (1, 2)` returns `Int(1)`.
> 4. `$x.ok.0` where `x = err 1` returns `Unit` (Prism failure absorbs).
> 5. `$x.0` where `x = (1, 2)` returns `Int(1)`.
> 6. `$x.code` where `x = err ExitCode(42)` returns `Int(42)`.
> 
> 
> ### 6. The CBV-only `let` decision
> 
> **Verdict: sound. This is the right call. It does not close doors that matter.**
> 
> **Formal analysis.** In CBPV (Levy 2004, §2.4), there are two binding forms:
> - `let x = V in M` — CBV. Evaluates V (a value term), binds the result, continues with M.
> - `x ← M; N` — sequencing. Runs M (a computation), binds its result, continues with N. This is NOT call-by-name — it's monadic bind.
> 
> psh's `let x = val` is the first form. There is no syntactic form for the second in `let` position. But psh has it operationally: `` let x = `{ cmd } `` evaluates the command substitution eagerly (forces the computation) and binds the result. The CBV `let` combined with command substitution as the ↓→↑ shift gives you `x ← M; N` by composition: `let x = `{ M }; N`.
> 
> **What call-by-name `let` would give you:** lazy binding — `$x` re-evaluates the RHS on every access. The spec correctly identifies that this is served by `.get` disciplines: `fn x.get { x = `{ recompute } }`. The discipline mechanism is strictly more powerful than CBN `let` because:
> 
> 1. It separates the re-evaluation trigger (`.get` fires on access) from the binding (the stored value is still CBV).
> 2. It allows the re-evaluation to have side effects (write to stderr, fire a pane query) that CBN would also allow but less explicitly.
> 3. It composes with the MonadicLens laws — `.get` is a notification hook that doesn't transform the value, so PutGet/GetPut hold for the stored value.
> 
> CBN `let` would introduce a fourth tier of variable behavior (alongside stored, exported, nameref) and break the uniformity that all `let` bindings have known evaluation time. The MonadicLens laws would not hold for CBN variables (PutGet fails: storing a value then reading re-evaluates the original expression, not the stored value).
> 
> **Doors this closes:**
> - Reactive/streaming variable updates without `.get` — you can't write `let x = stream { ... }` where `$x` pulls from a stream. But `.get` disciplines serve this use case.
> - Memoized lazy evaluation — no Haskell-style thunks. Not meaningful for a shell; every external command has side effects.
> 
> **Doors this keeps open:**
> - `${ cmd }` shared-state comsub (deferred in PLAN.md) — runs in the parent process, no fork, captures stdout. This is the CBV monadic bind (`x ← M`) where M has access to the parent's state. It's still CBV — evaluated at binding time, result stored.
> - Session-typed coprocess channels — orthogonal to let semantics.
> - `.get` disciplines for any live-query pattern.
> 
> The CBV-only decision is aligned with CBPV's value/computation distinction: `let` binds values, which are by definition already evaluated. If you want to bind a computation, you force it first (`` `{ } `` or `try { }`). The polarity discipline is cleaner with CBV-only `let`.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Cross-cutting concern: `RunOutcome` propagation
> 
> The introduction of `RunOutcome` (from item 3) interacts with every other feature. Here is the complete type that unifies all the control flow:
> 
> ```rust
> /// The result of running a command or command sequence.
> /// This is the evaluator's F(A) — the free computation type.
> enum RunOutcome {
>     /// Normal completion with a status.
>     /// The ⊕ convention: caller inspects.
>     Status(Status),
> 
>     /// A `return` injected a value.
>     /// CBPV return : A → F(A).
>     Value(Val),
> }
> 
> /// Additional wrapper for try-block contexts.
> /// Adds the abort case without polluting non-try code paths.
> enum TryOutcome {
>     /// Block completed normally.
>     Completed(Status),
>     /// A command failed and try-abort triggered.
>     Aborted(Status),
>     /// A `return` produced a value (propagates through try).
>     Returned(Val),
> }
> ```
> 
> The key design principle: `RunOutcome` replaces `Status` as the return type of `run_cmd` and `run_cmds`. `TryOutcome` is local to `run_cmds_try` and does not escape. `try` in value position calls `capture_subprocess` (which runs in a child process and returns `CaptureResult`), NOT `run_cmds_try`. The two `try` forms share the keyword and the "fallible computation" semantics but use different evaluation mechanisms: scoped `try` uses in-process abort; value-position `try` uses fork+capture.</result>
