> Here is my analysis across the six questions. I'll reference the formal definitions from Clarke, Elkins, Gibbons, Loregian, Milewski, Pillmore, and Roman (2024) ("Profunctor optics, a categorical update," *Compositionality* 6:1) — hereafter "Clarke et al." — and the "Don't Fear the Profunctor Optics" reference material, grounded in psh's concrete types and the syntax/specification docs.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 1. Accessor Syntax as Optic Composition (Phase C)
> 
> ### The formal picture
> 
> The accessor chain `$result.ok.name` is a composition of optics:
> 
> - `.ok` : **Prism**((Val, Val), (Val, Val)) — focusing the `"ok"` branch of a Sum
> - `.name` : **Lens**((Val, Val), (Val, Val)) — focusing a named field of a product
> 
> Their composition is an **AffineTraversal** — which Clarke et al. define (Definition 4.12, line 1530) as:
> 
> &gt; **Affine**((A,B),(S,T)) := C(S, T + A x [B,T])
> 
> In the profunctor encoding:
> 
> ```
> type AffineP s t a b = forall p. (Cartesian p, Cocartesian p) =&gt; p a b -&gt; p s t
> ```
> 
> Composition of a Cartesian-constrained function (Lens) with a Cocartesian-constrained function (Prism) gives a function constrained by both — which is exactly AffineP. This is the key insight from the "Don't Fear" reference (ProfunctorOptics.md, "Optic Composition is Function Composition"): "When Haskell composes two functions, it merges the constraints imposed for each of them."
> 
> ### The design question: eager Val-&gt;Val chain vs. optic AST
> 
> **Option A: Eager chain of Val -&gt; Result&lt;Val, ()&gt; functions.**
> 
> Each accessor step is a function `Val -&gt; Result&lt;Val, ()&gt;` composed via `and_then`. At evaluation time, the evaluator resolves the variable, then applies each accessor left-to-right:
> 
> ```rust
> enum Accessor {
>     Index(usize),   // Tuple projection: .0, .1, ...
>     Tag(String),     // Sum preview: .ok, .err, .name
>     Code,            // ExitCode extraction: .code
> }
> 
> fn apply_accessor(val: &amp;Val, acc: &amp;Accessor) -&gt; Val {
>     match (val, acc) {
>         (Val::Tuple(elems), Accessor::Index(i)) =&gt; {
>             elems.get(*i).cloned().unwrap_or(Val::Unit)
>         }
>         (Val::Sum(tag, payload), Accessor::Tag(t)) =&gt; {
>             if tag == t { *payload.clone() } else { Val::Unit }
>         }
>         (Val::ExitCode(n), Accessor::Code) =&gt; Val::Int(*n as i64),
>         _ =&gt; Val::Unit,
>     }
> }
> 
> fn eval_accessor_chain(val: Val, accessors: &amp;[Accessor]) -&gt; Val {
>     accessors.iter().fold(val, |v, acc| apply_accessor(&amp;v, acc))
> }
> ```
> 
> **Option B: Reified optic AST that composes before application.**
> 
> Each accessor is an optic descriptor. The chain is composed into a single optic representation, then applied:
> 
> ```rust
> enum OpticKind {
>     Lens { index: usize },
>     Prism { tag: String },
>     Getter { extract: fn(&amp;Val) -&gt; Val },
> }
> 
> struct OpticChain(Vec&lt;OpticKind&gt;);
> 
> impl OpticChain {
>     fn classify(&amp;self) -&gt; OpticClass {
>         // If all Lens -&gt; Lens
>         // If all Prism -&gt; Prism  
>         // If mixed Lens+Prism -&gt; AffineTraversal
>         // ...
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> ### Trade-off analysis
> 
> The question is whether composing-then-applying buys anything over applying-step-by-step. In Haskell, the profunctor encoding's payoff is **polymorphic composition** — the same composed optic works with `view`, `set`, `over`, `review`, etc. But psh accessors are **read-only in the current spec**. There is no `$result.ok.name = newval` setter syntax. Accessors in value position are pure Getters.
> 
> This is the critical observation: **Getter is the degenerate optic** that Clarke et al. define (Definition 5.4, line 1739) as a plain morphism C(S,A) — "getters are degenerate optics for the trivial action on the covariant side." A Getter has no meaningful update direction. Composing Getters is just function composition.
> 
> Since the accessor chain is always read-only, the profunctor machinery (Tambara modules, constraint merging) gives no benefit over simple Val-&gt;Val function composition. The constraint merging that makes profunctor optics beautiful in Haskell — where you compose `first . the'` and get an AffineP for free, usable as both a getter and a setter — collapses when you only need the getter direction.
> 
> **My recommendation:** Option A (eager chain) for Phase C, with the optic classification tracked in the AST for error reporting and future set-direction extension. Concretely:
> 
> ```rust
> // In the AST (Phase B parser output)
> pub enum Word {
>     // ...existing variants...
>     /// Variable with accessor chain: $x.0.ok
>     VarAccess(String, Vec&lt;Accessor&gt;),
> }
> 
> pub enum Accessor {
>     /// .N — Tuple index (0-based). Lens.
>     Index(usize),
>     /// .name — Sum tag projection. Prism.
>     Tag(String),
>     /// .code — ExitCode-&gt;Int extraction. Getter.
>     Code,
> }
> ```
> 
> The evaluator applies the chain as a fold. The **optic classification is implicit in the accessor sequence** — the evaluator doesn't need to know whether it's applying a Lens, Prism, or AffineTraversal, because the behavior difference only matters for the set direction (which doesn't exist yet).
> 
> **If/when set-direction accessors arrive** (e.g., `$pos.0 = 99` or `match $result { ok $v =&gt; ... }` with a modification arm), the AST already has the accessor chain, and you can add the optic classification at that point. Don't build the Tambara module machinery now for a capability the language doesn't have.
> 
> This is the Refactoring Principle from CLAUDE.md: "find the solution requiring the least investment in infrastructure that is ultimately orthogonal to the functionality it temporarily supports."
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 2. Val's Optic Structure and Laws
> 
> ### Lens laws for Tuple projection
> 
> For `Accessor::Index(i)` on `Val::Tuple(elems)`:
> 
> - **view**: `elems[i].clone()`
> - **update**: `elems[i] = b; Tuple(elems)` (hypothetical)
> 
> The three Lens laws (from Optics.md):
> 
> 1. **GetPut** (ViewUpdate): `update(view(s), s) = s`
>    *"If you get the focused value and put it back, the whole is unchanged."*
>    In psh: `$pos.0` followed by putting it back into position 0 gives the original tuple. **Holds trivially for Val::Tuple** since Vec indexing is deterministic and cloning is exact.
> 
> 2. **PutGet** (UpdateView): `view(update(a, s)) = a`
>    *"If you put a value in and get it out, you get what you put."*
>    **Holds** for Tuple — replacing `elems[i]` with `a` then reading `elems[i]` yields `a`.
> 
> 3. **PutPut** (UpdateUpdate): `update(a2, update(a1, s)) = update(a2, s)`
>    *"Two successive puts are equivalent to the last put."*
>    **Holds** for Tuple — replacing `elems[i]` twice means only the last replacement is visible.
> 
> **Runtime enforcement:** psh can enforce GetPut and PutGet at eval time if setters are ever added (just as discipline functions enforce reentrancy invariants). For Phase C (read-only accessors), the laws are vacuously satisfied on the update side.
> 
> ### Prism laws for Sum preview
> 
> For `Accessor::Tag(t)` on `Val::Sum(tag, payload)`:
> 
> - **match** (preview): `if tag == t then Left(payload) else Right(s)`
> - **build** (review): `Sum(t, b)`
> 
> The two Prism laws (from Optics.md):
> 
> 1. **MatchBuild** (ReviewPreview): `either build id (match s) = s`
>    *"If you match and then rebuild, you get back the original."*
>    In psh: if `$result` is `Sum("ok", v)`, then matching on "ok" yields `Left(v)`, and `build("ok", v)` gives `Sum("ok", v)`. If it's `Sum("err", e)`, match returns `Right(Sum("err", e))` unchanged. **Holds.**
> 
> 2. **BuildMatch** (PreviewReview): `match(build(a)) = Left(a)`
>    *"If you build from a focus and then match, you always find the focus."*
>    In psh: `Sum("ok", v)` matched on "ok" always yields `Left(v)`. **Holds.**
> 
> ### AffineTraversal for composed access
> 
> `$result.ok.name` composes Prism (`.ok`) then Lens (`.name`). The Affine laws (from Optics.md):
> 
> 1. **PreviewSet**: `either (\a -&gt; set(a, s)) id (preview s) = s`
>    *"If the focus exists and you put it back, or if it doesn't exist, you get the original."*
> 
> 2. **SetPreview**: `preview(set(a, s)) = either (Left . const a) Right (preview s)`
>    *"Setting a value and previewing: if the focus existed, you see what you set."*
> 
> 3. **SetSet**: `set(a2, set(a1, s)) = set(a2, s)`
>    *"Two successive sets are the last set."*
> 
> **What psh enforces at runtime vs. morally correct:**
> 
> - **Read direction** (Phase C): All laws that involve only `preview`/`view`/`match` hold by construction. The evaluator's `apply_accessor` is deterministic and referentially transparent for Tuple and Sum.
> - **Write direction** (hypothetical): The laws would hold for Tuple mutation (Vec index assignment is well-behaved). For Sum, the Prism's "set only modifies if focus exists" property is the critical one — `$result.ok = newval` should be a no-op when `$result` is `Sum("err", ...)`. This is the Affine law's guarantee.
> - **Morally correct but not enforced:** The interaction between accessor evaluation and `.get` disciplines. If `fn x.get` has side effects that change `x`'s value between two accesses, the "PutGet" law fails in spirit (the stored value changed underneath). This is exactly the affine resource discipline from `specification.md` section "The namespace as context" — tier-3 (pane namespace) values don't admit contraction, so the lens laws for live-evaluated variables are not guaranteed. The laws hold for tier-1 (local) and tier-2 (env) variables.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 3. Free Carets vs. Accessors (Phase B/C)
> 
> ### What the accessor gives
> 
> The accessor enables the entire optic decomposition story for psh's type system. Without it:
> 
> - `$result.ok` is string concatenation: `Val::Str("...") ^ ".ok"` — the ok branch of a Result is inaccessible without a `match` block.
> - `$pos.0` is string concatenation: `Val::Str("...") ^ ".0"` — tuple projection requires destructuring in `let`.
> - `$e.code` is string concatenation — ExitCode extraction requires... nothing, because there's no way to get the int out.
> 
> With the accessor, every Val variant has a direct projection syntax. This is what makes the typed value model *usable* — without it, the 9-variant Val is expressive but inconvenient.
> 
> ### What's lost by breaking `$stem.c`
> 
> The rc idiom `$stem.c` (meaning `$stem ^ ".c"`, building a filename) is common in shell scripts that manipulate C source files. The escape hatches are:
> 
> 1. `${stem}.c` — brace-delimited variable name, then free caret with `.c`
> 2. `$stem^.c` — explicit caret
> 
> Both are clear, both are shorter than the accessor syntax they'd compete with. The cost is: **any rc script that uses `$var.something` as filename construction needs updating.** This is a real migration cost.
> 
> ### The optics perspective
> 
> From the optics standpoint, there's no question: **accessor priority is correct.** The free caret is string concatenation — it operates in the Str universe where all values are flat. The accessor operates in the structured value universe where products, coproducts, and ExitCodes have internal structure. The type system exists to distinguish these universes. Giving the accessor priority means `.` after a variable reference is always structural access, and string-level concatenation requires explicit opt-in.
> 
> The spec already made this call (syntax.md line 441-449). From my position, the spec is right. The accessor enables the optic hierarchy; the free caret is a convenience that has two clear escape hatches. The migration cost is real but bounded (grep for `$[a-z].*\.[a-z]` in existing scripts).
> 
> One refinement worth considering: the accessor should only fire when the name after `.` is a valid accessor (a digit, or a known structural tag). `$stem.c` where `c` is not a tuple index or known Sum tag could fall back to free caret. But this requires runtime type information at parse time (is `$stem` a Tuple? a Sum?) which the parser doesn't have. The spec's decision to make this purely syntactic (accessor always wins after `$varref.`) is the right call for an LL(1) grammar. Runtime dispatch would complicate the parser-evaluator boundary.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 4. `match` as Prism Elimination
> 
> ### The formal structure
> 
> `match $result { ok $v =&gt; body1; err $e =&gt; body2 }` is coproduct elimination. In the concrete Prism encoding:
> 
> ```
> match : s -&gt; Either a t
> ```
> 
> For Sum("ok", v), `match` returns `Left(v)` (the focus is present). For any non-matching tag, it returns `Right(s)` (the original, unmatched).
> 
> The question is what happens when **no arm matches**.
> 
> ### Analysis of the three options
> 
> **Option A: Fall through to a default arm (`* =&gt;`).**
> 
> This makes `match` a total function — every input produces an output. The glob `*` arm is the `Right` case of the Prism's match: "I couldn't find the focus, so here's the original." This is the most faithful to the Prism law structure:
> 
> - **MatchBuild**: `either build id (match s) = s` — the `* =&gt;` arm handles the `Right` case, and since it receives the original value, it satisfies the law trivially.
> - A `match` without `*` is like a partial Prism — which is an AffineTraversal where the "no focus" case is possible.
> 
> **Option B: Return Unit (Prism preview returning Nothing).**
> 
> This treats unmatched cases as "the focus wasn't there" and produces the empty value. In the optic reading, this is the AffineTraversal behavior — `preview` returns `None`, and the entire expression evaluates to the zero of the value lattice. The problem: this is **silent data loss**. If you `match` on `$result` and forget the `err` arm, you get Unit with no indication that something was unhandled.
> 
> **Option C: Error.**
> 
> This treats unmatched cases as a runtime error. This is not directly suggested by the Prism laws, but it's suggested by the **exhaustiveness** property of coproduct elimination. In a sequent calculus reading, eliminating a coproduct A+B requires handling both cases — leaving one out is an incomplete proof. An error signals that the elimination is non-total.
> 
> ### What the Prism laws suggest
> 
> The Prism laws are agnostic about what happens when the match fails — the `Right(t)` case in `match : s -&gt; Either a t` means "here's a value of the output type when the focus wasn't present." The question is what `t` is:
> 
> - If the match block is in **value position** (RHS of `let`), the output type is Val, and Unit (Option B) is the natural zero of Val. But this is lossy.
> - If the match block is in **command position** (for side effects), there's no output type to worry about, but silent failure is still bad.
> 
> **My recommendation:** The strongest option is a layered approach:
> 
> 1. **If a `*` arm exists**: use it. The match is total by construction.
> 2. **If no `*` arm and the match fails**: produce `Val::Unit` **and** set a nonzero Status. This is the ⊕ convention from `specification.md` — the caller can inspect `$status` to know the match was incomplete. Inside a `try` block, this triggers the ⅋ abort.
> 
> This gives the Prism behavior (Unit as the "no focus" result) while preserving error signaling through psh's existing ⊕ channel. The alternative — a hard runtime error — would make match unusable in contexts where the caller wants to handle the incomplete case themselves.
> 
> But this is a design choice with real ergonomic implications, so I'm presenting the tradeoffs for Lane to decide.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 5. Tuple Destructuring in `let`
> 
> ### The optic reading
> 
> `let (x, y) = $pos` where `$pos : (Int, Int)` is **parallel Lens decomposition** — two Lenses applied simultaneously to the same product:
> 
> - `pi_0 : Lens (A, B) A` — projects the first component
> - `pi_1 : Lens (A, B) B` — projects the second component
> 
> This is not optic composition (which would be sequential); it's **parallel application** of multiple Lenses to one product. In profunctor terms, it's the diagonal followed by two independent projections.
> 
> ### With type annotations
> 
> `let (x, y) : (Int, Int) = $pos` — the annotation constrains the whole product type. Each binding gets a projected type:
> 
> ```rust
> // At evaluation time:
> fn eval_tuple_destructure(
>     names: &amp;[(String, Option&lt;TypeAnnotation&gt;)],
>     whole_ann: Option&lt;&amp;TypeAnnotation&gt;,
>     val: Val,
> ) -&gt; Result&lt;Vec&lt;(String, Val)&gt;, Error&gt; {
>     // 1. Validate the whole against the annotation
>     if let Some(ann) = whole_ann {
>         validate_type(&amp;val, ann)?;
>     }
>     
>     // 2. Destructure
>     let elems = match &amp;val {
>         Val::Tuple(elems) =&gt; elems,
>         _ =&gt; return Err(Error::TypeMismatch("expected tuple")),
>     };
>     
>     if elems.len() != names.len() {
>         return Err(Error::ArityMismatch(names.len(), elems.len()));
>     }
>     
>     // 3. Each binding gets its projected element
>     Ok(names.iter().zip(elems.iter()).map(|((name, _), elem)| {
>         (name.clone(), elem.clone())
>     }).collect())
> }
> ```
> 
> The type annotation on the whole `(Int, Int)` validates the product structure. The Lens projection gives each binding its element. Component-level annotations (if ever supported — `let (x: Int, y: Str) = $pair`) would be Lens projection followed by Prism validation at each component.
> 
> ### The AST representation
> 
> The Binding enum needs a new variant:
> 
> ```rust
> pub enum Binding {
>     // ...existing variants...
>     /// Tuple destructuring: let (x, y) = expr
>     LetTuple {
>         names: Vec&lt;String&gt;,
>         value: Value,
>         mutable: bool,
>         export: bool,
>         type_ann: Option&lt;TypeAnnotation&gt;,  // annotation on the whole
>     },
> }
> ```
> 
> The key design question for the implementation: should nested destructuring be supported? `let ((x, y), z) = $nested_tuple` is composition of Lens projections. The profunctor encoding handles this naturally (compose `pi_0` with another `pi_0`), but the parser complexity scales. I'd suggest Phase D starts with flat destructuring only, and nested destructuring arrives if/when the need manifests. The accessor syntax `$nested.0.0` already provides the nested projection path.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 6. ExitCode and the `.code` Accessor
> 
> ### What optic is `.code`?
> 
> `$e.code` where `$e : ExitCode(n)` extracts the numeric code as an Int. This is:
> 
> - **Not a Lens**: ExitCode is not a product. There's no meaningful "rest" after extracting the code — ExitCode(n) has exactly one piece of data.
> - **Not a Prism**: It's not pattern-matching on a coproduct. The extraction always succeeds.
> - **Not an Iso**: An Iso requires a roundtrip. ExitCode(0) is true (success), Int(0) is false (zero). The truthiness semantics differ, so ExitCode &lt;-&gt; Int is not an isomorphism in psh's value category — the coercion table in syntax.md explicitly prohibits `ExitCode -&gt; Int` as a silent coercion because it "reverses truthiness."
> 
> This is a **Getter**. Clarke et al. (Definition 5.4, line 1739):
> 
> &gt; **Getters** (morphisms C(S,A)) are degenerate optics for the trivial action on the covariant side.
> 
> A Getter is a plain function S -&gt; A. In the profunctor encoding:
> 
> ```
> type GetterP s a = forall p. (Bicontravariant p, Profunctor p) =&gt; p a a -&gt; p s s
> ```
> 
> Or more pragmatically, it's just `ExitCode -&gt; Int`: a total, one-directional extraction with no update path. The spec's prohibition on `ExitCode -&gt; Int` coercion is exactly the statement that this is a Getter, not an Iso — the reverse direction doesn't exist as an implicit coercion.
> 
> ### How it fits the accessor chain
> 
> Since Getter composes with any optic to produce at most a Getter (in the constraint-merging view, Getter adds the strongest constraint — it eliminates the update direction), `.code` should be **terminal** in a chain:
> 
> - `$e.code` — valid: Getter applied to ExitCode, yields Int
> - `$result.err.code` — valid: Prism (`.err`) then Getter (`.code`), yields an AffineFold (might not find the focus, read-only)
> 
> But:
> 
> - `$e.code.something` — likely invalid unless `Val::Int` has further structure (it doesn't)
> 
> In the evaluator, `.code` is straightforward:
> 
> ```rust
> (Val::ExitCode(n), Accessor::Code) =&gt; Val::Int(*n as i64),
> ```
> 
> The interesting design point is that `.code` is the **only accessor that crosses sort boundaries** (from computation-outcome to data-integer). The spec is explicit that this crossing requires the explicit `.code` syntax — it's not implicit. This is exactly the Getter's role: it makes a one-way extraction explicit.
> 
> ### Recommendation for the accessor enum
> 
> ```rust
> pub enum Accessor {
>     /// .N — Tuple index (0-based). Optic: Lens.
>     Index(usize),
>     /// .tag — Sum tag projection. Optic: Prism.
>     Tag(String),
>     /// .code — ExitCode -&gt; Int extraction. Optic: Getter.
>     /// Terminal — cannot be followed by further accessors
>     /// (Int has no internal structure).
>     Code,
> }
> ```
> 
> The evaluator should note that after `.code` produces a `Val::Int`, any further accessor in the chain will hit the `_ =&gt; Val::Unit` default case. This is correct behavior (Int has no substructure), but a diagnostic warning at parse time ("accessor after .code will always yield Unit") would be valuable. This is where tracking the optic classification in the AST pays off — without running the evaluator, the parser can see that `.code` produces a Getter (no substructure), and warn if more accessors follow.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Summary table
> 
> | Accessor | Optic | Constraint (profunctor) | psh behavior |
> |---|---|---|---|
> | `.N` on Tuple | Lens | Cartesian | `elems[N].clone()` or Unit |
> | `.tag` on Sum | Prism | Cocartesian | payload if tag matches, else Unit |
> | `.code` on ExitCode | Getter | (degenerate) | `Int(n)` |
> | Prism then Lens | AffineTraversal | Cartesian + Cocartesian | composed fold, Unit on any miss |
> | anything then Getter | AffineFold/Fold | (degenerate) | read-only, composed fold |</result>

> <summary>Agent "Phase B: combine parser rewrite" completed</summary>
> <result>The parser rewrite is complete and committed.
> 
> ## Summary
> 
> **File changed:** `/Users/lane/src/lin/psh/src/parse.rs` (1863 lines, up from 1674)
> 
> **What the new parser does:**
> 
> 1. **Six-layer architecture** — lexical primitives, word atoms, words with free carets, expression precedence tower, commands, program. Each layer is a section of `RecParser` methods.
> 
> 2. **Free caret insertion** — after parsing a word atom, if the next character (no whitespace) can start another word atom (`$`, `'`, `` ` ``, `&lt;{`, `~`, or any `word_char`), an implicit `^` is inserted. This produces `Word::Concat(parts)`. Examples: `$home/bin` becomes `Concat[Var("home"), Literal("/bin")]`.
> 
> 3. **`${name}` brace-delimited variables** — uses `word_char` alphabet inside braces, enabling `${x.get}` to look up a variable named `x.get`.
> 
> 4. **Dual match syntax** — detects old `case`-based syntax (for the 206 existing tests) vs new `=&gt;` arm syntax. Inside `match { }`, if the first word is `case`, parses old-style; otherwise parses `pat =&gt; body` with `;`/newline separation.
> 
> 5. **Line continuation** — `\` immediately followed by `\n` is consumed as whitespace in both `skip_ws` and `skip_terminators`.
> 
> 6. **Extended type annotations** — `ExitCode`, `Result[T]`, `Maybe[T]` in addition to existing types.
> 
> 7. **Planned stubs** — `try` returns "try blocks not yet implemented". `return` is fully parsed. Accessor syntax (`$x.0`) deferred to Phase C — the `var_ref` combinator structure allows easy extension.
> 
> 8. **Keyword boundary checking** — `iffy` does not parse as `if` + `fy`; `fortune` does not parse as `for` + error.
> 
> **Test results:** 243 passed (206 existing + 37 new), 0 failed, 0 clippy warnings in parse.rs.</result>

### `2026-04-07T04:10:24.492Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>aae3f84fe5922fdca</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_017wTbpWFpRcv6x97JcWt9Bn</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/25f44a96-9488-444d-b842-3df1553f5366/tasks/aae3f84fe5922fdca.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Be: first-class functions + dispatch" completed</summary>
> <result>I have a thorough picture now. Let me compose the response.
> 
> ---
> 
> This is a genuinely interesting question, and the polarity concern is exactly right. Let me work through the five sub-questions with as much precision as I can bring.
> 
> ## 1. BMessage as universal carrier vs. function objects: was separation the right call?
> 
> Yes, and it was the right call for reasons deeper than anyone articulated at the time.
> 
> BMessage was positive — flat data, `BFlattenable`, copyable, serializable. BInvoker (the closest thing to a "function object" in the App Kit) was a completely separate mechanism: it held a `BMessage*` template, a `BMessenger` target, and an `Invoke()` method. Looking at the actual header (`/Users/lane/src/haiku/headers/os/app/Invoker.h`), the key thing is that BInvoker was explicitly `!Clone` — the copy constructor is private. It was a live handle to a negative capability: "I can cause something to happen at that endpoint."
> 
> The Media Kit had its own separate approach — virtual hook functions on BBufferConsumer/BBufferProducer that the framework called at the right time. These were pure C++ virtual dispatch, nothing to do with BMessage at all.
> 
> What would have happened if Be had tried to unify them? The specification already identifies the answer in the BMessage polarity analysis (spec lines 716-733): BMessage's `reply_port` was the one place where a negative continuation got embedded in a positive data container, and that was "the single largest source of" architectural confusion. Embedding a functor/invoker *inside* a BMessage would have been the same mistake, amplified. You'd get:
> 
> - **Serialization breakage.** BMessage was flattenable across address spaces (and even across the network in some prototypes). A function object can't cross those boundaries. The moment you put a closure in a BMessage, you lose the property that messages are network-transparent.
> - **Clone semantics collapse.** BMessage was copyable. Functions with mutable captures aren't. You'd need to decide: does copying a message copy the function? Share it? What happens to captured state?
> - **Lifetime coupling.** The BMessage might outlive the captures. The BMessage might be forwarded to a process where the captured state doesn't exist. These are the BMessage reply_port bugs, but worse.
> 
> The separation was correct. The question for psh is whether it can maintain that separation while still making functions first-class *within their own sort*.
> 
> ## 2. Named functions + call-by-name vs. functions as values: Be's scripting protocol
> 
> Be's scripting protocol was fundamentally address-by-name. Look at `BPropertyInfo` (`/Users/lane/src/haiku/headers/os/app/PropertyInfo.h`): it's a static table mapping property *names* to supported *operations* (B_GET_PROPERTY, B_SET_PROPERTY, B_EXECUTE_PROPERTY from `AppDefs.h`). The protocol was: send a message with the property name as a specifier, the handler resolves it via `ResolveSpecifier()`, and then dispatches. The newsletter article from Issue 4-2 explains this clearly — scripting was "a format for passing messages between applications" where "applications can inter-operate without necessarily having to be specially designed to work together."
> 
> The critical insight: **named dispatch was what made the protocol composable**. You could script any application because you addressed properties by name, and handlers self-described their capabilities via `GetSupportedSuites()`. If functions had been passed as values instead, you'd need the receiver to understand the sender's function representation. The name is the universal interface.
> 
> psh's current model (`fn name { body }` + call by name) maps directly to this. It's the same pattern: the function table is the equivalent of `BPropertyInfo`, and looking up a function by name is the equivalent of `ResolveSpecifier()`. This is simple and it composes.
> 
> First-class functions would change the model to "pass the function itself." The Be experience suggests this is a *different* kind of power — useful within a single runtime, but hostile to the cross-boundary composition that made scripting work. The question is whether psh needs both, and if so, how to keep them from contaminating each other.
> 
> ## 3. The polarity problem: embedding negative in positive via U(C)
> 
> You've identified this precisely. Val is positive: CBV, Clone, inert. Functions are negative: they consume arguments, they may have effects, they can't be inspected without running them.
> 
> CBPV's `U(C)` — thunking a computation into a value — is the right theoretical tool. But the question is what concrete form it takes in psh.
> 
> Here's where Be's experience is most directly instructive. Be had **three** different reifications of negative capabilities as positive data:
> 
> 1. **BMessenger** — a copyable, serializable handle to a BHandler (negative endpoint). This is `U(BHandler)` in CBPV terms. It doesn't carry the handler; it carries a way to *invoke* the handler. The polarity classification table confirms this: BMessenger is "Positive (shifted negative): ↑(BHandler)."
> 
> 2. **BInvoker** — a non-copyable pairing of a message template with a target. More like a partially applied function. Not serializable.
> 
> 3. **reply_port** — a one-shot negative continuation embedded directly in BMessage data. The thing that caused bugs.
> 
> The lesson: **BMessenger got it right.** It was a *reference* to a computation, not the computation itself. It was copyable because it was just an address (team, port, token). It was serializable. It didn't carry captured state. And crucially, invoking it required sending a message — an explicit shift from the value world back into the computation world.
> 
> For psh, the clean translation is: **a function value is a name**, not a closure.
> 
> ```
> fn greet { echo 'hello '$1 }
> let f = greet                    # Val::Fn("greet") — a name, positive
> $f world                         # resolves name, invokes — shift back to negative
> ```
> 
> This is `U(C)` where the thunk is implemented as a name in the function table. The name is positive (a string, clonable, displayable). The thing it names is negative (a computation). The boundary crossing happens at invocation.
> 
> The **BMessenger pattern**, not the closure pattern. No captured environment. No GC needed. The function table is the "registry" (like BRoster), and the function value is the "messenger" — a copyable handle to a named computation.
> 
> What about closures — functions that capture their environment? This is where `es` went and needed a GC. The Be-informed answer is: **don't**. BMessenger didn't carry the handler's state. It relied on the handler being alive at the target. Similarly, a function name relies on the function being defined when it's called. If the function is deleted (`fn name {}` to undefine in rc), the name becomes dangling — but that's the same class of problem as a BMessenger pointing at a dead handler, and the same solution works: you get an error at invocation time, not at capture time.
> 
> If closures are genuinely needed (and they may be — pipelines like `filter { |x| $x.size &gt; 1024 }` are compelling), the honest approach is to fork: `@{ body }` already provides isolated scope + computation. A closure would be `@{ }` deferred — a subshell that hasn't run yet. But this is a heavyweight mechanism (it forks), and the lightweight version (name-based) should be the default.
> 
> ## 4. Lambda syntax
> 
> `es` used `@ args { body }`. psh already uses `@{ body }` for subshells (explicit namespace fork). These are close enough to collide.
> 
> The distinction that matters is:
> 
> - `@{ body }` = fork immediately, isolated scope (classical contraction — already defined)
> - A lambda = deferred computation, captures... what exactly?
> 
> If functions are names (the BMessenger pattern from point 3), you don't need lambda syntax at all for the common case. `fn name { body }` defines it, `let f = name` captures the reference. The question is only whether you need *anonymous* functions.
> 
> If you do want anonymous functions, the syntax needs to be unambiguous against `@{ }`. Some options:
> 
> - **`{ |args| body }`** — Ruby-style. Clean, but `{ }` already means braced block.
> - **`fn { body }`** — anonymous fn. Reads naturally: "a function without a name." The parser already knows `fn`: if a `{` follows instead of a NAME, it's anonymous.
> - **`\args { body }`** — Haskell-ish. But backslash is line continuation in psh.
> 
> My recommendation: **`fn { body }`** for anonymous functions, if you need them at all. It's consistent with the existing grammar (`fn` keyword), the parser can distinguish `fn NAME {` from `fn {` trivially, and it reads as "an unnamed function." Arguments come from `$1, $2, ...` as with named functions, which is the rc convention.
> 
> But I'd encourage deferring this. Named functions as values (the BMessenger pattern) cover 90% of the use cases without any new syntax.
> 
> ## 5. Display, truthiness, and concat for function values
> 
> If `Val::Fn(String)` is a name-reference:
> 
> **Display (`echo $f`):** The function's name. This is what `whatis` does in rc — `whatis greet` prints the definition. `echo $f` should print the name, not the body. The name is the value; the body is what the name resolves to. BMessenger's equivalent would be printing the target's team/port/token triple — the *address*, not the handler's implementation.
> 
> **Truthiness (`if $f`):** True if the function exists in the function table, false if it's dangling. This follows the pattern: `Val::Fn("greet")` is truthy when `greet` is defined, falsy when it isn't. It's the BMessenger validity check — `BMessenger::IsValid()`. This is more useful than "always true" because it lets you test `if $callback { $callback $args }` — the same pattern as checking a BMessenger before sending through it.
> 
> **Concat (`$f^suffix`):** String concatenation on the name. `$f^_v2` produces `Val::Fn("greet_v2")` if the resulting name exists, or `Val::Str("greet_v2")` if it doesn't. Actually — this is where I'd push back. Concat should just produce a string. Don't try to be clever about re-resolving. `$f^suffix` gives `Val::Str("greetsuffix")`. If the user wants a function reference, they go through `let g = greet_v2` explicitly. The shift from string to function-reference should always be explicit, never implicit through concat. Concat is a string operation; function resolution is a separate concern.
> 
> ## Summary recommendation
> 
> The Be-informed design is:
> 
> 1. **Functions as names, not closures.** `Val::Fn(String)` — a positive reference to a negative computation. The BMessenger pattern. No GC, no capture semantics, no polarity violation.
> 
> 2. **Named functions remain the primary mechanism.** `fn name { body }` + `let f = name` + `$f args`. The scripting protocol pattern: address by name, resolve at invocation.
> 
> 3. **Defer anonymous functions and closures.** They can be added later as `fn { body }` if needed, but the named-reference model should prove sufficient for pipelines, callbacks, and composition. If closures become necessary, `@{ }` (forked subshell) is already the mechanism for isolated-scope computation — a "closure" would be a deferred `@{ }`, which is heavyweight and honest about what it costs.
> 
> 4. **Display = name, truthiness = name-exists, concat = string coercion.** Clean, predictable, no surprises.
> 
> The key principle: **the shift from value to computation should always be visible**. BMessenger made you explicitly `SendMessage()` to invoke. psh should make you explicitly use `$f args` to invoke. The function-as-value is a *handle*, not the computation itself. This is what CBPV's `U(C)` means concretely: you wrap the computation in a thunk (the name), and you force the thunk (by invoking the name as a command). The wrapper is inert and positive. The forcing is effectful and negative. The boundary is always explicit.</result>
> <usage><total_tokens>76109</total_tokens><tool_uses>41</tool_uses><duration_ms>220406</duration_ms></usage>
> </task-notification>

### `2026-04-07T04:10:24.495Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a80e8a3ddd32a983f</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01R5gXEvaqzHHUM8GC4BPrQe</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/25f44a96-9488-444d-b842-3df1553f5366/tasks/a80e8a3ddd32a983f.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Plan 9: first-class functions in shell" completed</summary>
> <result>I now have a comprehensive picture of both the theoretical framework and the concrete implementation. Let me compose my response.
> 
> ---
> 
> Here is my assessment, addressing each of your five questions. I'll be direct about where Plan 9's philosophy applies, where it doesn't, and where psh has already made the decision.
> 
> ## 1. Has psh already crossed the threshold?
> 
> Yes. The "simplicity" argument from rc.ms does not hold against first-class functions for psh, because psh already departed from rc's design position in three load-bearing ways:
> 
> **rc's design position was explicitly anti-language.** Duff: "I have tried to fix its best-acknowledged shortcomings and to simplify things wherever possible, usually by omitting inessential features. Only when irresistibly tempted have I introduced novel ideas" (rc.ms, Design Principles). rc had no types, no structured data, no pattern matching. Functions existed as named command sequences stored in `/env/fn#name` -- they were opaque strings that the kernel persisted, not values the shell manipulated.
> 
> **psh has already added:**
> - A 9-variant typed `Val` enum with products (Tuple, Lens), coproducts (Sum, Prism), and ExitCode
> - `match` with structural pattern matching on Sum values
> - `try` as a scoped error-handling construct with value-position form returning `Result[T]`
> - `return` as an explicit polarity shift (command sort -&gt; value sort)
> - Type annotations with union types, `Result[T]`, `Maybe[T]`
> 
> These are not cosmetic extensions to rc. They are programming language constructs. psh has `match` arms that decompose coproducts and `return` that injects values -- this is a language with a type system, not a command interpreter with some convenience features. Duff's principle that "the shell is not a programming language" was a design *choice*, not a law of nature, and psh has already chosen differently.
> 
> **The honest statement:** psh is a programming language for system composition with rc heritage syntax. Once you have typed values, structural pattern matching, and value-producing blocks, the question is not "should we add first-class functions" but "what is the right form for first-class functions in a language that already has all the machinery around them."
> 
> rc's grammar was about 15 productions. psh's syntax.md already has roughly 35 distinct named productions (counting `match_arm`, `match_pat`, `try_cmd`, `tagged_val`, `accessor`, `type_ann`, etc.). The grammar ship sailed with `match` and `try`.
> 
> ## 2. rc's `/env/fn#name` as precedent
> 
> This is actually important. In rc, `fn g { grep $1 *.[hcyl] }` stored the function text in `/env/fn#g` -- a file in the per-process-group environment filesystem (rc.ms, Environment: "Functions also appear in the environment, named by prefixing `fn#` to their names, like `/env/fn#roff`").
> 
> This means rc's functions were *already serialized values* at the kernel level. The kernel stored them as strings. `whatis g` would print `fn g {grep -e $1 *.[hycl]}` -- the function's textual representation. They were inherited by child processes through the environment. They were inspectable. They were, in a real sense, data.
> 
> What rc *didn't* do was let you manipulate them as data within the shell language. You couldn't write `x = `{whatis g}` and then use `$x` as a callable thing (without `eval`, which Duff explicitly called a rule-breaker). The `/env/fn#name` representation was a serialization mechanism for the process group, not an in-language abstraction.
> 
> **What this teaches psh:** The Plan 9 environment already proves that functions *can* be represented as data without philosophical contradiction. The question is whether the shell's own value model should reflect what the environment already knows. rc kept the wall because rc wasn't a language with typed values. psh has typed values. The wall is artificial in psh's context.
> 
> ## 3. The GC question -- es's experience and Rust's alternative
> 
> This is the hardest technical question. Let me be honest about the costs.
> 
> **es's situation.** es (Rakitzis, ~1993) made functions first-class values. A function was a closure that captured its lexical environment. Because closures could reference each other, form cycles, and be stored in variables that outlived their defining scope, es needed a GC. Paul Haahr and Byron Rakitzis implemented a mark-and-sweep collector. This was a real cost: it complicated the implementation, introduced pause-time concerns, and made the shell's resource behavior less predictable.
> 
> **Why es needed a GC and psh might not.** The critical question is whether closures can form reference cycles. In es:
> - Functions captured their environment by reference (shared mutable state)
> - A closure stored in a variable could reference that same variable's scope
> - Two closures could mutually reference each other's scopes
> 
> psh's situation is different because of two properties:
> 
> 1. **Capture by value (Clone).** If closures capture their environment by cloning the values at definition time, there are no shared references. No shared references means no reference cycles. No reference cycles means `Arc` / reference counting is sound, and even `Arc` is unnecessary -- you can just `Clone` the captured `Vec&lt;(String, Val)&gt;`. This is exactly what Rust's ownership model buys you. The cost is that captured values are *snapshots*, not live references.
> 
> 2. **No mutable captured state.** If closures cannot mutate their captured environment (they can only read the snapshot), then a closure is a pure function from arguments to effects. This is a strong restriction, but it aligns with psh's existing CBV discipline: `let` is always eager, values are always fully evaluated.
> 
> **The concrete cost of capture-by-value:**
> 
> ```
> let x = 42
> let f = fn { echo $x }   # captures x=42 at definition time
> x = 100
> $f                         # prints 42, not 100
> ```
> 
> This is not pathological -- it's what POSIX shell functions do with variables in the environment at fork time. But it means closures are not live bindings into the enclosing scope. They're frozen snapshots. es gave you live bindings at the cost of a GC. psh would give you frozen snapshots at the cost of no GC.
> 
> **My assessment:** Capture-by-value without a GC is the right tradeoff for a shell. The `Val` enum is already `Clone`. A closure's captured environment is `Vec&lt;(String, Val)&gt;`, which is `Clone`. No `Rc`, no `Arc`, no GC. The restriction (no live capture) is honest -- it matches fork semantics, which shell users already understand: "a subshell gets a copy of the parent's variables." A first-class function gets a copy of the defining scope's variables. Same mental model.
> 
> **What you cannot do:** Mutable shared state between a closure and its defining scope. No upward funarg that mutates a parent's variable. This is a real limitation. If Lane needs this, you need `Rc&lt;RefCell&lt;Val&gt;&gt;` or similar, which brings reference counting (though still not a full GC, and Rust prevents cycles in `Rc` by default).
> 
> **Confidence:** High that capture-by-value + Clone is sufficient for shell use cases. Medium confidence that the "frozen snapshot" semantics won't surprise users who expect live binding. The es comparison is instructive: es's live closures were elegant but required machinery that a shell shouldn't need.
> 
> ## 4. What first-class functions enable -- concrete examples
> 
> **Callbacks for service supervision:**
> ```
> fn with_retry attempts cmd {
>     for i in `{seq 1 $attempts} {
>         try {
>             $cmd
>             return
>         }
>     }
>     return err 'retries exhausted'
> }
> 
> with_retry 3 fn { curl -f http://service/health }
> ```
> 
> Without first-class functions, you'd write this as `with_retry 3 'curl -f http://service/health'` and use `eval` internally -- exactly the re-scanning hole Duff eliminated.
> 
> **Higher-order list operations:**
> ```
> fn filter pred list {
>     let result = ()
>     for x in $list {
>         if $pred $x =&gt; result = ($result $x)
>     }
>     return $result
> }
> 
> let big = filter fn { test $1 -gt 100 } $numbers
> ```
> 
> Without first-class functions, you pass a function name as a string and call it via `$pred $1` (which already works in psh, since `$pred` expands to a command name). This is the rc pattern: pass the name, not the function. It works. First-class functions make it *compositional* rather than stringly-typed.
> 
> **Discipline function factories:**
> ```
> fn make_validator pattern {
>     return fn { ~ $1 $pattern || error 'invalid: '$1 }
> }
> 
> fn hostname.set `{make_validator '[a-z]*.[a-z]*'}
> ```
> 
> This is where first-class functions interact with psh's ksh93 heritage. Discipline functions are currently names in a global table. With first-class functions, a discipline could be a closure that captures its validation pattern. Without them, you'd need a separate named function for each validator -- workable but not compositional.
> 
> **Pipeline construction:**
> ```
> fn apply_filters filters input {
>     let result = $input
>     for f in $filters {
>         result = `{ echo $result | $f }
>     }
>     return $result
> }
> 
> let pipeline = (fn { grep -v '#' } fn { sort } fn { uniq })
> apply_filters $pipeline `{cat config.txt}
> ```
> 
> **My honest assessment of these examples:** The first two (callbacks, higher-order list ops) are genuinely useful for system scripts. The discipline factory is elegant but how common is it really? The pipeline construction is a toy -- you'd just write `grep -v '#' config.txt | sort | uniq`. The strongest argument is the callback pattern: `with_retry`, `with_timeout`, `on_change` -- these are real service-supervision patterns where passing a *command* (not a string) matters for correctness.
> 
> **What you can do today without first-class functions:** Pass function names as strings. `$name $args` already calls the named function. This is rc's model and it works for most cases. First-class functions add: (a) anonymous functions (no need to pollute the global namespace), (b) captured state (the closure carries its context), (c) compositional passing (no `eval`, no re-scanning).
> 
> ## 5. Risk to simplicity -- grammar impact
> 
> First-class functions require adding to the grammar:
> 
> **Minimal addition (one production, one new `word_atom` alternative):**
> ```
> word_atom   = ... | fn_literal
> fn_literal  = 'fn' param_list? body
> param_list  = NAME+
> ```
> 
> That's it. `fn` is already a keyword. `body` is already defined as `'{' program '}' | '=&gt;' command`. The lambda form reuses existing productions. You're adding one alternative to `word_atom` and one new production (`fn_literal`). Call it 1.5 new productions.
> 
> **What changes in semantics (not grammar):**
> - `Val` gains a variant: `Val::Fn(Vec&lt;String&gt;, Vec&lt;Command&gt;, CapturedEnv)`
> - `eval_word` handles `Word::FnLiteral` by capturing the current scope
> - Command dispatch checks if the first arg is `Val::Fn` and calls it directly
> - `whatis` prints the function literal
> 
> **What does NOT change:**
> - `fn name { body }` still defines a named global function (rc heritage)
> - Named functions still stored in `Env::functions` as `Vec&lt;Command&gt;`
> - Discipline functions still keyed by name (`fn x.get { }`)
> 
> The grammar grows by maybe 2 productions out of ~35. The semantics grow by one Val variant and one dispatch path. This is not the es situation where first-class functions required rethinking the entire execution model.
> 
> ## The polarity question
> 
> You flagged this: putting a function in `Val` embeds a negative thing in a positive container. This is real. Let me address it directly.
> 
> **Val is positive: Clone, inert, no embedded continuations.** The specification (BMessage polarity confusion section) explicitly rejected `Val::Err` because it embeds "a computation-mode signal (negative) in a value-mode type (positive)." Does `Val::Fn` commit the same sin?
> 
> **Not exactly, if closures are frozen.** A frozen closure (captured-by-value, no mutable state) is *data*. It's a serializable representation of a computation that hasn't happened yet -- a thunk in CBPV terms. In Levy's CBPV framework, `U(A -&gt; F B)` is the positive type that wraps a negative computation type. The `U` (thunk) operator is exactly the polarity shift that makes it safe to store a negative thing in a positive context. `Val::Fn` is `U(function)`.
> 
> The key invariant: **a stored closure doesn't *do* anything until forced.** It's data. It's `Clone`. It doesn't hold file descriptors. It doesn't have a continuation. It has a list of captured values (positive) and a body (syntax tree, data). Forcing it (calling it) is the polarity shift from positive back to negative -- the `↓` (force) operator.
> 
> This is different from BMessage's reply_port, which was a *live* negative resource (a one-shot continuation) embedded in positive data. A frozen closure is not a live resource. It's a recipe.
> 
> **The discipline: `Val::Fn` must not hold linear resources.** No file descriptors, no `ReplyPort`, no mutable references. If the captured environment is `Vec&lt;(String, Val)&gt;` and `Val` is `Clone`, this holds by construction. The moment you allow closures to capture non-Clone things, you've reintroduced the BMessage mistake. Rust's type system enforces this: `Val: Clone` means `Val::Fn`'s captured environment must be `Clone`.
> 
> **My verdict:** `Val::Fn` with captured-by-value semantics respects the polarity discipline. It is `U(negative)` -- a thunked computation stored as positive data. The specification's existing framework supports this. The danger is if someone later tries to make closures capture mutable state or linear resources -- that must be prohibited by the type, not by convention.
> 
> ## Summary recommendation
> 
> | Aspect | Assessment |
> |--------|-----------|
> | Threshold already crossed? | Yes. psh is a language, not rc. |
> | GC required? | No. Capture-by-value + Clone is sufficient. |
> | Grammar cost | ~2 productions. Minimal. |
> | Polarity violation? | No, if closures are frozen thunks (CBPV's U operator). |
> | Strongest use case | Callbacks for system composition (retry, timeout, on_change). |
> | Weakest use case | Pipeline construction (just use actual pipelines). |
> | Risk | "Frozen snapshot" semantics may surprise users expecting live bindings. Make this explicit in documentation. |
> | Alternative | Pass function names as strings (rc model). Works, but stringly-typed. |
> | Confidence | High that this is feasible and clean. Medium on whether the use cases justify the complexity *now* vs. implementing it when real scripts demand it. |
> 
> The Plan 9 perspective says: implement what you need, not what looks elegant. If the first real psh system scripts (s6 run/finish, service configuration) need callbacks, build first-class functions. If they don't, the rc model (pass function names as strings) is honest and sufficient. But the *design* should not close the door -- `Val::Fn` is theoretically sound and mechanically cheap. The question is timing, not architecture.</result>

> <summary>Agent "Optics: functions in the value hierarchy" completed</summary>
> <result>Now I have a comprehensive picture. Let me compose the analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Roundtable Response: First-Class Functions and the Optic Hierarchy
> 
> ### 1. Where do functions sit in the optic hierarchy?
> 
> The short answer: **exponentials correspond to Grates, not to Lenses/Prisms/Traversals.**
> 
> Clarke et al. 2024 (§Grates, Definition at line ~1655 of `arxivmain.tex`) define a **Grate** as:
> 
> &gt; **Grate**((A, B), (S, T)) := D([S, A] ● B, T)
> 
> where [S, A] is the internal hom (exponential). In the Haskell profunctor encoding, a Grate is:
> 
> ```
> type GrateP s t a b = forall p. Closed p =&gt; p a b -&gt; p s t
> ```
> 
> where `Closed` is the profunctor typeclass for exponentials:
> 
> ```haskell
> class Profunctor p =&gt; Closed p where
>   closed :: p a b -&gt; p (x -&gt; a) (x -&gt; b)
> ```
> 
> This is the **dual of Cartesian**. Where Cartesian (Strong) says "a focus inside a product," Closed says "a focus inside an exponential." The concrete representation of a Grate is a single function:
> 
> ```
> grate :: ((S -&gt; A) -&gt; B) -&gt; T
> ```
> 
> This is a nested continuation: you provide a way to make a B from any view function S -&gt; A, and the Grate builds a T. It's the optic of **representable functors** — containers whose shape is determined by a fixed index set.
> 
> **Where this maps in psh's hierarchy:**
> 
> | Type constructor | Optic | Profunctor constraint |
> |---|---|---|
> | Tuple (×) | Lens | Cartesian (Strong) |
> | Sum (+) | Prism | Cocartesian (Choice) |
> | List | Traversal | Monoidal |
> | Function (→) | **Grate** | **Closed** |
> 
> Clarke et al. note (line ~1685): "The description of a grate and its profunctor representation in terms of 'closed' profunctors was first reported by Deikun and O'Connor; it can be seen as a consequence of the profunctor representation theorem."
> 
> There is also a **Glass** (Clarke et al. Definition at line ~1701), which is the composition of a Lens and a Grate:
> 
> &gt; **Glass**((A, B), (S, T)) := C(S x [[S, A], B], T)
> 
> In Haskell: `forall p. (Cartesian p, Closed p) =&gt; p a b -&gt; p s t`. A Glass requires both product and exponential structure — it is to Lens+Grate what AffineTraversal is to Lens+Prism.
> 
> **The hierarchy extends:**
> 
> ```
>                 Adapter (Profunctor)
>                /                    \
>           Lens (Cartesian)       Prism (Cocartesian)
>              |        \           /        |
>              |    AffineTraversal          |
>         Grate (Closed)     |               |
>              |        Traversal (Monoidal) |
>         Glass (Cartesian+Closed)           |
>              |                             |
>              ...                          ...
> ```
> 
> So: functions DO have a natural place in the optic hierarchy. They live on the **Closed/Grate** branch. A Grate is not an accessor in the Lens sense — you cannot `view` a single element from a Grate (there is no `view :: s -&gt; a`). What you can do is **reconstruct the whole from a transformation of the focus**: given any way to extract A from S (a view function S -&gt; A), and a way to produce B from that, you can build T.
> 
> ### 2. Functions as morphisms vs. data: does Val need to be cartesian closed?
> 
> This is the critical design question. Currently Val has:
> - Products (Tuple) — makes it cartesian
> - Coproducts (Sum) — makes it cocartesian
> - Lists — adds the monoidal/traversal layer
> 
> Adding functions (A -&gt; B as a Val) would make Val's category **cartesian closed** (CCC). This is a significant categorical upgrade. Here is what changes:
> 
> **What you gain:**
> - **Closedness.** The Grate/Glass branch of the optic hierarchy becomes operationally reachable. Currently it's theoretically present but inert — no psh value has exponential structure.
> - **Higher-order composition.** Users can build combinators, pass strategies as data, abstract over behavior.
> - **Completeness of the four connectives.** The specification's four-connective table has `&amp; (with)` as "Handler/Offer (future)." A cartesian closed category has all four connectives populated.
> 
> **What you lose or risk:**
> - **Equality.** Functions are not comparable. `Val` currently derives `PartialEq`. A function variant breaks this. You'd need to either remove `PartialEq` from Val (disruptive) or make function equality always-false/always-error (semantically dubious). This is not merely an implementation annoyance — it means `match` on a Tuple containing a function cannot use structural equality for that position.
> - **Display.** Functions have no natural string representation. `echo $f` would need to print... what? The function's name? Its body source? An opaque marker like `&lt;fn&gt;` or `fn(arity=2)`? This matters because psh passes values through pipes as text.
> - **Serialization across fork/pipe boundaries.** Val crosses the fork boundary (command substitution, subshells). Functions — which are closures over the shell environment — do NOT cross fork boundaries cleanly. A function's body is a `Vec&lt;Command&gt;`, which is Clone, but its meaning depends on the lexical environment at the definition site. Fork copies the whole shell state, but pipe serialization discards it.
> - **Val's inertness.** The specification (§BMessage polarity confusion) explicitly argues that Val must be **pure positive data, Clone, no embedded continuations**. A function body is a **suspended computation** — it is negative. Embedding a negative value in a positive type is exactly the BMessage mistake the spec warns against.
> 
> **The categorical tension:** In the sequent calculus reading, Val is the value sort (positive, CBV). Functions live in the command/computation sort (negative, CBN). Putting functions into Val crosses the polarity boundary. In a cartesian closed category, this crossing is mediated by the exponential adjunction (curry/uncurry). But psh is a **duploid**, not a CCC — the whole point is that the two polarities are distinct with non-associative cross-polarity composition.
> 
> **My assessment:** The specification already has the right analysis. Functions are morphisms. Val is objects. The duploid structure depends on keeping them separate. Making Val cartesian closed would flatten the polarity distinction that the entire theoretical architecture rests on.
> 
> ### 3. The practical question: what happens if Val gains a function variant?
> 
> **`$f.something` — accessors on functions.** A function value COULD support Getters (read-only optics):
> - `.arity` — number of parameters (if known)
> - `.name` — the function's name
> - `.body` — source representation
> 
> But these are metadata Getters, not structural optics. They don't compose with Lenses/Prisms in a useful way. `.arity` is a Getter (C(S, A), degenerate optic with trivial action on the contravariant side). It does not participate in update.
> 
> **`match $x { ... }` on a function.** Structural matching on a function is meaningless. You cannot decompose a function the way you decompose a Sum (Prism's `match :: S -&gt; A + T`) or a Tuple (Lens's `view :: S -&gt; A`). Functions are opaque — they are defined by their **behavior**, not their **structure**. Any pattern match on a function would reduce to testing the tag "is this a function?" — a trivial Prism `Val -&gt; Fn + NotFn`, which gives you nothing useful.
> 
> **`$f ^ $g` — concat of functions.** Concat is rc's fundamental operation on values. It coerces both operands to Str via Display and concatenates. For functions, Display would produce something like `&lt;fn:greet&gt;`, so `$f ^ $g` would produce `&lt;fn:greet&gt;&lt;fn:add&gt;` — a garbage string. The alternatives:
> - **Type error**: breaks rc's "everything concats" heritage.
> - **Composition**: redefining `^` to mean composition for functions overloads a string operation with a categorical operation. Dangerous.
> - **Coerce to string**: produces garbage. Honest but useless.
> 
> **`echo $f | cmd` — what bytes flow through the pipe?** The serialization problem. psh values cross pipe boundaries as text. A function cannot be meaningfully serialized as text and deserialized on the other side. You could send the function's name (a string), but then the receiver has a string, not a function. This is the fundamental incompatibility: pipes are the **cut rule** in the sequent calculus, connecting a producer's stdout to a consumer's stdin. Both sides are byte streams. Functions are not byte streams.
> 
> ### 4. Composition of function values
> 
> If functions are values, composition is the natural operation. The question is syntax.
> 
> **`$f | $g` as function composition** is deeply dangerous. `|` is the pipe operator — it means "connect stdout of left to stdin of right" at the process level. Overloading it to mean "compose these function values" creates an ambiguity: is `$f | $g` a pipeline of two commands (each of which happens to be a function name that gets looked up and executed), or is it function composition producing a new function value?
> 
> Current psh: `$f | $g` expands `$f` and `$g` as words, looks them up as commands, and pipes them. This is correct and must not change.
> 
> **The optic-informed approach:** In the profunctor optics framework, composition IS function composition — `lens1 . lens2` composes because optics in profunctor encoding are literally functions `p a b -&gt; p s t`. But this composition happens at the **optic level**, not the **value level**. The user composes accessors (`$result.ok.name` — Prism then Lens), not function objects.
> 
> If psh must have function composition syntax, it should be:
> - A dedicated operator, not `|`. Something like `$f ; $g` (sequencing) or an explicit `compose` builtin.
> - Or, more naturally: anonymous functions via pipelines. `fn { $f $1 | $g }` — a new function whose body pipes through both. This is already expressible without first-class functions.
> 
> ### 5. The &amp; (with) connective and Val::Thunk
> 
> The specification's four-connective table:
> 
> | Connective | Concrete | Polarity |
> |---|---|---|
> | ⊗ (tensor) | Tuple | Positive product |
> | ⊕ (plus) | Sum | Positive coproduct |
> | ⅋ (par) | Coprocess | Negative coproduct |
> | &amp; (with) | Handler/Offer | **Negative product** |
> 
> This is the linear logic classification. The key insight: **&amp; (with) is NOT the exponential.** It is the negative product — a structure that **offers** multiple alternatives and the consumer **chooses** one. The exponential (→) is a separate connective that requires &amp; but is not identical to it.
> 
> In concrete terms:
> - A handler that offers `{.get, .set, .delete}` is an &amp;-product: three operations, consumer picks.
> - A function `A -&gt; B` is `!A ⊸ B` in linear logic — it requires the exponential `!` (bang) to allow the argument to be used multiple times.
> 
> **Does Val::Thunk model &amp;?** Not exactly. A thunk (suspended computation, ↓N in the shift notation) is the shift from negative to positive — it reifies a computation as a value. The spec already has this: `` `{cmd}` `` is force-then-return (↓→↑), and `&lt;{cmd}` is thunk/future (↓N, computation → storable value).
> 
> The &amp; connective would be better modeled as a **record of operations** — what Be calls a `BPropertyInfo` (the static schema of a handler's copattern structure). This is not a function value; it is a **dispatch table**. In psh terms, a variable with multiple discipline functions (`fn x.get`, `fn x.set`) is already an &amp;-product: it offers `.get` and `.set`, and the accessor syntax selects which operation to invoke.
> 
> **So the &amp; connective is already present in psh** — it's the discipline function mechanism. Adding Val::Thunk or Val::Fn does not give you &amp; — it gives you the exponential, which is a different thing.
> 
> ### 6. Does adding functions to Val break existing optic laws?
> 
> **Lens laws for Tuple.** A Tuple can contain any Val element. If one element is a function:
> - **GetPut** (`view` then `update` with the same value gives back the original): Requires equality on the element. If `view` extracts a function and `update` puts it back, GetPut holds **if** we track identity (pointer equality or name equality). With psh's current `PartialEq` on Val, this breaks — two identical-by-construction function values would not be `==`.
> - **PutGet** (`update` then `view` returns the updated value): Holds regardless, since `view` extracts whatever was stored.
> - **PutPut** (two updates, only the last matters): Holds regardless.
> 
> So Lens laws partially break: GetPut requires equality, and functions lack a meaningful equality.
> 
> **Prism laws for Sum.** A Sum payload can be a function:
> - **MatchBuild** (`build` then `match` returns the focus): Holds — building `Sum("fn", Box::new(fn_val))` and matching recovers the payload.
> - **BuildMatch** (`match` then `build` roundtrips): Same issue — requires equality to verify the roundtrip.
> 
> **The `PartialEq` problem is the real obstacle.** Every optic law is stated in terms of equality. Functions are not equal (in general, function equality is undecidable). This doesn't mean the laws "don't hold" — it means they become **unverifiable**. The laws hold extensionally but cannot be checked intensionally. This is a standard issue in any language with first-class functions, but psh has been refreshingly free of it because Val is pure data.
> 
> ### Synthesis: Three Options
> 
> **Option A: Functions stay outside Val. (Recommended from optics perspective.)**
> 
> Functions remain global bindings in the `Env::functions` table. They are **morphisms**, not **objects**. The polarity boundary stays clean. Val stays positive, inert, `PartialEq`, serializable. The Grate/Glass branch of the optic hierarchy remains dormant — which is fine, because Grates are rarely needed for data access patterns.
> 
> What you lose: you cannot pass a function as an argument to another function, store functions in lists, or return functions from functions. But psh is a **shell**, not a functional programming language. The compositional unit is the **pipeline**, not the lambda. Higher-order behavior is achieved by passing command names as strings and `eval`-ing them (rc heritage), or by passing function names to builtins.
> 
> **Option B: Val::Fn as an opaque thunk — the ↓N shift made explicit.**
> 
> Add `Val::Fn(String, Vec&lt;Command&gt;)` where the String is the function name and the Vec&lt;Command&gt; is the body. This is the **shift-down** ↓N — a computation reified as a positive value. Key constraints:
> - `PartialEq` compares by name only (or is always false for anonymous functions)
> - Display prints `&lt;fn:name&gt;` — an opaque marker
> - Concat coerces to the display string
> - Match can only test "is this a function?" — a trivial tag Prism
> - Pipe serialization sends the name, not the closure
> - The function **captures no environment** — it resolves variables at call time (dynamic scope), consistent with rc's function model
> 
> This preserves most of Val's properties at the cost of a somewhat degenerate new variant. The Grate optic becomes reachable but not terribly useful.
> 
> **Option C: Full cartesian closure — Val::Closure with environment capture.**
> 
> Add `Val::Closure(CapturedEnv, Vec&lt;Command&gt;)`. This is the full exponential. It breaks:
> - `PartialEq` (closures are incomparable)
> - Serialization (captured environments don't cross pipe boundaries)
> - The polarity discipline (negative embedded in positive)
> - The BMessage lesson (computation mixed with data)
> 
> I cannot recommend this from the optics perspective. It gains the Grate optic at the cost of the entire MonadicLens/profunctor framework that the spec builds on.
> 
> ### My Recommendation
> 
> **Option A** is the strongest from the optics and type-theoretic perspective. The theoretical argument is clean: Val is the positive subcategory of the duploid, functions are in the negative subcategory, and the specification already identifies the shift operators that mediate between them. Adding functions to Val does not extend the optic hierarchy in a way that pays for the damage to Val's properties.
> 
> The practical desire for first-class functions is real, but it is better served by:
> 1. **Function names as values** (already possible: `let f = greet; $f arg`)
> 2. **Computed variables** (`let x = try { body }` — the ↓→↑ shift)
> 3. **Process substitution** (`&lt;{cmd}` — the ↓N shift, already in the spec)
> 4. **The &amp; connective through discipline functions** (already present)
> 
> These give the user higher-order behavior without crossing the polarity boundary inside Val. The exponential is present in the **language** (functions exist, they compose via pipelines, they can be referenced by name) — it just does not need to be present in the **value sort**.
> 
> That said, Option B is defensible if Lane's use cases require passing function references as arguments to other functions. The key question for Lane: **is there a concrete psh script pattern that requires a function as a value, that cannot be expressed by passing the function's name as a string?** If such a pattern exists, Option B is the minimal accommodation. If not, Option A preserves the cleaner design.</result>
