> </task-notification>

### `2026-04-07T04:10:24.498Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>ab2e841d169dee171</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_0152pPdAi6hrmxJXuJHPLzy7</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/25f44a96-9488-444d-b842-3df1553f5366/tasks/ab2e841d169dee171.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Session types: functions as values in CBPV" completed</summary>
> <result>I have everything I need. Let me now compose the analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Verdict: Sound. The CBPV encoding is correct. Concrete recommendations follow.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 1. The CBPV answer: U(C) thunks are the right encoding
> 
> Levy 2004, §2.1 defines two type formers that mediate between value types and computation types:
> 
> - **U(C)** (thunk): takes a computation type C and produces a value type. U(C) is positive — it's inert data in the value sort. It can be stored, duplicated, passed as an argument.
> - **F(A)** (return): takes a value type A and produces a computation type. F(A) is `return` in your spec — already present.
> 
> A first-class function in CBPV is a value of type `U(A → F(B))`. The function arrow `A → F(B)` is a computation type (negative — it consumes an argument and produces an effect-laden result). Wrapping it in `U(...)` thunks it into the value sort. `force` eliminates the thunk, recovering the computation.
> 
> psh already has the `F(A)` side: `return word` is `return : A → F(A)` (spec §The shift operators, also Levy 2004 §2.1 Definition 2.1). What's missing is `U(C)` — the ability to suspend a computation as a value.
> 
> The spec's existing shift table (specification.md lines 42-48) lists `&lt;{cmd}` process substitution as "Thunk/future (↓N)". That's an OS-level thunk (a running process reified as a file descriptor). `Val::Thunk` would be a language-level thunk — a suspended computation reified as a value. These are complementary, not conflicting.
> 
> **Typing rule for thunk introduction:**
> 
> ```
> Γ, x₁ : A₁, ..., xₙ : Aₙ ⊢ body : F(B)
> ──────────────────────────────────────────
> Γ ⊢ thunk(x₁...xₙ, body, Γ|fv) : U(A₁ → ... → Aₙ → F(B))
> ```
> 
> where `Γ|fv` is the restriction of Γ to free variables of body. The captured environment is part of the thunk's positive data — it's already evaluated (CBV).
> 
> **Typing rule for force (application):**
> 
> ```
> Γ ⊢ v : U(A → F(B))    Γ ⊢ a : A
> ──────────────────────────────────
> Γ ⊢ force(v, a) : F(B)
> ```
> 
> In psh terms: `$f arg1 arg2` where `$f` is a thunked function forces the thunk with the given arguments, producing a `Status` (which is `F(B)` at the shell level — a command-sort outcome).
> 
> ### 2. The polarity question: analysis is correct
> 
> Your analysis is precise. A thunk IS positive in CBPV — `U(C)` is a value type by definition (Levy 2004, §2.1). The thunk wraps a negative thing (a computation waiting for arguments) in a positive envelope (storable, cloneable data). This is exactly the decomposition that BMessage failed to make.
> 
> The BMessage polarity confusion (specification.md line 718) was embedding raw negative structure (reply_port) in positive data without the mediating shift. A `Val::Thunk` IS the mediating shift — it's `U(A → F(B))`, which takes the negative `A → F(B)` and wraps it through the `U` shift into the positive sort. The body is not evaluated until forced. The captured environment snapshot is fully positive (all values already evaluated at thunk creation time).
> 
> The critical distinction: `Val::Thunk` does not embed a live continuation or a channel endpoint. It embeds a *recipe* — parameter names, a body (AST), and a snapshot of bindings. All of these are pure positive data. The negativity (computation, side effects, waiting for arguments) only appears at force time, which is a polarity crossing that happens in the evaluator's cut-elimination engine (run_simple), not in the value sort.
> 
> ### 3. Closures and capture
> 
> **Capture-by-value preserves positivity.** If the environment snapshot is taken at thunk creation time and all captured values are `Val` (which is `Clone`, positive, inert), then the closure's environment is positive data. The thunk is `(params, body, env_snapshot)` — all three components are positive. `U(C)` is satisfied.
> 
> **Captured mutable variables: snapshot, not alias.** When a thunk captures `$x` where `x` is mutable, the thunk gets the value of `x` at creation time, not a reference to `x`'s storage location. This is the CBPV-correct behavior: `U(C)` thunks a computation by recording its dependencies as values. If the thunk captured a mutable binding, it would embed a negative reference (a read/write endpoint) in the value sort — exactly the BMessage mistake.
> 
> This means:
> 
> ```sh
> let mut x = 1
> let f = fn(n) { echo $x $n }
> x = 2
> $f 3     # prints "1 3", not "2 3"
> ```
> 
> Capture-by-value is CBPV's requirement, not a limitation. The alternative (capture-by-reference) would require `Val::Thunk` to carry a mutable borrow into the environment, breaking both Clone and positivity. es needed a GC precisely because it chose capture-by-reference — the closure held a live pointer into the environment, creating a shared mutable reference that only a GC could manage safely.
> 
> **What about captured mutable variables the user expects to see change?** This is a design choice, not a theoretical necessity. Two options:
> 
> 1. **Snapshot (recommended).** Capture `Val` values at thunk creation. Simple, correct, Clone-compatible. The user who wants the latest value re-reads it inside the function body via `$x` (which resolves against the calling scope, not the captured scope — see below).
> 2. **Name capture.** Capture variable *names*, not values. At force time, resolve names against the calling scope chain. This is what rc/psh functions already do — `fn greet { echo $x }` resolves `$x` at call time, not definition time. A thunk with name capture is closer to rc's `fn` than to a closure.
> 
> Option 2 is actually what the existing `Binding::Fn` already does. The body is stored as AST; variable references resolve dynamically at call time. A `Val::Thunk` with name capture would be the same mechanism, just storable as a value.
> 
> This creates a clean two-tier model:
> - **Thunk without closure** (name capture): `fn(args) { body }` — resolves free variables dynamically. This is `fn` lifted into the value sort. Positivity holds because the AST is data and names are strings.
> - **Thunk with closure** (value capture): `fn(args) [x, y] { body }` — snapshots `x`, `y` at creation. An explicit capture list. Free variables not in the list resolve dynamically.
> 
> I recommend starting with name capture only (tier 1), which requires no changes to Env and no Clone on Env. This is what shells do. Explicit closures can be added later if the use case demands it.
> 
> ### 4. The concrete Rust type
> 
> **Recommendation: start with the simple form.**
> 
> ```rust
> /// A thunked computation storable as a value.
> /// CBPV's U(A₁ → ... → Aₙ → F(B)): a suspended function
> /// in the value sort.
> ///
> /// Levy 2004 §2.1: U(C) is a value type that wraps a
> /// computation type C. force eliminates the thunk.
> ///
> /// Parameter names + body only (no closure capture).
> /// Free variables resolve dynamically at force time,
> /// consistent with rc/ksh function semantics.
> #[derive(Debug, Clone, PartialEq)]
> pub struct Thunk {
>     /// Parameter names — positional binding at force time.
>     pub params: Vec&lt;String&gt;,
>     /// The suspended computation body.
>     pub body: Vec&lt;Command&gt;,
> }
> ```
> 
> And the Val variant:
> 
> ```rust
> pub enum Val {
>     // ... existing variants ...
>     /// First-class function — CBPV's U(A → F(B)).
>     /// A suspended computation storable as a value.
>     /// Positive (inert, Clone) — negativity appears only at force.
>     Thunk(Thunk),
> }
> ```
> 
> **Why not capture Env?** Three reasons:
> 
> 1. Env is not Clone (it holds `HashMap&lt;String, Vec&lt;Command&gt;&gt;` for functions, plus the scope stack). Making it Clone is substantial work with no payoff for the common case.
> 2. Dynamic name resolution is the shell convention and what users expect. `fn greet { echo $greeting }` already resolves `$greeting` at call time. A thunk should behave the same way.
> 3. Closure capture can be added later as `ThunkWithCapture { params, body, captures: Vec&lt;(String, Val)&gt; }` if needed. The `Val::Thunk` variant stays the same; the inner struct gains a captures field.
> 
> **Clone is satisfied.** `Vec&lt;String&gt;` and `Vec&lt;Command&gt;` are both Clone (Command derives Clone). The `Thunk` struct derives Clone. Val::Thunk(Thunk) is Clone.
> 
> **PartialEq.** Two thunks with the same params and body AST are equal. This is structural equality on the code, not behavioral equality (which is undecidable). Acceptable for a shell.
> 
> ### 5. Interaction with the rest of the type system
> 
> **Type annotation for functions:**
> 
> ```
> type_ann = ... | fn_type
> fn_type  = 'Fn' '(' type_list? ')' 
> ```
> 
> Spelled `Fn()`, `Fn(Int)`, `Fn(Int, Str)`. No return type annotation — shell functions return Status, which is outside the value type system. If you later add `return` with typed values, extend to `Fn(Int) -&gt; Int`.
> 
> Add to `TypeAnnotation`:
> 
> ```rust
> /// Function type — validates Val::Thunk with matching arity.
> /// No return type yet (shell functions return Status).
> Fn(Vec&lt;TypeAnnotation&gt;),
> ```
> 
> Validation: `Val::Thunk(t)` matches `Fn(args)` iff `t.params.len() == args.len()`. Argument types are unchecked at thunk creation (CBV only evaluates at force time). This is a weak Prism — it checks arity but not argument types. Argument type checking at force time is the CBPV-correct place (the computation hasn't run yet; you can't check what it will produce).
> 
> **Composition with existing constructs:**
> 
> | Construct | Interaction | Notes |
> |-----------|------------|-------|
> | `match` | Pattern match on thunks: `case fn $f { ... }` | Structural pattern extracts the thunk into a binding |
> | `try` | `try { $f arg }` works — forcing a thunk is a command | `try` wraps the outcome as Result[T] |
> | Accessors | No meaningful accessors on thunks | `.params` could return the parameter list as List, but this feels like reflection |
> | Pipes | `$f arg \| grep pattern` works — thunk force produces stdout | Thunk force is a command; commands compose in pipes |
> | Sum | `ok $handler` where `$handler` is a thunk — yes | Sum payload is Val; Thunk is Val. Clean composition. |
> | Concat | `$f^suffix` — coerces thunk to string via Display | See Display below |
> 
> **Display for thunks:**
> 
> ```rust
> Val::Thunk(t) =&gt; {
>     write!(f, "fn(")?;
>     for (i, p) in t.params.iter().enumerate() {
>         if i &gt; 0 { write!(f, " ")?; }
>         write!(f, "{p}")?;
>     }
>     write!(f, "){{...}}")
> }
> ```
> 
> `echo $f` prints `fn(x y){...}`. This is diagnostic — you rarely print a function. The representation is not round-trippable (the body is elided), which is fine. es printed `@ x y {body}` with full body — round-trippable but noisy. The `{...}` elision is the pragmatic choice; `whatis $f` can show the full definition.
> 
> ### 6. RunOutcome interaction
> 
> I don't see `RunOutcome` in the current codebase — it looks like a planned type. Given the description `RunOutcome { Status(Status), Value(Val) }`:
> 
> A thunked function that is forced (called) returns `Status` through the normal command execution path (`run_cmds(&amp;body)` → `Status`). This is the `RunOutcome::Status(Status)` arm. The thunk produces a command-level outcome, not a value-level result.
> 
> If the thunk is used in value position (e.g., `let x = $f arg` — calling a function to capture its output), the force goes through `capture_subprocess` which is the `↓→↑` shift: fork, run, capture stdout, return as Val. This is `RunOutcome::Value(Val)`.
> 
> The composition is clean because thunk forcing happens at the same level as any other command execution — the thunk's body is `Vec&lt;Command&gt;`, and running it goes through `run_cmds`, which returns `Status`. The `RunOutcome` distinction (Status vs Value) is orthogonal to whether the command came from a thunk or from any other source.
> 
> The one design question: should a thunk be forceable in value position *without* a fork? I.e., should `let x = $f arg` run the function body in-process and capture `return val`'s value? This would be the `${ cmd }` shared-state command substitution that PLAN.md defers. For now, value-position forcing goes through fork+capture, same as `` `{$f arg}` ``.
> 
> ### 7. Lambda syntax
> 
> Constraints:
> - `@{ }` is taken (subshell)
> - `@` is a word character in the two-alphabet split
> - `fn` is taken (function definition binding)
> - Must read naturally in shell context
> - Must compose with `let f = ...`
> 
> **Proposal: `fn(params) { body }`**
> 
> ```sh
> let greet = fn(name) { echo hello $name }
> let add = fn(a b) { return `{ expr $a + $b } }
> let handler = fn() { echo handled }
> ```
> 
> Forcing:
> ```sh
> $greet world        # prints "hello world"
> $add 2 3            # returns "5" via RunOutcome
> ```
> 
> **Why `fn(params) { body }` works:**
> 
> 1. **No conflict with `fn name { body }`** (the existing binding form). The parser distinguishes: `fn` followed by `(` is a thunk literal; `fn` followed by a bare name is a function definition. This is LL(1) — one token of lookahead.
> 2. **Consistent with the existing `fn` keyword** — functions are `fn`. Named functions are `fn name { ... }`, anonymous functions are `fn(params) { ... }`. The parenthesized parameter list is the syntactic marker for "this is a value, not a binding."
> 3. **Reads naturally.** `let double = fn(x) { expr $x '*' 2 }` is self-evident.
> 4. **No new keywords or sigils.** Reuses `fn`, `(`, `)`, `{`, `}`.
> 
> The `=&gt;` single-line body introducer composes:
> ```sh
> let inc = fn(x) =&gt; expr $x + 1
> ```
> 
> **Grammar production:**
> 
> ```
> thunk_literal = 'fn' '(' param_list? ')' body
> param_list    = NAME (NAME)*
> body          = '{' command* '}' | '=&gt;' command
> ```
> 
> This adds one case to the `value` or `word` production — a `fn(...)` thunk literal is a word (it evaluates to `Val::Thunk`). In the AST:
> 
> ```rust
> pub enum Word {
>     // ... existing variants ...
>     /// Thunk literal: fn(params) { body }
>     /// CBPV's U(A → F(B)) — suspends a computation as a value.
>     ThunkLiteral {
>         params: Vec&lt;String&gt;,
>         body: Vec&lt;Command&gt;,
>     },
> }
> ```
> 
> **Why not `\(params) { body }` or `|params| { body }`?**
> 
> - `\` is the line continuation character in the spec. Using it as lambda introduces ambiguity at line boundaries.
> - `|params|` conflicts with pipeline syntax — `|` is the pipe operator. Rust gets away with it because Rust's parser has enough context to disambiguate; a shell parser in pipeline context does not.
> 
> **Why not es's `@ params { body }`?**
> 
> - `@` is used for `@{ }` subshell. `@ x { echo $x }` could be parsed as `@` followed by the command `x`, then a block — ambiguous. es didn't have `@{ }` so it had no conflict. psh does.
> 
> ### Summary of concrete types
> 
> ```rust
> // value.rs — new struct
> #[derive(Debug, Clone, PartialEq)]
> pub struct Thunk {
>     pub params: Vec&lt;String&gt;,
>     pub body: Vec&lt;Command&gt;,
> }
> 
> // value.rs — new Val variant
> pub enum Val {
>     // ... existing 9 variants ...
>     Thunk(Thunk),
> }
> 
> // ast.rs — new Word variant
> pub enum Word {
>     // ... existing variants ...
>     ThunkLiteral {
>         params: Vec&lt;String&gt;,
>         body: Vec&lt;Command&gt;,
>     },
> }
> 
> // ast.rs — new TypeAnnotation variant
> pub enum TypeAnnotation {
>     // ... existing variants ...
>     Fn(Vec&lt;TypeAnnotation&gt;),
> }
> ```
> 
> Evaluator changes:
> 
> ```rust
> // In eval_word:
> Word::ThunkLiteral { params, body } =&gt; {
>     Val::Thunk(Thunk {
>         params: params.clone(),
>         body: body.clone(),
>     })
> }
> 
> // In run_simple, after builtin check, before external command:
> // If name_str resolves to a Val::Thunk via variable lookup:
> if let Val::Thunk(thunk) = self.env.get_value(&amp;name_str) {
>     // ... but this conflicts with the current dispatch model
>     // where get_fn returns Option&lt;&amp;Vec&lt;Command&gt;&gt;.
> }
> ```
> 
> Actually, the dispatch path needs thought. Currently `run_simple` checks: (1) builtin override, (2) function table via `get_fn`, (3) builtins, (4) external command. A thunk stored in a variable is accessed differently — through `$f arg1 arg2`, where `$f` evaluates to `Val::Thunk` and then the thunk is forced with `arg1 arg2`.
> 
> The key: when the command name is a variable reference that evaluates to `Val::Thunk`, the evaluator should force the thunk instead of trying to exec it as an external command. The existing dispatch in `run_simple` resolves the command name to a string (`name_str`). For thunks, the resolution step should produce the Val, not just its string representation. This requires a minor refactor of `run_simple`'s name resolution to handle `Val::Thunk` as a distinct case before falling through to string-based dispatch.
> 
> ```rust
> // In run_simple, replace:
> //   let name_str = raw_name.map(...).unwrap_or_else(|| self.eval_word(...).as_str()...)
> // with:
> let name_val = match &amp;cmd.name {
>     Word::Literal(s) =&gt; None,  // literals go through string dispatch
>     Word::Var(name) =&gt; {
>         let v = self.env.get_value(name);
>         match v {
>             Some(Val::Thunk(_)) =&gt; Some(v.unwrap().clone()),
>             _ =&gt; None,
>         }
>     }
>     other =&gt; {
>         let v = self.eval_word(other);
>         match &amp;v {
>             Val::Thunk(_) =&gt; Some(v),
>             _ =&gt; None,
>         }
>     }
> };
> 
> if let Some(Val::Thunk(thunk)) = name_val {
>     // Force the thunk: push scope, bind params, run body
>     self.env.push_scope();
>     for (i, (param, arg)) in thunk.params.iter().zip(args.iter()).enumerate() {
>         let _ = self.env.set_value(param, Val::scalar(arg.clone()));
>         let _ = self.env.set_value(&amp;(i + 1).to_string(), Val::scalar(arg.clone()));
>     }
>     let _ = self.env.set_value("*", Val::list(args.iter().map(|s| s.as_str())));
>     let status = self.run_cmds(&amp;thunk.body);
>     self.env.pop_scope();
>     let _ = self.env.set_value("status", Val::scalar(&amp;status.0));
>     return status;
> }
> ```
> 
> ### Polarity summary
> 
> | Construct | Polarity | CBPV type | Sort |
> |-----------|----------|-----------|------|
> | `fn(x) { body }` (literal) | Positive | U(A → F(B)) | Value (Word) |
> | `Val::Thunk(t)` (stored) | Positive | U(A → F(B)) | Value (Val) |
> | `$f arg` (force) | Cross-polarity | force : U(C) → C | Cut (Command) |
> | `fn name { body }` (binding) | Negative | A → F(B) | Binding |
> 
> The thunk literal creates a positive value. Storing it in a variable keeps it positive. Forcing it (calling `$f arg`) is a polarity crossing — the evaluator's cut-elimination engine (`run_simple`) eliminates the thunk by running its body. This is the same ↓ shift that all command execution performs. The CBPV structure is preserved throughout.
> 
> ### What this design does NOT give you
> 
> 1. **No typed return values from thunks.** The thunk returns `Status`, not `Val`. Getting a `Val` out requires the `↓→↑` shift (command substitution or `try`). This is correct — the function body is a computation, and computations produce effects + status, not values. Values exit computations through `return` (the `F(A)` injector) once that's implemented.
> 
> 2. **No partial application / currying.** `$f 1` with a two-parameter thunk would bind `1` to the first param and leave the second unbound (error, or worse, bound to empty). Currying requires returning a new thunk from a thunk, which works (`return fn(y) { ... }` once `return` handles values) but isn't first-class syntax.
> 
> 3. **No higher-kinded thunks.** A thunk that takes a thunk as argument works (`fn(f x) { $f $x }`) but the type system can't express `Fn(Fn(Int)) -&gt; Int`. The type annotation `Fn(Fn(Int))` would require recursive TypeAnnotation, which it already supports structurally.</result>

> </task-notification>

### `2026-04-07T04:36:24.426Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a996936fe2be1144d</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01R3fGyVL2fRUozv8B8CLmzQ</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/25f44a96-9488-444d-b842-3df1553f5366/tasks/a996936fe2be1144d.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Optics: lambda sort boundary analysis" completed</summary>
> <result>Now I have everything I need. Let me formulate the response.
> 
> ---
> 
> Here is the optics analysis of the `fn` vs `\` syntax split.
> 
> ## 1. Does the syntax split change any optic property?
> 
> No. The optic structure is determined entirely by what the syntax **produces**, not by the syntax used to produce it. Both forms yield `Val::Thunk` — the same runtime value, the same CBPV type U(A → F(B)), the same optic classification.
> 
> From the spec (`docs/specification.md`, lines 499-508):
> 
> &gt; Thunk is CBPV's U(A → F(B)) — a computation thunked into the value sort. [...] Thunk is an optic leaf — atomic from the accessor perspective, like ExitCode. PartialEq on Thunk is structural (same params + same AST = equal), which preserves the Lens laws in any Tuple or List containing a Thunk.
> 
> The optic hierarchy cares about:
> - **What Val variant is produced** (Thunk → leaf)
> - **Whether it supports PartialEq** (yes, structural — same params + same AST)
> - **How it composes in containers** (a Tuple containing a Thunk is Lens-accessible at that position; the Thunk itself is opaque)
> 
> None of these depend on the surface syntax that constructed the Thunk. `fn(x) { expr $x + 1 }` and `\x =&gt; expr $x + 1` produce the same `Val::Thunk { params: ["x"], body: &lt;AST&gt; }`. The optic classification is a property of `Val::Thunk`, not of the grammar production that built it.
> 
> This is purely a syntactic/parsing concern with no optic consequence.
> 
> ## 2. Does excluding `fn` from value position help the optic story?
> 
> Yes. Your intuition is correct, and the reason is precise.
> 
> The current spec already makes this distinction (`docs/syntax.md`, lines 679-683):
> 
> &gt; `fn` followed by `(` is a thunk literal; `fn` followed by a bare name is a function definition (`fn name { body }`). The parser disambiguates by one token of lookahead.
> 
> The proposed `\` syntax sharpens this: `fn name { body }` is a binding (μ̃-binder, extends Γ), and `\params =&gt; body` is a value (producer, yields Val::Thunk). The split maps exactly onto the four-sort AST:
> 
> | Form | Sort | Role |
> |---|---|---|
> | `fn name { body }` | Binding | μ̃-binder — extends context Γ with a named computation |
> | `\params =&gt; body` | Word/Value | Term (producer) — evaluates to Val::Thunk |
> 
> Why this helps the optic story: **Optics operate on values, not on bindings.** A Lens into a Tuple, a Prism into a Sum, an AffineTraversal composing both — all of these project or inject **values**. If `fn` definitions could appear inside value constructors, you'd get a Binding embedded in a value position, which is a sort violation. You can't project a Lens into something that extends Γ rather than producing a value.
> 
> The constraint "fn bindings never contaminate the value sort" means:
> - Every element of a Tuple is a value (Lens-accessible)
> - Every payload of a Sum is a value (Prism-accessible)
> - Thunks inside containers are always value-sort Thunks (constructed via `\` or `fn(params)` literal), never binding-sort definitions
> 
> This is the sort discipline doing its job. The optic hierarchy assumes it operates over values. The `fn`/`\` split makes that assumption syntactically enforced rather than relying on context-sensitive parsing rules.
> 
> ## 3. Is `match $x { ok $v =&gt; \y =&gt; echo $v $y }` ambiguous?
> 
> No. The parse is unambiguous, and your analysis is correct.
> 
> The grammar for match arms (from `docs/syntax.md`) is:
> 
> ```
> match_arm = pattern '=&gt;' command
> ```
> 
> After the arm's `=&gt;`, the parser expects a single command. `\y =&gt; echo $v $y` is a word-level expression (a lambda literal that evaluates to `Val::Thunk`). In command position, a word that isn't a keyword is the start of an `expr_cmd` (simple command). The lambda literal `\y =&gt; echo $v $y` is parsed as a single word token — the `\` sigil triggers lambda-literal parsing, which greedily consumes `y` as the parameter, then `=&gt;` as the lambda body introducer, then `echo $v $y` as the body.
> 
> The key structural fact: the arm's `=&gt;` and the lambda's `=&gt;` are disambiguated by what precedes them. The arm's `=&gt;` follows a pattern. The lambda's `=&gt;` follows `\params`. The parser never sees two `=&gt;` tokens in the same production — it sees `pattern =&gt; command`, and within that command, the lambda `\y =&gt; echo $v $y` is a self-contained word-level parse.
> 
> One thing to nail down in the grammar: the lambda's `=&gt;` body — does it extend to end-of-command (the next `;` or `}` or newline), or is it a single simple command? If it's greedy to end-of-command, the parse is clean. If the body is only a single word, you'd need `\y =&gt; { echo $v $y }` for multi-word bodies. The `=&gt;` single-line body introducer elsewhere in the spec (line 143 of PLAN.md: "uniform across all control flow") suggests greedy-to-terminator, which makes this parse work without braces.
> 
> ## 4. Does the syntax split affect thunk composition with other optics?
> 
> Confirmed: it does not. The composition chain you describe:
> 
> ```
> $result.ok    — Prism: Sum → payload (may fail, returns Maybe)
> $extracted_thunk args  — Force: U(C) → C (polarity shift, value → computation)
> ```
> 
> This is a two-step operation: (1) optic access (Prism preview), (2) CBPV force. Step (1) operates on the Val. Step (2) operates on the result of step (1). Neither step cares how the Thunk was constructed — only that it IS a `Val::Thunk`.
> 
> The syntax split affects **construction** (how Thunks enter the value world). The optic hierarchy affects **access** (how values are projected, injected, traversed). These are dual concerns:
> 
> - Construction: `\x =&gt; body` produces Val::Thunk (value sort, positive)
> - Access: `$container.field` projects via Lens/Prism/AffineTraversal (optic composition)
> - Forcing: `$thunk args` eliminates the U wrapper (CBPV force, polarity shift)
> 
> The three operations compose sequentially but don't interact structurally. The optic hierarchy is closed under Thunk-as-leaf — it gains no new optic from Thunk (the spec notes Grate exists but is inert for read-only accessors). The syntax split doesn't change this closure property.
> 
> ## Summary
> 
> The `fn` vs `\` split has **no optic-level consequences**. It is a syntactic concern that happens to align well with the sort discipline the optic hierarchy already assumes. Specifically:
> 
> - Same `Val::Thunk` at runtime → same optic classification (leaf)
> - `fn` excluded from value position → sort discipline enforced syntactically, which is good hygiene for the optic story but doesn't change the optic story itself
> - `=&gt;` is unambiguous in nested position (different parse contexts)
> - Thunk composition with Prism/Lens is unaffected — optics care about access patterns, not construction syntax</result>

> <summary>Agent "Plan 9: lambda vs fn syntax split" completed</summary>
> <result>This confirms it. `\` is **not** in `word_char` and **not** in `var_char`. In the current grammar, `\` only has meaning as line continuation (`\` + `\n`). A bare `\` followed by anything other than `\n` would be a lex error in the target grammar. The rc heritage of "backslash is not otherwise special" means it doesn't have escape semantics — it does NOT mean it's a normal word character.
> 
> Now I have everything. Here is my full analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Plan 9 Perspective: `\` for Lambda Syntax in psh
> 
> ### 1. Does `\` earn its production?
> 
> rc had one function form because rc had one sort. Functions were named, lived in the function table, and were invoked by name. There was no value position to put a computation into — rc's values were string lists, full stop. If you wanted to pass a function, you passed its name as a string.
> 
> psh has two sorts and `Val::Thunk`. The question is not whether lambdas should exist but what their surface syntax is. The current spec uses `fn(params) body`, disambiguated by one-token lookahead: `fn` + NAME = definition, `fn` + `(` = literal. This works but overloads a single keyword across both sorts.
> 
> Plan 9's design principle: **distinct operations get distinct syntax.** `bind` and `mount` both modify the mount table, but they are separate commands because they do different things (local rearrangement vs. attaching a 9P connection). `fn name { body }` extending Gamma is a different operation from `\x =&gt; expr` producing a Val. Separate syntax makes the polarity boundary visible without context-dependent disambiguation.
> 
> The `\` syntax pays off specifically for **inline thunk arguments**: `with_retry 3 \() =&gt; curl -f $url`. This is where `fn(params) body` gets positionally ambiguous — a reader must track whether they're in value position or command position to know what `fn` means. If thunks only appeared in `let` RHS, the existing `fn(params) body` would suffice (the `let` already signals value position). But the spec's examples show thunks passed as arguments, and that's the case where the overload hurts.
> 
> **Verdict:** Earns its keep. The inline-argument case is real, and the sort distinction is worth making visible.
> 
> ### 2. Line continuation ambiguity — the critical question
> 
> Current rule (syntax.md line 570, parse.rs line 157): `\` + `\n` is whitespace. "Backslash is not otherwise a special character."
> 
> Under Lane's proposal, `\` dispatches by the next character:
> - `\n` → line continuation
> - `(` → zero-param lambda `\()`
> - `var_char` → lambda with parameter(s) `\x =&gt; ...`
> - anything else → error
> 
> **This is LL(1).** The sets `{'\n'}`, `{'('}`, and `var_char = [a-zA-Z0-9_*]` are pairwise disjoint. One character of lookahead after `\` resolves the parse unambiguously.
> 
> **The rc heritage concern dissolves on inspection.** `\` is not in `word_char` (confirmed: `[a-zA-Z0-9_\-./+:,%*?\[\]@]` does not contain backslash). It is not in `var_char` either. In the target grammar, a bare `\` followed by anything other than `\n` is already a lex error — it cannot appear as part of a bare word. So `\` is currently a reserved character with exactly one use. Lane's proposal gives it a second use, cleanly disambiguated by one character of lookahead. There is no backward compatibility break because there is nothing to break.
> 
> The "backslash is not otherwise special" line in the spec means it lacks escape semantics (`\n` is not newline, `\t` is not tab). It does NOT mean `\` is a valid word character. Those are different claims.
> 
> **One practical note.** In interactive use, a user who types `\` at end of line and forgets the newline immediately following will get lambda-parse behavior instead of continuation. This is unlikely to cause real confusion because `\` + space + `\n` is already invalid (the space intervenes), and `\` + `\n` works identically regardless. The only new case is `\` + `var_char`, which was previously a lex error and now means something.
> 
> **Verdict:** Clean. No ambiguity. No heritage break.
> 
> ### 3. Making the sort boundary visible
> 
> The duploid has two sorts: command (negative, computation) and value (positive, data). The question is whether the syntax should expose this or hide it.
> 
> Plan 9's answer was always to **make the mechanism visible.** `/proc/N/ctl` doesn't pretend to be a regular file — you write specific control messages to it. `bind` and `mount` don't hide behind a unified "attach" verb. The system tells you what kind of operation you're performing.
> 
> `fn` in command position and `\` in value position is the same principle. A reader sees `fn greet { ... }` and knows: this extends Gamma, the name is now bound. A reader sees `\x =&gt; expr $x + 1` and knows: this produces a value, it's going somewhere (RHS of `let`, argument to a command). The sort boundary is in the syntax, not inferred from position.
> 
> The alternative — `fn` for both, disambiguated by lookahead — hides the boundary. It requires the reader to do the same positional analysis the parser does. Plan 9 taught me that making the user do the parser's job is a design smell.
> 
> **Verdict:** Good. Making the polarity boundary visible is consistent with the design philosophy of both Plan 9 and this spec's theoretical foundations.
> 
> ### 4. Zero-param lambdas: `\() =&gt; body`
> 
> This is the one case that needs care. A zero-param thunk is a suspended computation with no arguments — CBPV's `U(F(B))`, thunk wrapping a bare computation.
> 
> `\() =&gt; body` works syntactically. `\` + `(` is not ambiguous with anything — `(` is not `\n` and not `var_char`. The `()` is the empty parameter list. The `=&gt;` introduces the body.
> 
> The concern: `()` in psh's value grammar means the empty list `()` or a parenthesized word list. But here `()` follows `\`, not a command or value production. The parser is in lambda-parameter-list mode, not value mode. No conflict.
> 
> The question is whether `\() =&gt; body` looks natural. Compare:
> - `with_retry 3 \() =&gt; curl -f $url` — clear: zero-arg thunk passed to `with_retry`
> - `let thunk = \() =&gt; date` — clear: stored suspended computation
> - `\() =&gt; { long; multi; line; body }` — fine with braced body
> 
> The only slight oddness is that `\() =&gt; body` reads as "lambda of empty tuple" to someone coming from Haskell, but in psh `()` is the parameter list, not a pattern. This is fine — psh is not Haskell.
> 
> **Verdict:** Clean. `\() =&gt;` is unambiguous, readable, and consistent with the multi-param form.
> 
> ### 5. Grammar cost comparison
> 
> **Current spec: `fn(params) body` for thunk literals.**
> 
> Productions added to the grammar:
> 
> ```
> thunk_literal = 'fn' '(' NAME* ')' body
> ```
> 
> This is one production, but it requires the parser to disambiguate `fn` + NAME (fn_def) from `fn` + `(` (thunk_literal) at the `fn` dispatch point. The `fn` keyword participates in two unrelated productions.
> 
> **Proposed: `\params =&gt; body` for lambda.**
> 
> Productions:
> 
> ```
> lambda      = '\' lambda_params '=&gt;' (command | body)
> lambda_params = '(' ')' | NAME+
> ```
> 
> That's two productions (or one with an alternative for the parameter list). But `\` is a dedicated sigil — it does NOT overlap with `fn`'s productions. The `fn` dispatch simplifies:
> 
> ```
> fn_def = 'fn' NAME body       # always a named definition, no ambiguity
> ```
> 
> Net grammar change:
> - **Remove:** `thunk_literal = 'fn' '(' NAME* ')' body` from the `value` production
> - **Remove:** `fn` dispatch ambiguity (no more checking for `(` after `fn`)
> - **Add:** `lambda` production, dispatched from `\` in value position
> - **Add:** `lambda_params` production
> 
> Production count is roughly the same. But the **disambiguation complexity decreases.** The `fn` keyword now has exactly one production. The `\` sigil has exactly one production (plus the existing line-continuation rule, which is in the lexer, not the parser). Each sigil dispatches to exactly one thing. This is simpler to implement, simpler to document, and simpler for the user to hold in their head.
> 
> **One note on `=&gt;` in the lambda.** The spec already uses `=&gt;` as a single-line body introducer. In the lambda, `=&gt;` separates parameters from body. These are compatible — in `\x =&gt; expr $x + 1`, the `=&gt;` introduces the lambda body, which is a single command. The parser reads `\`, then parameters, then `=&gt;`, then either `{` (braced body) or a single command. This is consistent with how `=&gt;` works everywhere else in the grammar.
> 
> For multi-line lambda bodies, the user writes `\x =&gt; { ... }` or `\() =&gt; { ... }`. The `{` triggers multi-line body parsing. Consistent with `fn name { ... }`, `if cond { ... }`, etc.
> 
> ### Summary table
> 
> | Question | Assessment | Confidence |
> |----------|-----------|------------|
> | Does `\` earn its production? | Yes, for inline thunk arguments | High |
> | Is `\` vs `\n` ambiguous? | No, LL(1), `\` was already reserved | High — verified against both spec and implementation |
> | Sort boundary in syntax? | Good, consistent with Plan 9 design philosophy | High |
> | `\() =&gt; body` for zero-param? | Clean, no conflicts | High |
> | Grammar cost? | Roughly neutral on production count, lower disambiguation complexity | High |
> 
> ### One recommendation
> 
> If Lane adopts `\`, the spec line "Backslash is not otherwise a special character" (syntax.md:572) needs to be rewritten. It should say something like:
> 
> &gt; Backslash has two roles: immediately before a newline, it is line continuation (the pair is consumed as whitespace). Before a parameter list or variable name, it introduces a lambda expression (see §Thunks). It has no escape semantics — `\n` in a lambda is a parameter named `n`, not a newline character. Backslash cannot appear in bare words.
> 
> This makes all three uses of `\` explicit and documents the LL(1) dispatch.
> 
> ### What I'd push back on
> 
> Nothing major. The `\` syntax is sound. The one thing I'd watch is whether the `=&gt;` after lambda params creates any parse ambiguity with `=&gt;` as a body introducer elsewhere. It shouldn't — `=&gt;` only appears after specific keywords (`if`, `for`, `while`, `match` patterns, `fn`) and now after `\params`. The parser knows which context it's in. But when you implement this in `combine`, write a test for `if \x =&gt; expr $x + 1 { ... }` — a lambda as an `if` condition. That's the kind of nesting where `=&gt;` could create confusion for the parser if not carefully scoped.
> 
> Actually, on reflection, that test is important. `if \x =&gt; expr $x + 1 { ... }` — the `=&gt;` belongs to the lambda, not to the `if`. The parser sees `if`, then tries to parse a pipeline as the condition. The pipeline parser encounters `\x`, enters lambda parsing, reads `=&gt; expr $x + 1`, and... where does the lambda end? A lambda with `=&gt;` (single-line body) reads one command. `expr $x + 1` is one command. The lambda closes. Then `{ ... }` is the `if` body. This works, but it's subtle. Document it and test it.</result>

> <summary>Agent "Be: fn vs lambda sort distinction" completed</summary>
> <result>Good. I have enough context now. Let me compose the response.
> 
> ---
> 
> Here's my roundtable contribution on the `fn` / `\` split.
> 
> ## The Be Analogy: Where It Holds, Where It Doesn't
> 
> Lane's right that Be had a similar structural split. Let me be precise about it, because the analogy is instructive but also misleading if you push it too far.
> 
> **BHandler** was negative — defined by its copattern (what it does when messages arrive). It had to be *registered* with a BLooper via `AddHandler()` before it could receive anything. A free-floating BHandler was inert. You could construct one outside a looper context, but it was useless until registered — `Looper()` returned NULL, `LockLooper()` would fail, message delivery would silently drop. The registration was the activation.
> 
> **BMessage** was positive — data, copyable, flat, independent of any execution context.
> 
> **BInvoker** was the bridge — it carried a BMessage template *and* a BMessenger target. It was the "ready-to-fire" package: data + destination. Controls like BButton inherited from BInvoker so they could send a message to a target when activated.
> 
> Now, `fn name { body }` in psh maps well to BHandler registration. It's a named computation being installed in a dispatch table (the shell's function table). It has identity (a name), it's addressable (you call it by name), and its existence is a side effect on the environment. This is a mu-tilde binder — it extends Gamma.
> 
> The `\` lambda is... different from anything Be had. BInvoker is the closest, but BInvoker was never *anonymous*. You always attached it to something with a name (a BButton, a BMenuItem). Be didn't have anonymous computation-as-value. The closest thing was passing a `thread_func` to `spawn_thread()` — a C function pointer, not a first-class value in the API's type system.
> 
> So the analogy holds for `fn` (named registration = BHandler + AddHandler) but `\` is genuinely novel ground relative to Be. That's fine. The question is whether the *split* is right.
> 
> ## The Case For the Split: One Pattern Per Concept
> 
> Be's API philosophy — the one I'm proudest of — was that distinct concepts should have distinct surface forms. BMessage and BHandler were never the same class. They weren't "mode-switched" versions of each other. You never looked at a BMessage and wondered "is this a handler?" The type told you.
> 
> The current spec (`docs/syntax.md` lines 363, 679-684) has `fn` doing double duty:
> - `fn name { body }` — named definition (Binding)
> - `fn(params) body` — thunk literal (Value)
> 
> Disambiguated by one token of lookahead: `(` means thunk, NAME means definition. It works *mechanically*, but it violates the principle. You're looking at `fn` and asking "which sort am I in?" The parser can tell. Can the human?
> 
> Lane's proposal gives each concept its own sigil. `fn` is always command-sort, always a binding, always extends Gamma. `\` is always value-sort, always produces a Val::Thunk, always positive. You never have to ask. The sort boundary is visible at the lexical level.
> 
> From the Be API design perspective, this is cleaner. We would have done it this way.
> 
> ## The `\` Sigil
> 
> Backslash-lambda is Haskell's convention. It's visually distinctive in shell context — nothing else in rc/ksh heritage uses `\` as a keyword prefix. That distinctiveness is actually the *point*. When you see `\` in psh code, you immediately know: "this is a value-level computation, not a command." It's a sort marker.
> 
> The concern about unfamiliarity is real but manageable. Shell users won't know `\` from Haskell. But they also don't know `fn(params)` as thunk-literal syntax — that's equally novel. Between the two, `\` has the advantage of being shorter and having prior art in a well-known language family. And it doesn't collide with anything in rc/ksh grammar (backslash in those shells is only ever an escape character, and psh uses `''` for quoting, not backslash-escaping).
> 
> One practical concern: `\` followed by a newline. In traditional shells, `\&lt;newline&gt;` is line continuation. If psh preserves that (rc doesn't use backslash continuation, but ksh does), then `\params =&gt;` must have params on the same line as `\`. This is probably fine — lambdas are typically short — but it should be stated explicitly in the grammar.
> 
> ## Discipline Functions
> 
> Question 3 is straightforward. `fn x.set { body }` stays in the `fn` world. It's a named binding — it's registering a handler for the `.set` event on variable `x`. Writing `fn x.set = \val =&gt; body` would be mixing sorts: `fn` (command, binding) on the left, `\` (value, thunk) on the right. The `=` there is doing something weird — it's not an assignment (that's `x = val`), it's not a let binding. It's an unnecessary hybrid.
> 
> Discipline functions are fundamentally negative — they're copatterns, defined by what they do when triggered. They belong entirely in `fn` syntax. The body is a command block, not a value expression. Keeping them in `fn` is not just syntactically clean, it's polaritally correct.
> 
> ## The Constraint: `let f = fn { body }` is Illegal
> 
> This is the sharpest question. Under Lane's proposal, you can't write:
> 
> ```
> let f = fn { body }       # ILLEGAL: fn is command-sort only
> let f = \() =&gt; body        # LEGAL: lambda produces Val::Thunk
> ```
> 
> Is this the right constraint? Let me think about this through the Be lens.
> 
> In Be, you *could* construct a BHandler on the heap:
> ```cpp
> BHandler* h = new BHandler("myHandler");
> ```
> But this was useless until you did `looper-&gt;AddHandler(h)`. The object existed as a value, but it had no computational identity until registered. Nobody wrote library code that passed BHandlers around as data — they passed BMessengers (the positive shift of a handler).
> 
> The psh analogy: `fn name { body }` is the registration (AddHandler). `\() =&gt; body` is the "handler-as-portable-value" form — like BMessenger, it's the positive wrapping of a computation. You don't pass `fn` definitions around; you pass thunks.
> 
> The constraint is correct and it's not artificial. It prevents a category error: you cannot treat a mu-tilde binder (context extension) as a term (value). `fn` modifies Gamma; `\` produces a term in Gamma. These are different operations. The fact that they both involve "a block of code" is surface similarity, not structural identity.
> 
> The one wrinkle: `fn name { body }` captures no parameters. `\(x y) =&gt; body` does. So if you want a named function with explicit parameters, what's the syntax?
> 
> ```
> fn greet { echo hello $1 }       # positional params, rc-style
> let greet = \(name) =&gt; echo hello $name   # named params, lambda-style
> ```
> 
> These are genuinely different things with different semantics. The `fn` form uses positional parameters and dynamic scoping. The `\` form has named parameters. The split makes this difference *visible*, which is better than hiding it behind `fn` with lookahead disambiguation.
> 
> ## My Recommendation
> 
> **Split them.** `fn` for command-position named definitions. `\` for value-position lambdas. Two concepts, two forms. Be's principle: distinct things should look distinct.
> 
> The grammar changes:
> 
> ```
> fn_def        = 'fn' NAME body          # binding (command-sort)
> thunk_literal = '\' '(' NAME* ')' '=&gt;' body   # value (word-sort)
> ```
> 
> The `fn(params) body` production from the current spec (line 363) is replaced by `\(params) =&gt; body`. The parser no longer needs the lookahead disambiguation between `fn(` and `fn NAME`. `fn` is always a binding; `\` is always a value.
> 
> One thing to settle: does `\` require `=&gt;` always, or only for single-line bodies? I'd say: `\(params) =&gt; expr` for single-line, `\(params) { block }` for multi-line. Consistent with how `=&gt;` works everywhere else in the spec (single-line body introducer).
> 
> ```
> let inc = \(x) =&gt; expr $x + 1
> let greet = \(name) {
>     echo hello $name
>     echo welcome
> }
> ```
> 
> The `=&gt;` vs `{ }` distinction already exists throughout the control flow syntax. Reusing it here is free.</result>

> </task-notification>

### `2026-04-07T04:37:51.794Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a6ef15da11211199c</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01Pf6DFgfbdNkwibjtRxM3jn</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/25f44a96-9488-444d-b842-3df1553f5366/tasks/a6ef15da11211199c.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Session type: lambda syntax + sort boundary" completed</summary>
> <result>The prototype has no Thunk yet -- confirming the spec is ahead of the implementation. Now I can deliver the analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Verdict: Conditionally sound. The `\`-lambda proposal is CBPV-correct and grammar-safe, subject to two conditions: (1) the `=&gt;` token inside lambda bodies must be disambiguated from the `=&gt;` body introducer by parse context, and (2) currying with dynamic resolution requires explicit documentation that the intermediate thunk captures nothing -- the CBPV typing is honest but the ergonomics may mislead users into expecting closure semantics.**
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 1. Sort enforcement: `fn` vs `\`
> 
> The proposal maps cleanly onto Levy's CBPV (Levy 2004, §2.1-2.3). The two forms occupy different judgments:
> 
> - `fn name { body }` is a **context extension** judgment: Γ, f : U(A → F(B)) ⊢ program. It extends Γ with a named binding. In the sequent calculus reading (specification.md §The four sorts), this is a μ̃-binder -- it goes on the left of the turnstile.
> 
> - `\params =&gt; body` is a **term constructor** judgment: Γ ⊢ thunk(λx.M) : U(A → F(B)). It produces a value of thunk type. It goes on the right of the turnstile.
> 
> These are different judgment forms in Levy's §2.2 (Figure 2.1, the value/computation type formation rules). Making them syntactically distinct enforces the judgment-form boundary at parse time. This is strictly stronger than the current spec's `fn(params) body` syntax, which uses `fn` for both and disambiguates by one-token lookahead (`(` vs NAME). The `\` proposal eliminates the lookahead ambiguity entirely.
> 
> **Assessment: correct.** The sort boundary is real and syntactic enforcement is the right call. Levy's system has no single syntactic form that serves as both a context extension and a term constructor -- they are different rules. Making them look different in psh is honest.
> 
> ## 2. `fn`-only-in-command-position
> 
> The restriction that `fn` cannot appear in value position (`let f = fn { body }` is rejected) maps to Levy's type theory as follows:
> 
> - In CBPV, function definition in context is not `thunk(λx.M)`. It's a separate judgment that extends Γ. You don't "evaluate" a function definition to get a value -- you bind it, and it's available thereafter.
> - The thunk introduction `thunk(M)` (Levy 2004, §2.2, rule U-I) IS a value form. It's an expression that evaluates to a value of type U(C).
> 
> These are different things. The restriction enforces that difference. `fn name { body }` is Binding::Fn -- it extends Γ. `\params =&gt; body` is a Word variant -- it evaluates to Val::Thunk.
> 
> **Assessment: CBPV-correct.** The restriction is not arbitrary -- it reflects that context extension and term construction are different judgment forms. You can't "evaluate" Binding::Fn to get a Val, because binding is not evaluation. The `\` form exists precisely to produce a value.
> 
> ## 3. Grammar: `\` vs line continuation
> 
> This is the critical engineering question. The current spec (syntax.md §Line continuation, line 570-582) states:
> 
> &gt; A backslash immediately followed by a newline is consumed by the lexer as whitespace. Backslash is not otherwise a special character.
> 
> The `\` lambda proposal gives `\` a second role: when followed by a NAME (not a newline), it introduces a lambda. The lexer can disambiguate:
> 
> - `\` + newline = line continuation (consumed as whitespace)
> - `\` + NAME = lambda introducer
> - `\` + `(` = lambda with parenthesized parameter list (if that form is desired)
> - `\` + `=` `&gt;` = nullary lambda `\() =&gt; body` shorthand? (needs specification)
> 
> **This is unambiguous at the lexer level** because rc's rule is specifically `\` + `\n`. Any other character after `\` currently has no meaning (backslash is not an escape character in rc/psh). The `\` token is free for reuse in non-continuation contexts.
> 
> However, there is a subtlety: `\` must now become a word-starting token in value position. Currently `\` is not in `word_char` or `var_char` (syntax.md §Two character sets). It needs to be recognized as a value-position keyword, similar to how `fn(` is recognized as a thunk literal in the current spec. The parser, upon seeing `\` in value position (and the next character is not `\n`), enters the lambda production.
> 
> **No grammar conflict.** The `=&gt;` inside `\params =&gt; body` does not conflict with `=&gt; command` as a body introducer because:
> - The `=&gt; command` body form appears after control flow keywords (`if`, `for`, `while`, `match` pattern, `fn name`, etc.).
> - The `=&gt; body` inside `\params =&gt; body` appears after `\params`, which is a lambda introducer -- a value-position production, not a control flow keyword.
> - The parser knows which context it's in: if it's parsing a `value` production (RHS of `let`, function argument), `\` starts a lambda. The `=&gt;` after the params is the lambda arrow, not a body introducer.
> 
> But there's a composition case to address: `\params =&gt; { program }` should also be legal (multi-line lambda body). The `body` production already handles `{ program }` and `=&gt; command` -- but for lambdas, the `=&gt;` IS the separator between params and body, so the lambda production is:
> 
> ```
> lambda = '\' NAME* '=&gt;' body_content
> body_content = '{' program '}' | command
> ```
> 
> Wait -- this creates a subtle issue. If the lambda body is `=&gt; command`, then `\x =&gt; echo hello` parses as lambda with params `(x)` and body `echo hello`. Fine. But what about `\x =&gt; { echo hello; echo world }`? The `=&gt;` introduces the lambda, then `{` opens a block. This works because `body_content` tries `{` first.
> 
> What about `\x { echo hello }`? Without the `=&gt;`, this is just `\x` followed by `{ echo hello }` -- which would be a lambda `\x` with... what? If `=&gt;` is mandatory in the lambda form, this parses as a nullary lambda applied to argument `x` followed by a block, which is nonsensical in value position. **The `=&gt;` must be mandatory in the lambda form.** This is actually cleaner than the current `fn(params) body` spec, which uses the `body` production (allowing both `{...}` and `=&gt; cmd`) and could create `fn(x) =&gt; =&gt; cmd` ambiguity.
> 
> **Recommendation: the lambda production is `'\' NAME* '=&gt;' ('{' program '}' | command)`.** The `=&gt;` is the lambda arrow, not the body introducer. The braces are the block delimiter. This is unambiguous.
> 
> ## 4. Interaction with `return`
> 
> `\x =&gt; return $x` is `thunk(λx. return x)`. In CBPV:
> - `λx.M` has type `A → F(B)` (a computation that takes A and produces F(B))
> - `return x` has type `F(A)` (the value injection into the computation sort)
> - `thunk(λx. return x)` has type `U(A → F(A))` -- the identity thunk
> 
> This composes correctly. The lambda body is a computation (it can have effects, run commands, etc.), and `return` injects a value from the command sort into the value sort, exactly as specified (syntax.md §Value-producing blocks, line 240-266).
> 
> The interaction with `match` inside a lambda:
> 
> ```
> let classify = \x =&gt; match $x {
>     *.txt =&gt; return text;
>     *.rs  =&gt; return rust;
>     *     =&gt; return other
> }
> ```
> 
> This is `thunk(λx. match x { ... return ... })` = `U(Str → F(Str))`. Each arm's `return` is the F-introduction. The `match` is the coproduct/case elimination. Composition is standard CBPV.
> 
> **No RunOutcome interaction** -- RunOutcome doesn't exist in psh (that's a pane-app concept). The relevant concept is Status. A lambda body, when forced, produces a Status like any other command sequence. `return` inside the lambda produces a value AND a successful status. This is the same as `return` in any other block.
> 
> ## 5. Interaction with `try`
> 
> Two cases:
> 
> **Case A: `try` wrapping a thunk force.**
> ```
> try { $f arg }
> ```
> `$f` is a Val::Thunk. Forcing it runs the body. If the body fails (nonzero status), `try` catches it. This is straightforward -- `try` wraps any computation, and forcing a thunk IS a computation.
> 
> **Case B: lambda inside `try`.**
> ```
> let result = try { \() =&gt; curl $url }
> ```
> This creates a thunk inside the `try` block, then... does nothing with it. The thunk is created (value sort), not forced (computation sort). `try` checks status after each command, but creating a thunk is not a command -- it's a value expression on the RHS of... wait. This doesn't have a `let` inside the `try`. Let me re-read.
> 
> Actually: `try { \() =&gt; curl $url }` in value position. The `try` forks and captures. The forked body has one "command" that's... a bare value expression? A lambda literal in command position is not a command -- it's a value with no consumer. This is the same as writing `try { 42 }` -- the value `42` isn't a command.
> 
> The grammar says `command = binding | control | return_cmd | expr_cmd` and `expr_cmd = or_expr ('&amp;')?`. A bare lambda would need to be an `expr_cmd`, which means it would need to be parseable as a `simple_cmd`. But `\` doesn't start a `WORD` in command position.
> 
> **This is actually a non-issue.** If `\` only starts lambdas in value position, then `\() =&gt; curl $url` can only appear where values are expected. Inside `try { ... }`, the body is a `program` -- a sequence of commands. A lambda literal in command position is a parse error, which is correct. You'd write:
> 
> ```
> let result = try { curl $url }
> ```
> 
> or if you want to capture a thunk:
> 
> ```
> let f = \() =&gt; curl $url
> let result = try { $f }
> ```
> 
> **The syntax is unambiguous.** Lambda literals appear in value position. `try` bodies are command position. No confusion.
> 
> ## 6. Currying and dynamic resolution
> 
> ```
> \x =&gt; \y =&gt; expr $x + $y
> ```
> 
> In CBPV, this is `thunk(λx. return thunk(λy. return (x + y)))`. The typing:
> - Inner: `U(Int → F(Int))` 
> - Outer: `U(Int → F(U(Int → F(Int))))`
> 
> The inner lambda captures `$x` from the outer scope. But specification.md §Thunks (line 700) is clear: **"Free variables in the body resolve against the calling scope at force time, not the defining scope at creation time."**
> 
> This means currying does NOT work as expected. Consider:
> 
> ```
> let add = \x =&gt; \y =&gt; expr $x + $y
> let add3 = `{ $add 3 }
> $add3 5    # expects 8, but $x is not bound in the calling scope
> ```
> 
> When `$add` is forced with argument 3, the body `\y =&gt; expr $x + $y` evaluates. But `\y =&gt; expr $x + $y` is a value (thunk literal) -- it doesn't "run," it just constructs a Val::Thunk. The `$x` inside it is an AST reference, not a captured value. When `$add3` (the inner thunk) is later forced with 5, `$x` resolves in the calling scope of THAT force, not in the scope where `$add 3` ran.
> 
> **This breaks currying.** The CBPV type `U(A → F(U(B → F(C))))` is correct, but the dynamic resolution semantics means the intermediate thunk doesn't close over `$x`. The outer lambda's parameter binding is gone by the time the inner lambda is forced.
> 
> There's a subtlety: `\x =&gt; \y =&gt; expr $x + $y` -- when the outer lambda is forced with `$add 3`, the body runs in a scope where `x = 3`. The inner lambda `\y =&gt; expr $x + $y` is constructed as a Val::Thunk (AST data, no closure). The outer lambda returns this thunk (via `return` or as the last expression). The inner thunk is now a free-standing value. When later forced, `$x` looks up the calling scope -- and `x` may or may not be bound there.
> 
> **This is a fundamental consequence of dynamic scoping + no closures.** It's not a bug -- it's the design. But it means `U(A → F(U(B → F(C))))` is a lie at the semantic level. The type says "a function that returns a function," but the inner "function" doesn't actually close over the outer parameters.
> 
> **Assessment: the CBPV typing is structurally correct (the AST has the right shape) but semantically weaker than CBPV would guarantee.** In Levy's CBPV, `thunk(λx. return thunk(λy. M))` DOES close over `x` because CBPV has lexical scoping. psh's dynamic resolution breaks this. The `\` syntax makes this more visible (users will try to curry), which could be a footgun.
> 
> **Condition for soundness:** document that `\` lambdas do not capture free variables. Currying requires explicit binding:
> 
> ```
> let add = \x =&gt; {
>     let captured_x = $x
>     return \y =&gt; expr $captured_x + $y
> }
> ```
> 
> Wait -- this doesn't work either, because the inner thunk still doesn't close over `captured_x`. The only way currying works is if the inner thunk is forced in a scope where the outer parameter is still bound -- i.e., the inner thunk is forced INSIDE the outer lambda body, not returned and forced elsewhere.
> 
> **This is the honest analysis: psh thunks are not closures. `\` sugar doesn't change that. Currying is not expressible.** The spec already says this (syntax.md line 700-702: "Closures (capture-by-value at creation time) are a possible future extension but not part of the initial design"). The `\` syntax just makes the gap more salient.
> 
> ## 7. Concrete Rust types
> 
> The AST change is:
> 
> **Current spec** (syntax.md line 363):
> ```
> thunk_literal = 'fn' '(' NAME* ')' body
> ```
> 
> **Proposed**:
> ```
> lambda = '\' NAME* '=&gt;' ('{' program '}' | command)
> ```
> 
> In the AST enum `Word`:
> ```rust
> // Current (not yet implemented):
> // ThunkLiteral { params: Vec&lt;String&gt;, body: Vec&lt;Command&gt; }
> 
> // Proposed:
> Lambda { params: Vec&lt;String&gt;, body: Vec&lt;Command&gt; }
> ```
> 
> The rename from `ThunkLiteral` to `Lambda` is appropriate. In CBPV, the thunk is `U(λx.M)` -- the lambda IS the content of the thunk. `Word::Lambda` says "this word evaluates to a thunk containing this lambda." The name is honest.
> 
> **No `=&gt;` conflict with match arms.** Match arms use `pattern '=&gt;' command+` inside `match { }`. Lambda uses `'\' NAME* '=&gt;' body_content` in value position. These are in different parse contexts and do not interfere. The parser enters the match arm production only inside `match { }`. The parser enters the lambda production only in value position upon seeing `\`.
> 
> ## 8. The `fn(params)` vs `\params =&gt;` comparison
> 
> The current spec uses `fn(params) body` for thunk literals. The `\` proposal replaces this. Comparison:
> 
> | Property | `fn(params) body` | `\params =&gt; body` |
> |----------|-------------------|-------------------|
> | Disambiguation | `fn` + `(` = thunk; `fn` + NAME = binding | `\` = always thunk; `fn` = always binding |
> | Lookahead | 1 token (`(` vs NAME) | 0 tokens (`\` is unambiguous) |
> | Sort visibility | `fn` used for both sorts (confusing) | Different tokens for different sorts (honest) |
> | `=&gt;` in body | Uses `body` production (both `{...}` and `=&gt; cmd`) | `=&gt;` is the lambda arrow; `{...}` for multi-line |
> | Familiarity | shell-like | Haskell/ML-like |
> 
> The `\` form is strictly better for sort enforcement and parse simplicity. The one concern is that `\` in a shell context may surprise users who expect it to be line continuation. But rc's rule is specific: `\` + `\n` = continuation, `\` + anything else = currently undefined. The `\` lambda proposal fills the undefined space.
> 
> ## Summary of conditions
> 
> 1. **The `=&gt;` inside `\params =&gt; body` must be specified as the lambda arrow, not the `body` production's `=&gt; command` form.** The lambda production is its own thing: `'\' NAME* '=&gt;' ('{' program '}' | command)`. This prevents `\x =&gt; =&gt; echo hello` from being parseable.
> 
> 2. **Currying must be documented as unsupported.** The CBPV type `U(A → F(U(B → F(C))))` is structurally valid but semantically hollow without closures. Users who see `\x =&gt; \y =&gt; ...` will expect Haskell-like currying. They will get dynamic-scope confusion. The spec should state this explicitly where it introduces `\`.
> 
> 3. **`\` in `word_char` / token classification.** `\` is currently not in either character set and is only special before `\n`. The lexer needs a new rule: in value position, `\` followed by a non-newline character starts the lambda production. This is clean because `\` is currently unused in that context.
> 
> 4. **Nullary lambdas.** `\() =&gt; body` or `\ =&gt; body`? The spec needs to pick one. `\() =&gt; body` parallels the current `fn() body` form. `\ =&gt; body` is shorter but may look like a bare `\` (line continuation with no newline?). I'd recommend `\() =&gt; body` for nullary, keeping `\x =&gt; body` for unary, and `\x y =&gt; body` for multi-param.
> 
> The proposal is CBPV-sound, grammar-safe, and an improvement over the current `fn(params)` form for sort enforcement. The main risk is the currying footgun, which is inherent to the dynamic-scoping decision, not to the `\` syntax itself.</result>

> <summary>Agent "Update: thunk captures for currying" completed</summary>
> <result>Good. The spec shows `Thunk` as a separate struct with captures. The implementation uses an inline enum variant instead of a separate struct, but the fields match — that's a fine implementation choice.
> 
> Here is the relay message for the pane-architect agent:
> 
> ---
> 
> **DESIGN UPDATE from Lane -- Thunk gains capture-by-value for currying.**
> 
> The `Val::Thunk` variant in `value.rs` now has a `captures` field. The change has been applied:
> 
> ```rust
> Thunk {
>     params: Vec&lt;String&gt;,
>     body: Vec&lt;Command&gt;,
>     captures: Vec&lt;(String, Val)&gt;,
> }
> ```
> 
> The spec (`docs/syntax.md`, lines 743-788) already documents the full semantics. Here is what the evaluator must do when you get to implementation:
> 
> **At lambda construction time** (`\params =&gt; body`):
> 1. Walk the body AST, collect free `$var` references
> 2. Subtract the lambda's own params
> 3. Snapshot remaining bound values from the current scope into `captures`
> 
> **At force time** (`$f args`):
> 1. Restore captures into the scope before running the body
> 
> This enables currying:
> ```
> let add = \x =&gt; \y =&gt; expr $x + $y
> let add3 = `{ $add 3 }    # inner thunk captures x=3
> $add3 5                     # 8
> ```
> 
> **Key constraints:**
> - Named functions (`fn name { body }`) do NOT capture -- they continue using dynamic resolution with positional params. Capture is lambda-only.
> - `PartialEq` compares captures too (structural equality). This is already handled since `Val` derives `PartialEq` and the `captures` field is `Vec&lt;(String, Val)&gt;`.
> - Thunks with no free variables have empty captures (the common case).
> 
> **What was changed:**
> - `/Users/lane/src/lin/psh/src/value.rs` -- `Val::Thunk` variant now includes `captures: Vec&lt;(String, Val)&gt;`. Doc comment updated. All existing match sites use `{ .. }` wildcards and compile cleanly.
> - The spec was already updated by Lane before this change.
> - `cargo check`, `cargo clippy --workspace` -- zero errors, zero warnings.
> - Tests: 155 pass, 4 pre-existing lambda parser failures (combine rewrite in progress, unrelated to this change).
> 
> **No evaluator changes yet** -- `exec.rs` is awaiting the full rewrite. The free-variable walker and capture logic will be part of the evaluator implementation (roadmap items for thunk forcing). The AST node `Value::Lambda` stays as-is since captures are computed at eval time, not parse time.</result>
