### `2026-04-07T05:32:03.067Z`

> ---
>   To: psh plugin architecture exploration agent
>   From: psh development session (Opus 4.6, 2026-04-06)
>   Re: The shell as kernel — discipline functions, namespace, and visual widgets as the plugin API
> 
>   ---
>   Context
> 
>   psh is a system shell at /Users/lane/src/lin/psh/. Read these in order:
> 
>   1. docs/syntax.md (1103 lines) — the normative grammar. This is the spec. Features marked [planned] are the
>   target. The current prototype implements a subset and is being scrapped — an implementation restart from the
>   spec is planned (PLAN.md §Now).
>   2. docs/specification.md (801 lines) — theoretical foundation (duploid, polarity, profunctor AST, CBPV).
>   3. PLAN.md — roadmap. Read §"Design position", §"What the spec adds", and §"Implementation restart".
>   4. CLAUDE.md — project instructions.
> 
>   The existing prototype (5940 lines, 157 tests) validated feasibility. The spec supersedes it. The
>   reimplementation uses combine (monadic parser combinators, mandated in PLAN.md) and targets the full syntax.md
>   grammar: 9-variant Val, match with => arms and ; separators, try (scoped ⅋ and value-position capture), return
>   for value-producing blocks, accessors, free carets, two-alphabet split.
> 
>   psh must be an excellent standalone shell first — usable as a login shell on any Unix without pane.
> 
>   The thesis
> 
>   Three existing mechanisms constitute the plugin API. No plugin system needs to be designed.
> 
>   Discipline functions ARE the plugin interface. .get fires on variable read as a notification hook (syntax.md
>   §Discipline functions: "a side-effect hook, not a view transformer" — the .get body cannot influence what $x
>   evaluates to; it runs for side effects only, in a readonly scope). .set fires on write with reentrancy guard.
>   Function wrapping (fn cd { builtin cd $*; ... }) intercepts builtins. Signal functions (fn sigint { }) handle
>   signals. A plugin is a psh script that defines functions and disciplines.
> 
>   The namespace IS the plugin's data layer. get/set resolve against a unified namespace: local variables,
>   environment, and (when available) pane at /pane/. Live re-evaluation uses .get disciplines (syntax.md §Live
>   re-evaluation):
> 
>   let mut cursor : Int = 0
>   fn cursor.get { cursor = `{ get /pane/editor/attrs/cursor } }
> 
>   Every $cursor access fires the discipline, refreshing the stored value. For error-tracked live variables:
> 
>   let mut cursor : Result[Int] = try { get /pane/editor/attrs/cursor }
>   fn cursor.get { cursor = try { get /pane/editor/attrs/cursor } }
> 
>   Namerefs ARE the composition mechanism. ref cursor = /pane/editor/attrs/cursor — plugins compose through
>   indirection.
> 
>   The value model plugins operate on
> 
>   syntax.md §Type system defines nine Val variants:
> 
>   pub enum Val {
>       Unit,                       // empty value, false
>       Bool(bool),                 // true | false
>       Int(i64),                   // data integer
>       Str(String),                // universal type, rc heritage
>       Path(PathBuf),              // filesystem path
>       ExitCode(i32),              // computation outcome, inverted truthiness
>       List(Vec<Val>),             // homogeneous sequence
>       Tuple(Vec<Val>),            // products, 0-based Lens access via .0 .1
>       Sum(String, Box<Val>),      // coproducts, Prism access via match
>   }
> 
>   Key features plugins use:
> 
>   - Sum values for structured results: ok 42 → Sum("ok", Int(42))
>   - Result[T] = T | ExitCode, sugar with implied tags ok/err. Produced by try.
>   - match with => and ;: structural (tag + binding) and glob arms. Newlines inside match { } are trivia.
>   match $result {
>       ok $v  => echo $v;
>       err $e => echo 'error: '$e
>   }
>   - try block (scoped ⅋): abort on first error, transfer to else. Boolean contexts exempt.
>   try {
>       let title = `{ get /pane/focused/attrs/title }
>       echo $title
>   } else $e {
>       echo 'unavailable: '$e
>   }
>   - try in value position: let result : Result[Int] = try { cmd } — forks, captures stdout + exit status, returns
>    Result[T]. Shares capture_subprocess primitive with `{cmd} (siblings, not desugaring).
>   - Accessors [planned]: $result.ok (Prism preview), $pos.0 (Lens projection), $e.code (ExitCode extraction).
>   Compose: $result.ok.name is Prism then Lens (AffineTraversal).
>   - return for value-producing blocks (CBPV's return : A → F(A)):
>   let icon = match $type {
>       editor   => return '📝';
>       terminal => return '💻';
>       *        => return '?'
>   }
>   - => as single-line body introducer, uniform across all control flow:
>   if cond => cmd, for x in list => cmd, match pat => cmd.
>   - ExitCode inverts Int truthiness: ExitCode(0) is true (success), Int(0) is false. Different sorts.
>   ExitCode→Int coercion prohibited.
>   - Sum is always true — dispatch on tag via match, not if. Prevents conflating error handling with truthiness.
>   - Type erasure at pipe boundaries (syntax.md §Display): pipes carry bytes. Sum displays payload-only (tag
>   stripped). Types serve in-process safety and interactive affordances.
> 
>   The visual plugin API
> 
>   A four-agent roundtable established the architecture. Key constraint: psh the interpreter must not render.
> 
>   Two layers, matching the two-binary architecture:
> 
>   psh (interpreter)          produces structured UI requests
>                              fn complete, fn keymap.*, fn prompt, fn status.*
>                              menu sends requests, receives selections
> 
>   host (rendering backend)   consumes requests, renders, returns results
>                              standalone: reedline compiled into psh binary
>                              pane-terminal: LooperCore, compositor rendering
>                              ssh/dumb: graceful degradation
> 
>   Plugins write to menu and function conventions. They never touch the rendering layer. Plugin scripts are
>   identical in all modes.
> 
>   The menu protocol — a structured request, not a rendering builtin:
> 
>   menu returns Result[T] where T inherits the input list's element type. Cancellation is Sum("err", ExitCode(1)).
>    All examples use syntax.md grammar:
> 
>   # which-key popup
>   fn keymap.leader {
>       let choice = try { menu -popup -timeout 2 ('g : git' 'f : files' 'p : pane') }
>       match $choice {
>           ok $k => match $k {
>               g* => git_menu;
>               f* => file_menu;
>               p* => pane_menu
>           };
>           err $e => ()
>       }
>   }
> 
>   # vertical fuzzy completion (ivy/vertico style)
>   fn complete.history {
>       menu -vertical -fuzzy `{ history -list }
>   }
> 
>   # transient command menu (magit/transient.el style)
>   fn git_menu {
>       let choice = try { menu -transient ('s : stage' 'c : commit' 'p : push') }
>       match $choice {
>           ok $action => match $action {
>               s* => git add -p;
>               c* => git commit;
>               p* => git push
>           };
>           err $e => ()
>       }
>   }
> 
>   # status line segments
>   fn status.git {
>       let branch = try { `{ git branch --show-current } }
>       match $branch {
>           ok $b  => return $b;
>           err $e => return ''
>       }
>   }
>   fn status.path { return `{ basename `{ pwd } } }
>   fn status.jobs { return $#jobs }
> 
>   Formal structure (from session type and optics agents):
> 
>   ┌──────────────────┬──────────────────────────────┬────────────────────────────┬──────────────────────────┐
>   │     Pattern      │           Polarity           │           Optic            │      New machinery?      │
>   ├──────────────────┼──────────────────────────────┼────────────────────────────┼──────────────────────────┤
>   │ menu             │ ↓→↑ (same as comsub)         │ AffineTraversal +          │ No — blocking call, ⊕    │
>   │                  │                              │ effectful witness          │ for cancel               │
>   ├──────────────────┼──────────────────────────────┼────────────────────────────┼──────────────────────────┤
>   │ Keymap dispatch  │ Cut (positive event ×        │ Prism per trie level       │ No — standard dispatch   │
>   │                  │ negative handler)            │                            │                          │
>   ├──────────────────┼──────────────────────────────┼────────────────────────────┼──────────────────────────┤
>   │ Keymap → menu    │ ↓→↑→↓→↑                      │ Same                       │ Depth-1 reentrancy,      │
>   │ nesting          │                              │                            │ modal stack              │
>   ├──────────────────┼──────────────────────────────┼────────────────────────────┼──────────────────────────┤
>   │ Transient keymap │ Focused phase (save/restore) │ Prism composition          │ Push/pop keymap stack    │
>   ├──────────────────┼──────────────────────────────┼────────────────────────────┼──────────────────────────┤
>   │ Completion       │ Fold + user-resolved         │ Plugins provide Fold, host │ Plugins never touch      │
>   │                  │ AffineTraversal              │  resolves                  │ rendering                │
>   ├──────────────────┼──────────────────────────────┼────────────────────────────┼──────────────────────────┤
>   │ Status line      │ Traversal over Getter        │ fn status.* enumerated     │ Registration = function  │
>   │                  │ segments                     │                            │ definition               │
>   ├──────────────────┼──────────────────────────────┼────────────────────────────┼──────────────────────────┤
>   │ Type flow        │ Prism projection             │ List[T] → T preserved      │ Result[T] wraps          │
>   │ through menu     │                              │                            │ cancellation             │
>   └──────────────────┴──────────────────────────────┴────────────────────────────┴──────────────────────────┘
> 
>   What the host implements (compiled widget surface):
> 
>   - Popup overlay (anchored at cursor, dismissable, optional timeout)
>   - Vertical list with selection (scrollable, optional fuzzy filter)
>   - Transient keymap display (show available bindings, consume one key)
>   - Status line (segmented, styled)
>   - Prompt rendering (multi-line, styled)
> 
>   What plugins never touch: terminal raw mode, escape sequences, cursor positioning, screen dimensions.
> 
>   What to explore
> 
>   1. Is the discipline+namespace+function+menu model sufficient? Walk through concrete plugins using the full
>   syntax.md grammar (try/match/=>/Result[T]/Sum/accessors):
>     - Prompt rendering (git branch, pane state, exit code coloring)
>     - Tab completion (filesystem, pane namespace, git refs, history)
>     - Syntax highlighting (type-aware: red for mismatches in let bindings)
>     - Directory hooks (auto-activate virtualenvs, update title)
>     - Keybindings (Ctrl-R, leader key sequences, vi/emacs modes)
>     - Theme system (colors, prompt style, menu appearance)
>   2. Where does the model break? Syntax highlighting needs the parser's token stream during keystroke processing.
>    Accessors ($result.ok) need type-level dispatch. What else can't be psh functions + menu protocol?
>   3. The composition problem. Two plugins both define fn cd. Options:
>     - Hook lists (fn cd calls multiple handlers in order)
>     - Advice/wrapping (chain without shadowing)
>     - rc's model (last wins, explicit chaining)
>   4. The host interface. What Rust trait does the host implement? Capability negotiation or graceful degradation?
>   5. The ~/.psh/ convention. Plugin loading, ordering, dependencies.
>   6. The pane dimension. Pane-aware plugins use get/set on /pane/ paths + .get disciplines for live variables
>   (syntax.md §Live re-evaluation). Same scripts, better when pane is available.
>   7. The acid test: a package manager in psh.
> 
>   fn pkg.install {
>       let name : Str = $1
>       try {
>           let registry = `{ get pkg.registry }
>           curl -s $registry/$name/latest > ~/.psh/plugins/$name^.psh
>           . ~/.psh/plugins/$name^.psh
>           echo pkg: installed $name
>       } else $e {
>           echo 'pkg: install failed: '$e
>       }
>   }
> 
>   fn pkg.search {
>       let results = `{ curl -s `{get pkg.registry}^/search?q=$1 }
>       let choice = try { menu -vertical -fuzzy $results }
>       match $choice {
>           ok $name => pkg.install $name;
>           err $e   => echo cancelled
>       }
>   }
> 
>   fn pkg.list {
>       for f in ~/.psh/plugins/*.psh => echo `{ basename $f .psh }
>   }
> 
>   fn pkg.remove {
>       let name : Str = $1
>       try {
>           rm ~/.psh/plugins/$name^.psh
>           echo pkg: removed $name
>       } else $e {
>           echo 'pkg: '$name' not installed'
>       }
>   }
> 
>   Design constraints
> 
>   - The prototype is ~6000 lines. An implementation restart is planned against the spec.
>   - docs/syntax.md is the normative grammar. The plugin architecture should work with the full spec.
>   - combine is mandated for the parser rewrite (PLAN.md §Implementation restart).
>   - The four design agents (Plan 9, Be, session type, optics) are available for roundtables.
>   - ksh26's SPEC.md at /Users/lane/src/ksh/ksh/SPEC.md is a reference for polarity analysis.
>   - If plugin needs reveal missing primitives, add them to the spec before reimplementing.
> 
>   What we're NOT doing
> 
>   - Not building a package manager into psh. The package manager validates the API.
>   - Not building a DSL for plugin configuration. Variables and disciplines.
>   - Not adding an event bus. Disciplines and signals ARE the event system.
>   - Not making the parser extensible. The grammar is fixed (syntax.md). Plugins add behavior, not syntax.
>   - Not making psh depend on a rendering library. The host provides rendering.
>   - Not coupling to pane. Plugins work standalone. Pane is a superpower.
> 
>   ---

> 
> ## Roundtable Analysis: psh Plugin Architecture as Session Types
> 
> **Overall verdict: Conditionally sound.** The thesis -- that psh needs no plugin framework, only discipline functions + namespace + function conventions + `menu` -- is well-grounded in CBPV and session type theory. The conditions are stated below per question.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 1. `menu` as a Session Type
> 
> **Protocol characterization:**
> 
> The proposed protocol `Send(MenuRequest) ; Recv(Result[T]) ; End` is correct but underspecified. The full session type is:
> 
> ```
> menu : !MenuRequest(items: List[A], opts: DisplayOpts) . ?Result[A] . end
> ```
> 
> where `A` is parametrically polymorphic in the element type of the input list. In Honda/Yoshida/Carbone's MPST notation (Honda et al., POPL 2008, Def. 2.1), this is a two-party synchronous protocol between the shell process (initiator) and the host (pane-terminal or a stub in standalone mode).
> 
> **What's right:** This is a *degenerate* session type -- a single request-response with no branching or recursion. In the classification of Toninho/Caires/Pfenning (ESOP 2011, Section 3), it's a linear channel with exactly one exchange. The parametric polymorphism in `T` -- the element type of the input list flowing through to the result type -- is well-typed under the polymorphic session types of Caires/Pfenning/Toninho (ESOP 2013, Section 5). Concretely: if you pass `List[Sum("git", Unit) | Sum("files", Unit)]`, you get back `Result[Sum("git", Unit) | Sum("files", Unit)]`.
> 
> **What could be more structured:** The protocol as stated doesn't distinguish between user cancellation and selection failure. Three outcomes exist:
> - User selected item `a : A` -- `ok a`
> - User cancelled (Escape, click-away) -- this is not a failure, it's a coproduct branch
> - Host unavailable -- actual failure
> 
> A more precise protocol would be:
> 
> ```
> menu : !MenuRequest . ?(selected A + cancelled + failed ExitCode) . end
> ```
> 
> In psh's value model, this maps to `Result[A | Unit]` where `ok $v` is selection, `ok ()` is cancellation, `err $e` is failure. But this conflates cancellation with "returned Unit." The cleaner encoding uses Sum tags: `selected $v | cancelled () | err $e`. Whether this matters depends on whether plugins need to distinguish "user said no" from "host not available." I suspect they do -- a keymap handler that opens a menu should take different action on cancel (return to editing) vs. failure (show error).
> 
> **Recommendation:** `menu` should return `Sum` with three tags. The session type stays single-exchange; the return type gains a branch.
> 
> **Static guarantees:** In psh, none of this is enforced by session types at compile time. `menu` is a builtin. The protocol is in the evaluator's implementation of the builtin, not in a type-level session channel. The guarantee is: the `menu` builtin always returns one of the three Sum variants. This is enforced by the Rust match on the internal result in the builtin implementation. Sufficient -- the protocol is too simple to benefit from external session typing.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 2. The Discipline Function Protocol
> 
> **Characterization:**
> 
> The `.get` and `.set` disciplines are NOT session types in the standard sense. They are *callback protocols* -- synchronous, non-negotiated, single-invocation. In session-type terms, they would be:
> 
> ```
> .get : ?trigger . !(side-effects) . end     -- notification, value flows through unchanged
> .set : ?set(newval) . !(side-effects) . end -- mutation hook, store happens afterward
> ```
> 
> But this is misleading. Session types describe protocols between *independent agents*. Discipline functions run *within the same thread, same process, same scope chain*. They are function calls with a dynamic reentrancy guard, not channel communications. The appropriate formal framework is not session types but CBPV's effect discipline.
> 
> **The reentrancy guard as linearity:**
> 
> The claim that the reentrancy guard "prevents re-entry -- this is linearity enforcement" is *partially* correct but needs precision. The guard ensures that during a single `.set` execution, recursive `.set` on the same variable is bypassed. This is not linearity (use exactly once) but *mutual exclusion on a reentrant path* -- closer to the "no nested critical sections on the same lock" property.
> 
> In the sequent calculus reading from `specification.md` Section "The let/control duality," `.set` is a mu-tilde binder (Kleisli, effectful mutation) and `.get` is its dual (co-Kleisli, pure observation). The reentrancy guard prevents the Kleisli arrow from being applied recursively during its own execution. In the duploid framework (Munch-Maccagnoni, "Syntax and Models of a Non-Associative Composition of Programs and Proofs," Section 2.1), this prevents the non-associative composition from collapsing -- without the guard, `set x = (fn x.set { x = $1 })` would produce an infinite descent where the cut-elimination procedure does not terminate.
> 
> **Protocol violations the current design allows:**
> 
> 1. **`.get` side effects that observe stale state.** The `.get` discipline fires as a notification, runs in a readonly scope, and the stored value flows through unchanged. But the `.get` body can fire external commands (e.g., `get /pane/...`) whose results are discarded. If a `.get` body has side effects to stderr or to external state, those effects happen on every `$x` access. Multiple accesses produce multiple side effects. This is contraction on the side-effect channel -- the `.get` discipline admits contraction (multiple firings) even though the value read is idempotent. Not a protocol violation per se, but a source of surprises if `.get` bodies are expensive or non-idempotent.
> 
> 2. **`.set` followed by `.get` ordering.** After `x = val`, the `.set` fires, then the value is stored. A subsequent `$x` fires `.get`. But `.get` can reassign `x` (via `cursor = \`{ get /pane/... }`), which fires `.set` again. The reentrancy guard only prevents recursive `.set` during the *same* `.set` execution -- it does not prevent the `.get`-triggered assignment from firing a new `.set`. This is a two-step cycle: `.get` -&gt; assignment -&gt; `.set` -&gt; store -&gt; return. The `active_disciplines` HashSet prevents infinite descent (the `.get`-triggered assignment's `.set` won't re-trigger `.get`), but the interaction is subtle. The specification at lines 102-115 of `syntax.md` says ".get body runs in a readonly scope (mutations rejected)" -- if this is enforced, the cycle cannot occur. But `specification.md` lines 433-435 says the `.get` body fires on `$x` access and can "observe, log, trigger side effects (to stderr)." The live re-evaluation pattern at `syntax.md` lines 1016-1017 shows `fn cursor.get { cursor = \`{ get /pane/... } }` -- an assignment inside `.get`. This contradicts the "readonly scope" claim unless the assignment bypasses the readonly check for the discipline's own variable.
> 
> **This is a real inconsistency in the specification.** Either `.get` runs in a readonly scope (mutations rejected, breaking the live re-evaluation pattern) or `.get` can mutate its own variable (requiring the reentrancy guard to prevent the cycle). The specification says both. The live re-evaluation pattern requires the latter. The formal characterization requires resolving which one it is.
> 
> **My reading of intent:** `.get` is readonly *except for the variable it's attached to*. The discipline can update its own stored value (the "live re-evaluation" pattern), but cannot mutate other variables. The reentrancy guard prevents the update from re-triggering `.get`. This is a *self-referential update* -- the co-Kleisli observation can refresh its own cache. This is sound if and only if the guard prevents the cycle, which it does: `.get` fires -&gt; body assigns `cursor` -&gt; `.set` fires (if defined) with guard active -&gt; `.set` bypassed (already active? no -- `.set` and `.get` are different disciplines). Wait. The guard is keyed on the discipline name. `cursor.get` and `cursor.set` are different entries. So `fn cursor.get { cursor = ... }` would trigger `cursor.set`. Does `cursor.set` re-trigger `cursor.get`? No -- the assignment inside `.set` bypasses the discipline if `cursor.set` is already active. But `cursor.get` is still in the active set... unless the guard tracks the *variable* not the *discipline-name*.
> 
> This needs to be nailed down in implementation. The protocol is: `.get` fires -&gt; body runs -&gt; body assigns to `cursor` -&gt; check if `cursor.set` is active (no) -&gt; fire `cursor.set` -&gt; `.set` body assigns to `cursor` -&gt; check if `cursor.set` is active (yes, guard blocks) -&gt; return -&gt; `.get` returns -&gt; value read. The depth is bounded at 2 (`.get` -&gt; `.set`, then guarded). Sound, but the bound is not obvious from the spec.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 3. Keymap Dispatch as Protocol Composition
> 
> **The multi-step protocol:**
> 
> ```
> keymap.leader : ?Recv(Key) . !Send(MenuRequest) . ?Recv(Selection) . 
>                 Branch { git =&gt; P_git, files =&gt; P_files, pane =&gt; P_pane } . end
> ```
> 
> This is a *dependent* session type -- the branch after `Recv(Selection)` depends on the *value* of the selection. In the framework of Toninho/Caires/Pfenning (ESOP 2011, Section 4, dependent session types), this is:
> 
> ```
> x : A |- case x of { git =&gt; P_git | files =&gt; P_files | pane =&gt; P_pane }
> ```
> 
> where the continuation protocol depends on the received value.
> 
> **Composition correctness:**
> 
> The nesting (keymap -&gt; menu -&gt; sub-menu) is sequential composition of session types. In Ferrite's framework (Chen/Balzer/Toninho, ECOOP 2022, Section 4.1, Def. 4.1 -- protocol sequencing), `S1 ; S2` requires `S1` to reach `End` before `S2` begins. Here: `menu` completes (returns `Result[T]`), then `match` dispatches. The `menu` session is consumed before the dispatch begins. Composition is correct by the sequential nature of shell evaluation -- psh is single-threaded, CBV, one command at a time.
> 
> **Timeout:**
> 
> `menu -timeout 2` introduces a temporal constraint. In session type theory, timed session types (Bocchi/Yang/Yoshida, "Timed Asynchronous Session Types," CONCUR 2014, Def. 3.1) annotate each communication action with a time bound. The timeout converts a potentially-infinite wait into a bounded one, changing the return type from `Result[A]` to `Result[A]` where the `err` branch now includes timeout as a failure case.
> 
> In psh's model, this is straightforward: `menu -timeout 2` returns `err 'timeout'` (or `cancelled ()` if we adopt the three-tag model from question 1). No protocol composition issue -- the timeout is internal to the `menu` builtin's implementation. The shell sees a return value; it doesn't see the timeout mechanism.
> 
> **Does composition preserve properties?**
> 
> Yes, trivially. Each step is a synchronous builtin call or function invocation. There is no concurrent interaction, no interleaving, no channel that could be left dangling. The "protocol" is really just function call sequencing with typed return values. Session types are descriptive here, not prescriptive -- they characterize the interaction pattern but don't add enforcement beyond what the evaluator already provides.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 4. The `fn cd` Composition Problem
> 
> **This is a genuine protocol compatibility problem, and session types can characterize it precisely.**
> 
> Two plugins both define `fn cd`. psh's function namespace is flat -- later definitions shadow earlier ones. Only one `fn cd` survives. This is not composition; it is replacement.
> 
> The actual mechanism for before/after advice in shells is the discipline function pattern: wrap the original, call it, then do your thing. In psh:
> 
> ```
> # Plugin A: change prompt after cd
> fn cd {
>     builtin cd $*
>     prompt = `{ pwd }' $ '
> }
> 
> # Plugin B: sync pane namespace after cd  
> fn cd {
>     builtin cd $*
>     set /pane/terminal/attrs/cwd `{ pwd }
> }
> ```
> 
> If A loads first, B replaces it. A's prompt update is lost.
> 
> **Session type characterization:** Each `fn cd` wrapper has the protocol:
> 
> ```
> cd_wrapper : ?args . !builtin_cd(args) . ?status . !post_effects . end
> ```
> 
> The two wrappers have *identical protocol structure* but different post-effects. They are not composable by session type sequencing because they both claim the same channel endpoint (the `cd` function name). This is a *name collision*, not a protocol incompatibility.
> 
> **Can session types help?**
> 
> Not directly -- this is a namespace problem, not a protocol problem. But the *advice pattern* (aspect-oriented programming terminology) can be characterized. Define:
> 
> ```
> type BeforeAdvice = ?args . !modified_args . end
> type AfterAdvice  = ?args . ?status . !effects . end  
> type AroundAdvice = ?args . !(call_inner(args)) . ?status . !effects . end
> ```
> 
> If psh had a discipline-like mechanism for functions (not just variables), plugins could declare:
> 
> ```
> fn cd.before { ... }    # fires before cd, can modify args
> fn cd.after  { ... }    # fires after cd, can react to status
> ```
> 
> Multiple `.before` and `.after` disciplines could stack (unlike variable disciplines, which are single-layer). The protocol for the composed stack is:
> 
> ```
> cd_composed : before_1 ; before_2 ; ... ; builtin_cd ; after_1 ; after_2 ; ... ; end
> ```
> 
> This is sequential composition, which preserves all session-type properties (Ferrite, Section 4.1). The ordering of `.before` hooks is source order (load order); the ordering of `.after` hooks is the same. This is well-defined.
> 
> **Recommendation:** Function disciplines (`.before`/`.after` on function names) are the principled solution. They compose sequentially, they don't shadow, and the protocol is characterizable. This is a ksh93 FPATH convention made explicit. Whether psh should add this is a design question, not a session-type question. The session types just confirm that if you add it, it composes correctly.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 5. Plugin Loading as Context Extension
> 
> **CBPV characterization:**
> 
> `. ~/.psh/plugins/foo.psh` extends Gamma with new bindings. In the sequent calculus reading (`specification.md` Section "The four sorts"), each `fn` definition is a mu-tilde binder:
> 
> ```
> Gamma, f : A |- Delta
> ```
> 
> Sourcing a plugin script is a sequence of such extensions. Order matters because later bindings shadow earlier ones. In CBPV terms (Levy, "Call-by-Push-Value: A Functional/Imperative Synthesis," Section 2.3), this is a sequence of `let` bindings:
> 
> ```
> let f1 = thunk1 in let f2 = thunk2 in ... in body
> ```
> 
> where `body` is the rest of the shell session.
> 
> **Can session types reason about composition order?**
> 
> Not in the standard sense. Session types describe communication protocols between concurrent agents. Plugin loading is a sequential, single-threaded operation. The relevant formal framework is *module systems* and *mixin composition*, not session types.
> 
> However, if we view each plugin as declaring a set of *protocol endpoints* it provides (functions) and *protocol endpoints* it requires (functions it calls), then the composition becomes a dependency graph. Plugin B depends on Plugin A if B calls functions defined in A. The correct loading order is a topological sort of this dependency graph.
> 
> This is precisely the structure of *linking* in Benton/Wadler's "Linear Logic, Monads and the Lambda Calculus" (LICS 1996, Section 4) -- each module provides and requires linear resources (here: function bindings), and composition requires that every requirement is satisfied. The "shadowing" problem is that two modules providing the same resource create an ambiguity -- which provider wins?
> 
> **Practical answer:** Session types don't help here. What would help is a declaration mechanism: `plugin requires cd` / `plugin provides cd.after`. This is a module system concern. psh's current design (implicit via load order) is the rc/ksh convention. It works. It doesn't compose cleanly when plugins conflict. That's the tradeoff.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 6. RunOutcome Interaction Through `try` Blocks
> 
> **The chain:**
> 
> ```psh
> fn complete.history {
>     let result = menu $history_list
>     match $result {
>         selected $v =&gt; { set /pane/terminal/input $v };
>         cancelled   =&gt; return;
>         err $e      =&gt; echo 'completion failed: '$e &gt;[2]
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> In CBPV terms: `menu` is a computation (`F(Result[A])`). `let result = menu ...` is the CBV binding (`return` in CBPV -- force the computation, bind the value). `match` is coproduct elimination. Each arm is a command (cut).
> 
> **Does the protocol compose through `try`?**
> 
> Consider:
> 
> ```psh
> let outcome = try {
>     let items = `{ get /pane/terminal/history }
>     let sel = menu $items
>     match $sel {
>         selected $v =&gt; return $v;
>         cancelled   =&gt; exit 1;
>         err $e      =&gt; exit 2
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> Here `try` wraps the entire interaction. The protocol is:
> 
> 1. `try` forks (if in value position) or sets `in_try` flag (if block form)
> 2. `get /pane/terminal/history` -- may fail (nonzero status)
> 3. If fail in `try` block: abort to `else` handler with status string
> 4. `menu $items` -- synchronous, returns `Result[T]`
> 5. `match` dispatches
> 6. `return $v` injects value into the value sort
> 
> **Block-form `try`:** The `in_try` flag checks status after each command. `menu` always succeeds (it returns a value, even on cancellation). The match arms use `exit 1` / `exit 2` for non-success paths. Inside `try`, `exit 1` triggers the `else` handler. `return $v` produces the value. The protocol composes correctly because `try` is a *scoped error handler* -- it intercepts nonzero status, not specific value patterns. The `menu` return value flows through `match` normally; only explicit `exit N` triggers `try`'s abort.
> 
> **Value-position `try`:** `let outcome = try { ... }` forks. The child runs the body. If `return $v` is reached, the child writes `$v` to stdout and exits 0. The parent captures stdout + status. `Result[T]` is constructed. The `menu` call happens *in the child process*. If `menu` communicates with the host (pane-terminal), the child must have a connection to the host. This works if the connection is inherited across fork (unix socket fds are inherited). The protocol composition is:
> 
> ```
> parent: fork -&gt; child
> child:  get /pane/... -&gt; menu -&gt; match -&gt; return/exit
> parent: capture stdout + status -&gt; Result[T]
> ```
> 
> The child's `menu` call is a synchronous request to the host. The host doesn't know or care that the requester is a forked child. The protocol composes correctly because:
> 
> 1. Each step is synchronous (no concurrent channels)
> 2. `try` in value position wraps the entire sequence in a fork, making it total (child can crash without affecting parent)
> 3. The `Result[T]` wrapper at the boundary reifies all outcomes as values
> 
> **Potential issue:** If `menu` requires terminal state (cursor position, window dimensions) that the child inherits as a snapshot but the parent may have changed, the child's `menu` could display with stale layout information. This is not a protocol violation -- it's a coherence issue inherent to forked children with shared state. The same issue exists with any command substitution that interacts with the terminal.
> 
> **Verdict on RunOutcome composition:** Sound. The key property is that psh's `try` is the `ErrorT` monad transformer applied at the evaluator level (`specification.md` lines 556-570). It intercepts status codes, not values. `menu` returns a value (always exits 0); `try` doesn't interfere with it. The match on the menu result is ordinary coproduct elimination. Value-position `try` adds a fork boundary, which isolates the protocol but doesn't change its structure.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Summary of Conditions for Soundness
> 
> 1. **Resolve the `.get` readonly/self-update inconsistency** in `syntax.md`. The live re-evaluation pattern requires `.get` to mutate its own variable. The "readonly scope" claim must be qualified: readonly for all variables *except* the discipline's own variable. Document the reentrancy bound (depth 2: `.get` -&gt; `.set`, then guarded).
> 
> 2. **`menu` return type should use three-tag Sum**, not two-tag `Result[T]`, to distinguish cancellation from failure. This affects every plugin that consumes `menu` results.
> 
> 3. **Function disciplines (`.before`/`.after`)** are the principled answer to the `fn cd` collision problem. Without them, plugin composition is load-order-dependent shadowing with no recovery. With them, composition is sequential and protocol-preserving. This is a design decision, not a correctness requirement -- the current design is *safe* (no unsoundness), just *fragile* (plugins silently break each other).
> 
> 4. **Plugin loading order** is outside the scope of session types. A dependency declaration mechanism (even informal, like comments or a manifest) would help but is an engineering concern, not a theoretical one.
> 
> 5. **Value-position `try` + `menu`** requires that the forked child can communicate with the host. This holds if pane-terminal's connection fd is inherited across fork. Verify this is the case when implementing `menu`.</result>
> <usage><total_tokens>63539</total_tokens><tool_uses>26</tool_uses><duration_ms>205834</duration_ms></usage>
> </task-notification>

> <task-notification>

> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/25f44a96-9488-444d-b842-3df1553f5366/tasks/a4d70829c73049e24.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Plan 9: plugin model sufficiency" completed</summary>
> <result>Good. I now have a thorough reading of the full grammar, spec, shell design doc, and all relevant serena memories. Here is my analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Roundtable Response: psh Plugin Architecture
> 
> ### 1. Is this the Plan 9 model?
> 
> Yes. More precisely than you might expect.
> 
> Plan 9 had no plugin system because the namespace *was* the extensibility surface. Let me be specific about what that means mechanically.
> 
> When you wanted to extend acme, you wrote a process that opened `/mnt/acme/new/ctl`, wrote to get a window, then read/wrote files under `/mnt/acme/&lt;id&gt;/body`, `/mnt/acme/&lt;id&gt;/tag`, `/mnt/acme/&lt;id&gt;/event`. The protocol was open/read/write/close on synthetic files. The "plugin API" was the directory layout. No headers, no vtables, no registration.
> 
> The plumber was a "plugin" to the system. It ran as `plumber(4)`, read rules from `$home/lib/plumbing`, and served `/mnt/plumb/send` (write to route a message) plus `/mnt/plumb/&lt;port&gt;` (read to receive messages routed to that port). Applications didn't import a plumber library. They opened a file and read from it.
> 
> rio's windows were directories under `/mnt/wsys/&lt;id&gt;/` with files `cons`, `consctl`, `label`, `mouse`, `snarf`, `text`, `wctl`, `wdir`, `winid`, `window`. A "plugin" that manipulated windows was any program that could read and write files.
> 
> The pattern: **the namespace is the plugin interface, the file protocol is the plugin API, and composition is mounting/binding.**
> 
> psh's discipline+namespace model achieves the same thing through a different mechanism. Let me map it precisely:
> 
> | Plan 9 mechanism | psh mechanism | Isomorphism |
> |---|---|---|
> | Synthetic file (computed on read) | `.get` discipline | Both: side effect on access, value returned from store |
> | `ctl` file (command on write) | `.set` discipline | Both: effectful mutation triggered by write |
> | Mount table (name resolves to server) | `get`/`set` namespace tiers (local/env/pane) | Both: same operation, different backend |
> | `bind -b` / `bind -a` (union directories) | `ref` (nameref aliasing) | Partial: ref is one-to-one, not union |
> | File server (process serving 9P) | Discipline function body | Both: computation triggered by name access |
> 
> **Where it differs.** Three places:
> 
> First, Plan 9 had **union directories**: `bind -b /usr/local/bin /bin` prepended a directory to a union, so `ls /bin` showed files from both. psh has no union equivalent. A nameref (`ref x = target`) is one-to-one aliasing, not many-to-one union. This matters less than you'd think, because psh's namespace isn't a filesystem -- it's a three-tier lookup chain (local, env, pane). The scope chain already provides layered resolution: inner scope shadows outer, which is the `bind REPLACE` (no flag) semantic, not union.
> 
> Second, Plan 9's extensibility was **process-granular**. Each "plugin" was a separate process serving a file tree. psh's disciplines are in-process functions. This means no isolation between "plugins" (two disciplines in the same shell share mutable state), but also no IPC overhead. For a shell, in-process is right. rc's functions were in-process too. The isolation boundary in psh is the fork (`@{ cmds }`), which duplicates the entire context -- same as rc's `rfork(RFNAMEG)`.
> 
> Third, Plan 9's namespace was **discoverable**: `ls /mnt/plumb/` showed you what ports existed. `ls /mnt/acme/` showed you what windows existed. psh's function table isn't exposed as a namespace path. You can't `get /fn/cd` to see the function definition. `whatis cd` gives you the information, but it's a separate mechanism, not part of the namespace. This is a defensible choice -- rc's function namespace was also separate (`whatis fn`, not `cat /env/fn#cd`) -- but it means the "everything is the namespace" claim has a gap. Functions and disciplines live in a parallel namespace that `get`/`set` don't reach.
> 
> **My assessment:** The thesis is substantially correct. Discipline functions *are* the plugin interface (co-Kleisli observation + Kleisli mutation). The three-tier namespace *is* the plugin data layer. Function wrapping *is* composition. You don't need a plugin system because you already have one. The gap is discoverability -- Plan 9 let you `ls` the namespace and see what was available. psh should consider whether `get /fn/` or `get /discipline/` (or similar) should enumerate defined functions and disciplines through the same namespace mechanism. I wouldn't rush this, but it closes the last analogy gap.
> 
> **Confidence: high.** The structural correspondence is real, not superficial.
> 
> ### 2. The composition problem
> 
> Two plugins both define `fn cd`. What happens?
> 
> **Plan 9 precedent is clear and unsatisfying.** rc had no mechanism. Last definition won, period. If you sourced two files that both defined `fn cd`, the second replaced the first. If the second wanted to chain to the old behavior, it had to explicitly call `builtin cd` -- the same thing you're describing.
> 
> Plan 9's `bind` model doesn't apply here because functions weren't file servers in rc. In the Plan 9 kernel, `bind -b /new /old` created a union where `/new` was searched first, falling through to `/old`. `bind -a /new /old` did the reverse. This was for directories, not for function dispatch. Applying it to functions would mean: "look up `cd` in the first function table; if not found, try the second." But function tables don't have "not found" for a specific name -- either the function is defined or it isn't.
> 
> **The three options, assessed:**
> 
> **Option A: rc model (last wins, explicit chaining via `builtin cd`).** This is what rc did. It is simple, predictable, and debuggable. When something goes wrong, `whatis cd` tells you what `cd` is. There is exactly one definition. If a plugin wants to wrap `cd`, it does:
> 
> ```
> fn cd {
>     builtin cd $*
>     # additional behavior
> }
> ```
> 
> The second plugin that tries to wrap `cd` replaces the first wrapper. This is a known problem with a known solution: the second plugin saves the old definition first:
> 
> ```
> let _old_cd = `{ whatis cd }   # this doesn't quite work in rc either
> fn cd {
>     # do my thing
>     builtin cd $*
> }
> ```
> 
> Except this is fragile -- `whatis` returns a human-readable description, not something you can reinvoke. In ksh93, you could use `typeset -f` to get the function body and `eval` it back. In psh, with first-class thunks, you could do:
> 
> ```
> let _prev_cd = $cd    # ERROR: cd is not a thunk, it's a named function
> ```
> 
> Named functions and thunks are different sorts in psh's grammar. You can't capture a named function as a value. This is by design (the sort split), but it means programmatic chaining of named functions is not straightforward.
> 
> **Option B: bind-before/bind-after on the function table.** This would mean the function table supports ordered lookup: multiple definitions of `cd` exist, and resolution searches them in order. This is Plan 9's union directory mechanic applied to function dispatch.
> 
> The problem is: what does "not found" mean for a function? In a union directory, you walk into `/bin`, find that `/usr/local/bin` doesn't have `cd`, fall through to `/usr/bin`, find it there. The walk mechanism has a clear "not found → try next" semantic. For functions, the function either runs or it doesn't. There's no "this function declines to handle this invocation, try the next one."
> 
> You could invent a convention: the function returns a special status meaning "I didn't handle this, try the next definition." This is the middleware/chain-of-responsibility pattern. It works, but it's foreign to shell semantics. No shell has ever done this. It would be novel, which in a shell is suspicious.
> 
> **Option C: Hook lists (multiple handlers fire in order).** This is the Observer pattern applied to function dispatch. Multiple definitions of `fn cd` create a hook list. All fire in sequence.
> 
> This fails immediately: what if two hooks produce different exit statuses? What if one hook does work that the second hook undoes? What if one hook is slow? The composition semantics are unclear, and unclear composition semantics are the opposite of what psh's theoretical foundations promise.
> 
> **My recommendation: Option A (rc model) with one enhancement.**
> 
> Keep last-definition-wins. It's simple, debuggable, and honest. The enhancement: make `builtin cd` genuinely useful for chaining by ensuring the `builtin` keyword bypasses function lookup entirely and reaches the shell's built-in `cd` implementation. This is already in the grammar (`builtin cd` is in the builtins list in syntax.md). But also consider exposing the *previous* function definition under a convention, perhaps `fn.prev`:
> 
> ```
> fn cd {
>     builtin cd $*
>     echo 'now in '$PWD
> }
> ```
> 
> If a second wrapper wants to chain through the first:
> 
> ```
> # This is the user's responsibility, not a framework feature.
> # The convention is: save a reference before overriding.
> let _cd_hook = \() =&gt; { builtin cd $* }  # snapshot current behavior
> fn cd {
>     $_cd_hook $*
>     # additional behavior
> }
> ```
> 
> Wait -- this doesn't work either because the thunk captures `$*` at construction time, not invocation time. The correct pattern with psh's thunk semantics would be:
> 
> ```
> let _cd_hook = \args =&gt; { builtin cd $args }
> fn cd {
>     $_cd_hook $*
>     # my stuff
> }
> ```
> 
> This works within psh's existing grammar. It's explicit, it's the user's responsibility, and it composes because each layer wraps the previous through a captured thunk. The key insight: **the thunk sort is the mechanism for capturing a function as a value**. Named functions are commands (negative). Thunks are values (positive). The ↓→↑ shift (capture the command as a value) is what `let _hook = \args =&gt; { builtin cd $args }` does.
> 
> **Don't build bind-before/bind-after.** Plan 9's union directories worked because the search mechanism was walk(5), which had a clear "not found → try next mount" semantic at the kernel level. Function dispatch has no equivalent. Adding one creates complexity that isn't justified by the use cases.
> 
> **Confidence: high on the recommendation, moderate on the thunk-chaining pattern** (needs validation against the full grammar -- see acid test below).
> 
> ### 3. Syntax highlighting
> 
> This is the hard question, and Plan 9's answer is instructive precisely because Plan 9 chose not to solve it.
> 
> **What Plan 9 did.** acme had no syntax highlighting. It had structural editing: chording, execution of selected text, and tag lines as a command interface. sam had structural regular expressions (`x/pat/ c/replacement/`, addresses) but no highlighting. The 8½/rio terminal emulator had no highlighting at all -- it rendered fixed-width text in Plan 9's bitmap font, period.
> 
> The reason wasn't laziness. Pike's design philosophy was explicit: text is text. You don't need color-coding to understand code if the code is well-structured and the editor gives you structural navigation. This is a defensible position for Plan 9's population of 60 Bell Labs researchers who agreed on coding style. It is not a defensible position for a modern shell that wants adoption.
> 
> **The technical constraint is real.** Syntax highlighting during keystroke processing requires:
> 
> 1. A parser that can handle incomplete/invalid input (the user is mid-keystroke)
> 2. Token-level classification (keyword, string, variable, operator, path, etc.)
> 3. Sub-millisecond latency (must complete before the next keystroke at 60+ WPM)
> 
> psh's recursive descent parser (to be rewritten with `combine`) is designed for complete, valid programs. Incremental parsing of partial input is a fundamentally different problem. Tree-sitter exists to solve this problem, but adding a tree-sitter grammar is a significant dependency.
> 
> **Three options, assessed from Plan 9's perspective:**
> 
> **Option A: Host concern (rendering backend has a parser).** The host (reedline, pane-terminal, or dumb) owns syntax highlighting. psh exposes nothing about its token stream. reedline already supports syntax highlighting via a `Highlighter` trait. pane-terminal could embed a tree-sitter grammar.
> 
> This is Plan 9's model inverted: instead of the shell being ignorant of rendering (Plan 9), the renderer is ignorant of the shell. Both work, but both have the same problem -- the highlighting fidelity depends on how closely the external parser matches the real parser. If psh's grammar evolves, the highlighter lags.
> 
> **Option B: psh concern (expose token stream).** psh provides a `tokenize_partial(input: &amp;str) -&gt; Vec&lt;Token&gt;` function that classifies a partial input string into tokens. The host calls this during keystroke processing. psh owns the grammar knowledge; the host owns the rendering.
> 
> This is the right factoring. It keeps the grammar in one place (the parser) and the rendering in one place (the host). The `tokenize_partial` function is a degraded version of the parser that returns best-effort tokens for incomplete input. It doesn't need to build a full AST -- it just needs to identify token boundaries and types (keyword, string literal, variable reference, operator, comment, path, glob pattern). A lexer, not a parser.
> 
> psh's two-alphabet split (`var_char` / `word_char`) already provides the basis for token boundary detection. The lexer would classify:
> 
> - `#` through end-of-line: comment
> - `'...'`: string literal (handle unmatched quote gracefully)
> - `$VARNAME`: variable reference
> - `$#`, `$"`: count/stringify operators
> - Keywords: `if`, `else`, `for`, etc.
> - Operators: `|`, `&amp;&amp;`, `||`, `&gt;`, `&lt;`, etc.
> - `word_char+` after command position: command name
> - `word_char+` in argument position: argument (possibly path or glob)
> 
> This is ~200 lines. It doesn't need `combine`. It runs at keystroke speed because it's a single-pass lexer with no backtracking.
> 
> **Option C: Skip it (Plan 9 model).** No syntax highlighting. Text is text.
> 
> I would not recommend this for adoption reasons, but I want to flag that it's not as crazy as it sounds. psh's syntax is simple -- simpler than bash, simpler than zsh. rc had no highlighting and was usable. The question is whether psh's audience will accept this tradeoff.
> 
> **My recommendation: Option B.** Expose `tokenize_partial` as part of psh's library API. The host calls it; psh owns the grammar knowledge. This is a library function, not a protocol -- it runs in-process, synchronously, and returns a `Vec&lt;(Range&lt;usize&gt;, TokenKind)&gt;` or similar.
> 
> The key insight from Plan 9: **the rendering is the host's concern, the grammar is the shell's concern.** Plan 9 got this right for content (rio rendered text, the shell produced text). Apply the same principle to highlighting: psh classifies tokens, the host renders them.
> 
> The `menu` builtin you propose follows the same factoring: psh produces structured data (choices, filter criteria), the host renders UI (popup, list, keymap). Highlighting is the same pattern: psh produces structure (token classifications), the host renders color.
> 
> **Confidence: high on the factoring, moderate on effort.** A partial-input lexer for psh's grammar is tractable but needs careful handling of edge cases (unclosed quotes, heredocs, multi-line input).
> 
> ### 4. The ~/.psh/ convention
> 
> **Plan 9 precedent.** `$home/lib/profile` was the per-user rc startup file. That's it. No directory hierarchy. No plugin loading. No dependency declarations. Plan 9's philosophy was that complexity belonged in external tools, not in the shell's configuration mechanism.
> 
> For psh, I'd recommend something similarly minimal:
> 
> ```
> ~/.psh/
>     profile         # sourced on login shell startup (interactive)
>     env             # sourced on every shell startup (before profile)
>     lib/            # user's function libraries, sourced explicitly
> ```
> 
> **Loading order:**
> 
> 1. `/etc/psh/env` (system-wide, for all psh instances)
> 2. `~/.psh/env` (user, for all psh instances -- set PATH, configure namespace)
> 3. `/etc/psh/profile` (system-wide, interactive only)
> 4. `~/.psh/profile` (user, interactive only -- define functions, set prompt)
> 
> This is rc's model (`/rc/bin/rcmain` then `$home/lib/profile`) with the env/profile split from ksh93 (`.kshrc` vs `.profile`).
> 
> **Plugin loading.** There shouldn't be a plugin loading mechanism. A "plugin" is a file in `~/.psh/lib/` that the user sources from their profile:
> 
> ```
> # ~/.psh/profile
> . ~/.psh/lib/git-helpers
> . ~/.psh/lib/pane-shortcuts
> ```
> 
> Explicit sourcing. No auto-discovery. No dependency resolution. If a library depends on another, it sources it:
> 
> ```
> # ~/.psh/lib/git-helpers
> . ~/.psh/lib/path-utils    # I need path_canonicalize
> fn git_root { ... }
> ```
> 
> Double-sourcing is idempotent if the file only defines functions (which it should). If it has side effects, use a guard:
> 
> ```
> # ~/.psh/lib/path-utils
> if ~ $__path_utils_loaded true { return }
> let __path_utils_loaded = true
> fn path_canonicalize { ... }
> ```
> 
> This is the Plan 9 way: explicit, simple, no framework. rc's `/rc/bin/` directory was a collection of scripts. You sourced what you needed. Nothing auto-loaded.
> 
> **Why not auto-discovery.** zsh's plugin managers (oh-my-zsh, zinit, etc.) auto-discover and auto-load plugins from directories. This creates startup latency, ordering dependencies, and a class of bugs where one plugin breaks another. Plan 9 avoided this entirely. psh should too.
> 
> **Confidence: high.** This is the least controversial question. Explicit &gt; implicit for configuration.
> 
> ### 5. The host trait
> 
> This is where the thesis really gets tested.
> 
> **Plan 9's model.** rio served `/dev/cons` (terminal I/O), `/dev/mouse` (pointer), `/dev/draw` (graphics), `/dev/snarf` (clipboard). The shell didn't know it was talking to rio. It read from and wrote to `/dev/cons`. The interface was the file protocol.
> 
> This worked because Plan 9 had per-process namespaces at the kernel level. rio could bind its per-window `/dev/cons` into the child's namespace, shadowing the kernel's `/dev/cons`. The shell saw a file; the file was backed by a file server; the file server was the window manager. 33 lines of C in the child (from the 8½ paper).
> 
> **psh cannot replicate this.** Linux doesn't have per-process namespace mounting without CAP_SYS_ADMIN (the divergence is documented in your Plan 9 divergences tracker). psh can't mount a synthetic `/dev/cons` into its own namespace.
> 
> **Three levels of abstraction, assessed:**
> 
> **Level 1: 9P server as host.** The host is a 9P file server. psh mounts it (via FUSE or 9P library) and talks to files. Reading from `/psh/input` gets keystrokes. Writing to `/psh/output` emits text. Reading from `/psh/menu` after writing a request gets the user's selection.
> 
> This is maximally Plan-9-faithful. It's also maximally complex for what is essentially "the shell asks the terminal to do something." The 9P round-trip for every keystroke adds measurable latency. 9P's read/write protocol doesn't naturally express rich interactive operations like "show a fuzzy-filter list and return the selection." You'd end up encoding the request as a write to a ctl file and reading the result from a data file, which is the `proc(3)` ctl pattern applied to UI. It works but it's clunky.
> 
> **Level 2: Rust trait (what you're proposing).** The host is a Rust trait with methods like `read_line()`, `menu_popup()`, `menu_list()`, `complete()`, `highlight()`. Implementations exist for reedline (standalone terminal), pane-terminal (pane compositor), and dumb (no interactive features).
> 
> This is the pragmatic answer. The trait is the interface contract. Different hosts implement it differently. The shell never touches terminal escape sequences. The trait methods return `Result[T]` values that the shell can match on.
> 
> **Level 3: Hybrid (trait locally, 9P remotely).** The Rust trait is the in-process interface. For remote shells (psh running on a CPU server, displaying on a local terminal), the trait is backed by a 9P or protocol connection to the local host. The trait methods translate to protocol messages.
> 
> This is the Plan 9 `cpu` pattern: compute remotely, interact locally. The terminal exports its devices; the remote shell imports them. In psh's terms: the remote psh's `Host` trait impl speaks protocol to the local pane-terminal.
> 
> **My recommendation: Level 2 (Rust trait) with Level 3 as the remote extension path.**
> 
> The Rust trait is the right abstraction for v1. It's simple, it's testable, it composes with Rust's ownership model. The trait should be designed so that a 9P-backed implementation *could* exist later without changing the trait signature:
> 
> ```rust
> trait Host {
>     fn read_line(&amp;mut self, prompt: &amp;str) -&gt; Result&lt;String&gt;;
>     fn menu(&amp;mut self, request: MenuRequest) -&gt; Result&lt;Val&gt;;
>     fn complete(&amp;mut self, input: &amp;str, cursor: usize) -&gt; Vec&lt;Completion&gt;;
>     fn tokenize_highlight(&amp;self, input: &amp;str) -&gt; Vec&lt;(Range&lt;usize&gt;, TokenKind)&gt;;
>     // ... psh owns this one, not the host
> }
> ```
> 
> Wait -- `tokenize_highlight` shouldn't be on `Host`. That's psh's job (see section 3). The host gets the token classifications and renders them. Let me revise:
> 
> ```rust
> trait Host {
>     fn read_line(&amp;mut self, prompt: &amp;str, highlighter: &amp;dyn Highlighter) -&gt; Result&lt;String&gt;;
>     fn menu(&amp;mut self, request: MenuRequest) -&gt; Result&lt;Val&gt;;
>     fn complete(&amp;mut self, input: &amp;str, cursor: usize) -&gt; Vec&lt;Completion&gt;;
> }
> 
> trait Highlighter {
>     fn highlight(&amp;self, input: &amp;str) -&gt; Vec&lt;(Range&lt;usize&gt;, TokenKind)&gt;;
> }
> ```
> 
> psh implements `Highlighter`. The host calls it during keystroke processing. This is the factoring: psh owns grammar knowledge, host owns rendering.
> 
> **What Plan 9 teaches here is not the mechanism (9P) but the principle: the host provides capabilities (display, input, selection), the shell consumes them. The shell should not know how the host renders a menu -- it should know that it asked for a selection and got one back.** The `menu` builtin returning `Result[T]` is exactly right. The host could be a TUI popup, a pane-terminal dropdown, a rofi-like external process, or a 9P file server. The shell doesn't care.
> 
> **The menu builtin specifically.** This is well-designed from a Plan 9 perspective. It's a builtin that produces a value (positive, CBV). The computation (user interaction) is encapsulated behind the host trait. The shell sees: I called `menu`, I got back a `Result[T]`. The host handles the details. This is exactly how rc interacted with the plumber -- you wrote to `/mnt/plumb/send`, you read from `/mnt/plumb/&lt;port&gt;`. The mechanism was hidden behind file I/O. In psh, the mechanism is hidden behind the `Host` trait.
> 
> **Confidence: high on the trait, moderate on the exact signature** (the `menu` request type needs design work -- what goes into `MenuRequest`?).
> 
> ### 6. The acid test: package manager walkthrough
> 
> Let me walk through a realistic package manager plugin using the full syntax.md grammar.
> 
> **The scenario:** A package manager integration that provides `pkg install`, `pkg search`, `pkg list`, with interactive selection via `menu`.
> 
> ```
> # ~/.psh/lib/pkg
> 
> # Configuration with discipline
> let mut pkg_backend : Str = 'apk'
> fn pkg_backend.set {
>     if ~ $1 apk apt nix {
>         pkg_backend = $1
>     } else {
>         echo 'pkg: unknown backend: '$1 &gt;[2]
>     }
> }
> 
> # Core function
> fn pkg {
>     match $1 {
>         install =&gt; {
>             if ~ $#* 1 {
>                 # No package specified -- interactive search
>                 let query = `{ menu -prompt 'search: ' -filter }
>                 if ~ $query '' =&gt; return
>                 let results = `{ $pkg_backend^'-search' $query }
>                 let selection = `{ echo $results | menu -list }
>                 match $selection {
>                     ok $pkg =&gt; $pkg_backend^'-install' $pkg;
>                     err $e  =&gt; echo 'cancelled' &gt;[2]
>                 }
>             } else {
>                 # Package specified directly
>                 for p in $*(2-) {
>                     $pkg_backend^'-install' $p
>                 }
>             }
>         };
>         search =&gt; {
>             let results = `{ $pkg_backend^'-search' $*(2-) }
>             echo $results
>         };
>         list =&gt; $pkg_backend^'-list';
>         * =&gt; {
>             echo 'usage: pkg install|search|list' &gt;[2]
>             return err 'bad usage'
>         }
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> **Grammar check against syntax.md:**
> 
> 1. `let mut pkg_backend : Str = 'apk'` -- valid: let_binding with mut qualifier and type annotation. Line 59.
> 2. `fn pkg_backend.set { ... }` -- valid: discipline function. Line 98-109.
> 3. `if ~ $1 apk apt nix { ... }` -- valid: `~` as match builtin in command position. Line 491.
> 4. `match $1 { install =&gt; { ... }; search =&gt; { ... }; ... }` -- valid: match_cmd with `=&gt;` arms separated by `;`. Lines 129-160. `install` and `search` are glob patterns matching literal strings.
> 5. `let query = `{ menu -prompt 'search: ' -filter }` -- valid: command substitution in let RHS, type inferred as Str. Line 538.
> 6. `if ~ $query '' =&gt; return` -- valid: single-line body with `=&gt;`. Line 137-138. `return` without a value produces Unit.
> 7. `$pkg_backend^'-search' $query` -- valid: free caret between variable and quoted literal. Line 428-438. Produces `apk-search`.
> 8. `echo $results | menu -list` -- valid: pipeline. `menu` is a builtin (proposed).
> 9. `match $selection { ok $pkg =&gt; ...; err $e =&gt; ... }` -- **[planned]**: structural match on Sum values. Lines 229-238. `ok $pkg` is a structural arm (tag + `$`-prefixed binding).
> 10. `$*(2-)` -- **NOT in the grammar.** Syntax.md defines `$x(n)` for single-element indexing (line 355, 522) but does not define range indexing. This is a ksh93 feature (`${@:2}`) that rc didn't have. You'd need `for` iteration or a list-slice builtin.
> 11. `return err 'bad usage'` -- **[planned]**: `return` with Sum construction (tag `err` + payload `'bad usage'`). Lines 240-266.
> 
> **Missing primitives identified:**
> 
> **A. List slicing.** `$*(2-)` (all arguments from the second onward) is not in the grammar. rc had no range indexing. ksh93 had `${@:2}` and `${x[@]:start:length}`. Without this, the workaround is:
> 
> ```
> # Shift-equivalent
> fn pkg {
>     let subcmd = $1
>     shift          # Not in the grammar either!
>     match $subcmd { ... }
> }
> ```
> 
> `shift` is listed in PLAN.md as a missing rc feature (line 220: `- [ ] **shift** — shift positional parameters`). This is a genuine gap: without `shift` or list slicing, functions that consume positional parameters need `$2`, `$3`, etc. explicitly, which doesn't compose.
> 
> **B. The `menu` builtin itself.** The thesis proposes `menu` as a builtin. The grammar doesn't define it, but that's fine -- builtins are extensible. The question is what `menu` returns. If `menu -list` returns a `Result[T]` (the user selected something or cancelled), the caller needs structural matching on the result. The example uses `match $selection { ok $pkg =&gt; ...; err $e =&gt; ... }`. This works if `menu` returns `Sum("ok", Str(selection))` on success and `Sum("err", Str(reason))` on cancellation. That's consistent with `try`'s Result[T] convention.
> 
> But `menu` isn't a computation that succeeds or fails in the Unix sense (exit code 0 or nonzero). It's a user interaction that may be cancelled. Should cancellation be `err` (failed computation) or a different tag (e.g., `cancelled`)? The Sum type's open tag namespace supports either. I'd lean toward `ok $selection` / `none ()` (a `Maybe[T]`, not a `Result[T]`), because cancellation isn't an error -- it's the absence of a selection.
> 
> ```
> let selection = menu -list $results
> match $selection {
>     ok $pkg   =&gt; $pkg_backend^'-install' $pkg;
>     none $()  =&gt; echo 'cancelled'
> }
> ```
> 
> **C. `echo $results | menu -list` pipeline interaction.** The example pipes `echo $results` into `menu -list`. This means `menu` reads choices from stdin. This is good -- it composes with pipelines. But `menu` also needs to interact with the user (display a list, accept keystrokes). If `menu`'s stdin is the pipe, how does it get user input? Answer: through the `Host` trait. `menu` reads choices from stdin (pipeline), delegates interactive rendering to the host, and returns the selection to stdout. This is the correct factoring: data flows through pipes, interaction flows through the host.
> 
> **D. Piping into `menu`.** When `menu` receives choices via stdin pipe, it must collect them before showing UI. This means `menu` blocks until stdin is closed (the pipeline stage before it finishes). For large result sets, this could be slow. A streaming alternative (show results as they arrive, update the fuzzy filter incrementally) would require the host to support streaming. This is a v2 concern, but worth noting: the `Host::menu()` API should accept an iterator or channel, not just a complete list, to enable future streaming.
> 
> **Verdict on the acid test:** The example works with the full syntax.md grammar, with two gaps:
> 
> 1. **List slicing / `shift`** -- acknowledged in PLAN.md, needs implementation.
> 2. **`menu` return type** -- should be `Maybe[T]` (cancellation is absence, not error), which is in the type system (line 799: `Maybe[T] = T | Unit, implied tags: ok, none`).
> 
> The three-mechanism thesis holds. The package manager example composes through discipline functions (backend configuration), namespace (pane integration if backend is pane-aware), and function wrapping (the `fn pkg` function is the plugin entry point). No plugin framework needed.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Summary of recommendations
> 
> | Question | Recommendation | Confidence |
> |---|---|---|
> | Is this Plan 9? | Yes, with a discoverability gap (functions aren't in the namespace) | High |
> | Composition | rc model (last wins), thunk capture for chaining, no union semantics | High |
> | Syntax highlighting | `tokenize_partial` as psh library function, host renders | High |
> | ~/.psh/ convention | Minimal: env + profile + lib/, explicit sourcing, no auto-discovery | High |
> | Host trait | Rust trait (Level 2), design for future 9P backing (Level 3) | High |
> | Acid test | Works with two gaps: list slicing and menu return type (Maybe[T]) | High |
> 
> The thesis is sound. Three mechanisms, no plugin system. Plan 9 would approve.</result>
