### `2026-04-07T05:38:17.934Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>af2f4d6c9befa75ff</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01ShzBmpXc72fPJ4kCrjotrg</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/25f44a96-9488-444d-b842-3df1553f5366/tasks/af2f4d6c9befa75ff.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Optics: plugin data flow analysis" completed</summary>
> <result>Now I have a thorough understanding of both the reference material and psh's architecture. Let me compose the analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Optics Analysis: psh Plugin Architecture
> 
> ### 1. menu as AffineTraversal with effectful witness
> 
> The classification of `menu` as AffineTraversal is **almost right but not quite precise enough**. Let me walk through the formal structure.
> 
> An AffineTraversal in Clarke et al. (Definition 3.15, `arxivmain.tex` line 1530) is:
> 
> &gt; **Affine**_{⊗}((A, B), (S, T)) := C(S, T + A ⊗ [B, T])
> 
> The concrete signature decomposes into `access : S → T + A × (B → T)` — the whole either has no focus (yielding T directly) or decomposes into a focus A together with a reconstruction function B → T.
> 
> For `menu`:
> - S = List[T] (the input list of candidates)
> - A = T (the selected element, if any)
> - The cancellation path yields T directly? No — cancellation doesn't produce a T; it produces *nothing useful*. The return type is Result[T], i.e., Sum("ok", T) | Sum("err", ExitCode).
> 
> This is the critical distinction. A pure AffineTraversal's failure path `S → T` means "the whole is returned unchanged." Menu's failure path doesn't return the list unchanged — it returns an error value from a *different type*. The reconstruction is not `List[T] → List[T]` (put the selection back); it's `List[T] → Result[T]` (wrap the outcome).
> 
> What `menu` actually is: a **Prism** composed with an effectful witness, not an AffineTraversal. The decomposition is:
> 
> ```
> menu : List[T] →_IO (T + Err)
> ```
> 
> This is `S →_M (A + E)` where M is the IO action. In Clarke et al.'s framework, the right optic is a **mixed optic** (Definition 3.2, line 870):
> 
> &gt; Optic_{⊛_L, ⊛_R}((A,B), (S,T)) := ∫^{M ∈ M} C(S, ⊛_L(M, A)) ⊗ D(⊛_R(M, B), T)
> 
> For menu, the monoidal action on the left (decomposition) side is the Kleisli embedding of the IO monad: ⊛_L(M, A) = M ⋊ A where ⋊ is the strong action from the monad's strength (line 1069). The right (reconstruction) action is trivial — the selected T passes through as-is. This makes menu a **MonadicPrism**: the Kleisli-category analog of a Prism where `match` is effectful.
> 
> Concretely:
> - `match : List[T] →_IO (T + Err)` — effectful, partial decomposition
> - `build : T → Result[T]` — pure injection (`ok $v`)
> 
> The profunctor encoding would be:
> 
> ```
> type MenuP s t a b = forall p. (Cocartesian p, MonadicAction IO p) =&gt; p a b -&gt; p s t
> ```
> 
> The `Optic_{×, M}` notation from the memo conflates the monoidal action with the product structure. The IO action is not on the product side (that would be a MonadicLens); it's on the coproduct/match side (MonadicPrism). The asymmetry matters: the effect lives in the decomposition, not the reconstruction.
> 
> **Verdict:** menu is a MonadicPrism (Kleisli-Prism with IO effects in the match), not an AffineTraversal. The "at most one" cardinality is correct, but the failure mode is coproduct injection (Prism), not context-preserving pass-through (Affine). The effectful witness (user interaction) lives in the Kleisli category of IO.
> 
> ### 2. Type flow through menu
> 
> The type preservation story composes correctly, but the composition path is different from what the memo describes.
> 
> The memo says: "List[T] → T (Traversal selects one) → Result[T] (wrap in Sum)." This decomposition is misleading — Traversal selects *all* foci, not one. Selecting exactly one from a list is not a Traversal; it's a non-deterministic choice resolved by an external oracle (the user).
> 
> The correct decomposition:
> 
> 1. **Fold**: `List[T] → [T]` — enumerate candidates. This is the *read-only* portion: present the list. In Clarke et al. (Definition 3.21, line 1754), Fold((A, \*), (S, \*)) = V(S, L(A)) where L is the free monoid functor.
> 
> 2. **MonadicPrism** (the IO-effectful selection): `[T] →_IO (T + Err)` — render, collect user input, resolve to one element or failure.
> 
> 3. **Review** (injection): `T → Result[T]` — wrap in the ok branch of the coproduct. This is the degenerate optic from Definition 3.19 (line 1739): a morphism C(B, T).
> 
> The element type T is preserved throughout because:
> - The Fold is parameterized by T
> - The MonadicPrism's focus type is T
> - The Review injects T into Result[T]
> 
> This means the plugin's `match` arms on `$result.ok` know the element type. The composition is type-correct. But it's a composition of *mixed* optics (Fold is read-only / degenerate on the covariant side; the MonadicPrism mixes Kleisli and pure categories). Clarke et al.'s framework handles this — mixed optics compose when the intermediate categories match (Theorem 4.10 in the paper). Here: the Fold's output category (Set) matches the MonadicPrism's input category, and the MonadicPrism's output matches the Review's input. The composition is valid.
> 
> **The key observation for psh:** The type parameter T flows through the menu pipeline via parametric polymorphism, not via optic composition laws. The optics describe the *access pattern* (read many, select one, inject into sum); the type preservation is a consequence of the pipeline being natural in T. This is the right thing — the optics classify the structure, parametricity preserves the types.
> 
> ### 3. Status line as Traversal over Getters
> 
> `fn status.git`, `fn status.path`, `fn status.jobs` — each is a Getter (Definition 3.19, line 1739): a morphism S → A where S is the shell state and A is a string segment. The host enumerates `fn status.*` and applies each.
> 
> The classification as "Traversal over a product of Getters" is **structurally correct but requires careful unpacking**.
> 
> A Getter is a degenerate mixed optic: Optic with the trivial action on the covariant side, yielding C(S, A). It's read-only — there is no B → T reconstruction path. In the profunctor encoding:
> 
> ```
> type GetterP s a = forall p. (Bicontravariant p) =&gt; p a a -&gt; p s s
> ```
> 
> (or equivalently, using `Forget`: `forall p. Forget r =&gt; p a (Forget r a) -&gt; p s (Forget r s)`)
> 
> The host's enumeration pattern — find all functions matching `status.*` — is a **dynamic product**. At any moment, the set of registered `fn status.*` functions forms a product ∏_i A_i (where each A_i = Str). The host constructs the status line by projecting each component.
> 
> The right classification depends on whether the host needs:
> - **Independent reads (product of Getters):** Each `fn status.git`, `fn status.path` etc. is an independent Getter. The host applies them independently and concatenates. This is not a Traversal — it's a product in the category of Getters. No Traversal is needed because the host doesn't need to "traverse" a uniform container; it's accessing named components of a product.
> - **Uniform enumeration (Fold):** If the host treats `fn status.*` as an opaque collection and maps a uniform operation (render-to-string) over all of them, that's a Fold: S → L(A) where L is the list functor.
> 
> The glob-based discovery (`fn status.*`) adds a dynamic dimension that pure optics don't capture. The set of status components is determined at runtime by what plugins have registered. This is **not** a fixed product (which would give a Lens for each component) but a **runtime-determined family of Getters**.
> 
> In optic terms: the glob pattern `status.*` is itself a **Fold** over the function namespace — it enumerates all matching names. For each name, the function is a Getter. So the full pattern is:
> 
> ```
> Fold (over namespace, filtered by glob) ∘ Getter (each function)
> ```
> 
> Since Getter embeds into Fold (every Getter is a single-element Fold), the composition collapses to a **Fold** — which is the correct classification.
> 
> **Does the enumeration interact with the optic hierarchy?** Yes, in a subtle way. The glob pattern is a *runtime* filter on the namespace, which means the set of foci is not statically known. This is fine for Fold (its cardinality is unspecified), but it means the host cannot statically verify that all expected status segments are present. This is a limitation of the plugin model, not of the optics — the optics correctly classify the access pattern as "read zero or more string values from the namespace."
> 
> **Verdict:** Fold, not Traversal. The key distinction: Traversal includes a reconstruction path (fill the container back); status line segments are read-only. Fold is the degenerate read-only case of Traversal, which is exactly what the status line needs.
> 
> ### 4. Completion as Fold + user-resolved AffineTraversal
> 
> The memo's classification: plugin provides a list (Fold over candidates), host renders and user selects (resolving the AffineTraversal), result flows back.
> 
> This is **structurally identical to menu** (question 1) and the same correction applies. The precise decomposition:
> 
> 1. **Fold**: Plugin enumerates completion candidates. `candidates : (Context, Prefix) → [Candidate]`. Read-only — the plugin cannot "put back" into the input context.
> 
> 2. **MonadicPrism**: Host renders the candidate list, user selects or cancels. `select : [Candidate] →_IO (Candidate + Cancelled)`. The IO effect is the interactive rendering/selection.
> 
> 3. **Injection**: The selected candidate flows into the input buffer. This is a **Setter** or **Review** depending on whether the insertion modifies existing state (Setter: `(A → B) → (S → T)`) or constructs new state (Review: `B → T`).
> 
> The completion case has one additional wrinkle compared to menu: **the candidate list may be refined incrementally** (as the user types more characters). This means the Fold is re-evaluated repeatedly, narrowing the focus set. Each re-evaluation is a fresh Fold application; the narrowing is external (driven by the evolving prefix), not internal to the optic.
> 
> In Clarke et al.'s framework, this iterative refinement is a *sequence* of Fold applications, not a single optic. The optic classifies each step; the sequencing is control flow.
> 
> **Verdict:** Fold followed by MonadicPrism, same as menu. The "AffineTraversal" classification in the memo is imprecise in the same way — the failure path is coproduct (Prism), not context-preserving (Affine), and the effect is in the match, not the update. The iterative refinement is outside the optic.
> 
> ### 5. Cross-plugin accessor composition
> 
> The accessor chain `$cursor.ok.line` decomposes as:
> 
> 1. `$cursor` — variable lookup (Getter from namespace Γ)
> 2. `.ok` — Prism preview into the `ok` branch of a Sum (Result[T] → T + Err)
> 3. `.line` — Lens projection into a named field of a product (T → Int, assuming T has a `line` field)
> 
> Composition: Getter ∘ Prism ∘ Lens. In the profunctor encoding, composition is function composition (as the "Don't Fear" reference explains at line 323 of `ProfunctorOptics.md`):
> 
> ```
> type AccessorP s a = forall p. (Cartesian p, Cocartesian p) =&gt; p a a -&gt; p s s
> ```
> 
> The Getter constrains to read-only (no reconstruction). The Prism adds Cocartesian. The Lens adds Cartesian. The composition inherits all constraints plus the Getter's read-only restriction, yielding an **AffineFold** — a read-only AffineTraversal. This is the correct optic for a chain that may fail (the Prism's `.ok` can miss the `err` branch) and projects a field (the Lens's `.line`).
> 
> **Cross-plugin boundary:** When `cursor` is a live variable from another plugin (refreshed via `.get` discipline), `$cursor` fires the discipline. The discipline is a MonadicLens (as specification.md section "Discipline functions as MonadicLens" establishes). The accessor chain then becomes:
> 
> ```
> MonadicLens (discipline access) ∘ Prism (.ok) ∘ Lens (.line)
> ```
> 
> This is a MonadicAffineFold — a read-only affine access with monadic effects in the get.
> 
> **Do the optic laws hold across plugin boundaries?**
> 
> For the Prism (.ok): The Prism laws require that `match ∘ build = id` (ReviewMatch) and `build ∘ match = id` when the focus exists (MatchReview). These hold for Sum decomposition — constructing `ok(v)` and then matching `.ok` yields `v`, and matching `.ok` on `ok(v)` and rebuilding gives `ok(v)`. The Prism laws are properties of the Sum type constructor, not of the plugin boundary. They hold.
> 
> For the Lens (.line): PutGet and GetPut hold for Tuple field access — these are structural, not dependent on the plugin boundary.
> 
> For the MonadicLens (discipline): PutGet and GetPut hold for tiers 1-2 (specification.md line 463). For tier 3 (remote/computed), PutGet degrades — the discipline fires a fresh query, and the result may differ from what was set. The specification is explicit about this (line 466-471).
> 
> **The composition preserves laws when all components satisfy their individual laws.** The weak point is the discipline at tier 3, and the specification already identifies this honestly. The accessor chain `$cursor.ok.line` is lawful if `cursor` is tier 1-2 (local). If `cursor` is tier 3 (remote/computed), the accessor chain is an AffineFold in practice — read-only, no round-trip guarantees — and this is correct because the affine resource discipline at tier 3 already restricts contraction.
> 
> **Verdict:** The composition is sound. The optic laws hold because each link in the chain satisfies its own laws, and composition of lawful optics is lawful (this is the category structure from Clarke et al. Theorem 4.10). The tier-3 degradation is already accounted for by the specification's structural-rule analysis.
> 
> ### 6. Where optics break
> 
> Three patterns that strain the optic hierarchy:
> 
> **(a) Syntax highlighting is not an optic at all.** Syntax highlighting takes a string (the input line) and produces an annotated string (with color/style metadata). This is a **morphism** — a plain function `Str → AnnotatedStr`. It's not an optic because there's no focus/whole decomposition; it's a transformation of the entire input. The closest optic would be a Setter (`(A → B) → (S → T)`, applying a transformation), but syntax highlighting doesn't decompose into "access a part, transform it, put it back." It's a *global* analysis (parsing) followed by *global* annotation.
> 
> If forced into the optic framework: highlighting is a **coend over token positions** — for each token, there's a Getter (read the token) and a Setter (annotate it), but the annotation depends on *context* (what precedes and follows the token, the parse state). Context-dependent annotation breaks the optic assumption of independent foci. A Traversal assumes each focus can be independently transformed; syntax highlighting requires that the transformation of token n depends on tokens 0..n-1 (the parser state). This is a **stateful traversal** or **Moore machine** — beyond the standard optic hierarchy.
> 
> Clarke et al.'s Kaleidoscope (Definition 3.17, line 1578) comes closest: it operates on `Vec n A → B` — applying a function that sees all foci simultaneously. But a Kaleidoscope's foci are uniform (all the same type), while syntax tokens carry heterogeneous metadata (keyword vs. string vs. operator).
> 
> **The honest answer:** Syntax highlighting requires a parser (stateful transducer), not an optic. The plugin provides a `fn highlight { }` function that takes the input line and returns annotations. This is a plain function, and the plugin API should treat it as such — no optic structure to preserve.
> 
> **(b) Undo/redo requires a temporal optic.** If a plugin needs to access the undo history, it needs something like a **Lens into a zipper** — the current state plus a list of past states. The standard optic hierarchy has no built-in notion of temporal navigation. This could be modeled as a Lens into a product `(past: List[State], present: State, future: List[State])`, where undo is a Lens that focuses on the present while shifting the zipper. But the coherence between past/present/future is an *invariant* that the Lens laws don't capture — arbitrary put into `present` without updating `past` breaks the undo stack. This requires a **lawful Lens with an invariant**, which is an optic constrained by a predicate on the whole. Not standard, but representable as a sub-optic.
> 
> **(c) Layout/geometry queries.** A plugin asking "what is the bounding rectangle of pane X?" needs a Getter over geometry state that the pane compositor owns. This is straightforward (Getter into the namespace). But a plugin asking "move pane X next to pane Y" needs a **dependent Setter** — the target position depends on Y's current position. The optic for the move is:
> 
> ```
> set : (PaneId × PaneId) → (Geometry → Geometry)
> ```
> 
> This is a Setter where the transformation depends on external state (Y's geometry). In the profunctor encoding, this dependency makes it a **Glass** (Definition 3.14, line 1494 in Clarke et al.): `C(S × [[S, A], B], T)` — the update can observe the whole S to decide how to modify it. Glass = Optic_{×[,], ×[,]} is the optic for "update that can read the whole." If psh plugins need geometry-aware layout operations, Glass is the optic that would model them. It exists in the hierarchy but is exotic — Val's type constructors (Tuple, Sum, List, Thunk) don't naturally give rise to Glass.
> 
> **Summary of where optics break:**
> 
> | Pattern | Why optics don't fit | What to use instead |
> |---|---|---|
> | Syntax highlighting | Context-dependent, global analysis | Plain function (stateful transducer) |
> | Undo/redo | Temporal invariant not captured by Lens laws | Constrained Lens into zipper (with invariant) |
> | Geometry-dependent layout | Update depends on reading the whole | Glass optic (exists but exotic) |
> | Event streams (keypress sequences) | Temporal, stateful, not decomposable into focus/whole | Session types or stream transducers |
> 
> The first and last are cleanly outside the optic hierarchy. The middle two are within it but require optics (Glass, constrained Lens) that Val's type constructors don't provide out of the box. This is the boundary: Val gives you products (Lens), coproducts (Prism), sequences (Traversal/Fold), and their compositions (AffineTraversal). Anything requiring the update to read the whole (Glass), or requiring stateful/temporal coherence, exceeds what the standard type constructors can model through accessors alone.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Net assessment:** The memo's optic classifications are directionally right but systematically conflate AffineTraversal with Prism-in-Kleisli. The pattern across menu, completion, and interactive selection is MonadicPrism (effectful partial decomposition), not AffineTraversal (context-preserving partial access). The status line is Fold, not Traversal (read-only). The accessor composition story is solid and the laws hold as documented. The genuine breaks are syntax highlighting (needs a parser, not an optic) and geometry-dependent updates (needs Glass, which Val doesn't provide).</result>

> </task-notification>

### `2026-04-07T05:38:50.098Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>ac38cf72a87d9a514</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_017KUvmqozRSVPprwJaMNeob</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/25f44a96-9488-444d-b842-3df1553f5366/tasks/ac38cf72a87d9a514.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Be: plugin model + host interface" completed</summary>
> <result>Now I have all the source material. Let me compose the roundtable response.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 1. Be's Add-On Model vs psh's "No Framework" Approach
> 
> Be had three distinct add-on architectures, and they're worth understanding individually because they represent different points on the explicit-implicit spectrum.
> 
> **Translators** were the most structured. Looking at `BTranslatorRoster` in Haiku (`headers/os/translation/TranslatorRoster.h`), the roster was a registry with explicit capability discovery: `GetInputFormats()`, `GetOutputFormats()`, `GetAllTranslators()`, `Identify()`. A translator implemented a C++ interface. The roster loaded shared libraries from well-known directories, called the C entry point (`make_translator()`), and built a catalogue of what each translator could do. This was capability-based discovery -- "I can convert B_TRANSLATOR_BITMAP to B_PNG_FORMAT" -- and the system could match source format to available translators without the source knowing anything about PNG.
> 
> **Media add-ons** were similar but richer. From `BMediaAddOn` in Haiku (`headers/os/media/MediaAddOn.h`): `CountFlavors()`, `GetFlavorAt()` returning `flavor_info` with `in_formats`/`out_formats`, `kinds` (producer, consumer, filter). The `make_media_addon()` C entry point loaded from a directory scan. The media_server built a registry of dormant nodes by querying flavors. Capability discovery was explicit and typed: what formats in, what formats out, what kinds.
> 
> **Input server add-ons** (`headers/os/add-ons/input_server/InputServerDevice.h`) were the simplest: `Start()`, `Stop()`, `Control()`, `RegisterDevices()` with `input_device_ref` structs declaring device type. More like a driver interface.
> 
> The common pattern across all three: **the add-on declares its capabilities to a registry, and the registry mediates discovery**. The app that needs a translator doesn't scan the filesystem -- it asks the roster.
> 
> Now, psh's proposal. "Plugins are just scripts that define functions and disciplines." No explicit interface. No registry. No capability declaration. Is this sufficient?
> 
> I think the answer is: **it's sufficient for a different kind of composition, and it's the right choice for a shell**.
> 
> Here's why. Be's add-on architectures solved a specific problem: runtime loading of compiled code with no source-level visibility. The roster/registry bridged the information gap between separately compiled binaries. A translator and its consumer never shared a source tree; the `translation_format` structs were the contract.
> 
> psh scripts exist in a unified namespace. When you source a plugin, its functions enter the function table directly. Discovery isn't "what can you do?" -- it's "what names exist?" `whatis` already answers this. If a plugin defines `fn git.prompt { }` and `fn git.complete { }`, the function table IS the capability registry. The naming convention (namespace prefix + discipline suffix) IS the interface contract.
> 
> The question is: **what does psh lose by not having explicit capability declaration?**
> 
> Two things that mattered in Be's model:
> 
> 1. **Format negotiation.** BTranslatorRoster could find the best path from format A to format B by chaining translators. This required structured capability metadata, not just "I exist." psh plugins don't need this -- they're not doing format conversion. A plugin either provides `git.prompt` or it doesn't.
> 
> 2. **Lazy loading.** Be loaded add-ons from disk on demand. The roster knew about a translator's capabilities without loading it. psh's equivalent would be: scan plugin directories, read a header comment or a manifest function, register names without sourcing the whole file. This is worth considering -- a `fn plugin.manifest { }` convention that returns a list of provided functions, without sourcing the full plugin.
> 
> My recommendation: **psh should have a manifest convention, not a manifest framework**. Something like:
> 
> ```
> # In git.psh:
> fn git.provides {
>     return (git.prompt git.complete git.menu)
> }
> 
> # Everything else
> fn git.prompt { ... }
> fn git.complete { ... }
> fn git.menu { ... }
> ```
> 
> The shell can source just the `*.provides` functions for lazy discovery, then source the full plugin on demand. This is the translator roster pattern translated to shell conventions: capability discovery through a naming convention, not a type system.
> 
> ## 2. The Menu Protocol and BMessage Analogy
> 
> Your analogy is precise and it holds.
> 
> In Be's model, a BMenu was a BView (a BHandler) attached to a BLooper (the BWindow's thread). When you clicked a menu item, it sent a BMessage with the item's command code to a target BHandler. The flow was: user gesture -&gt; view interprets gesture -&gt; BMessage sent -&gt; handler receives. The menu itself was rendered by the BView, but the semantic content (which items, which labels, which commands) was data.
> 
> `BAlert::Go()` is the clearest illustration. From `headers/os/interface/Alert.h`: it blocks the caller on a semaphore (`fAlertSem`), presents UI, and returns `int32` (the button index). Synchronous request-response. The alert is the structured request ("here's a question, here are the choices"); the return value is the response. `Go(BInvoker*)` is the async variant -- fire and forget, callback via messenger.
> 
> psh's `menu` as a structured request that the host renders and returns `Result[T]` maps exactly onto `BAlert::Go()`. The shell says "here is a selection to present"; the host renders it however it can; the shell gets back a value.
> 
> What Be's experience teaches about the request format:
> 
> **BMenu items carried: label, command code, shortcut key, target handler, enabled/disabled, marked/unmarked.** That's it. The semantic content was minimal -- enough for the renderer to present and the handler to interpret. psh's menu request should be similarly minimal:
> 
> ```
> # The structured request
> menu --title 'Branch' --items (main develop feature/auth) --default main
> 
> # Richer variant with metadata
> menu --title 'Action' --items (
>     (label 'Commit' key 'c')
>     (label 'Push' key 'p' enabled false)
>     (label 'Pull' key 'l')
> )
> ```
> 
> The key lesson from BMenu: **the menu data model and the menu rendering are completely separate**. BMenu had `B_ITEMS_IN_ROW`, `B_ITEMS_IN_COLUMN`, `B_ITEMS_IN_MATRIX` as layout hints, not rendering commands. The data said "here are items and their relationships." The view said "I'll draw them as a popup" or "I'll draw them as a menubar."
> 
> For psh, this means the menu request should carry items + metadata + layout hints. The host decides whether that becomes a TUI popup, a Wayland popup surface, a dmenu-style bar, or a numbered list on a dumb terminal. The shell never says "draw a popup at (x,y)." It says "the user needs to choose from these items."
> 
> **BMessage's field model is relevant here.** BMessage was a typed key-value bag. `AddString("label", ...)`, `AddInt32("shortcut", ...)`. This self-describing format meant any handler could inspect any message. psh's menu request should be a `Val` -- probably a List of Sum values or Tuples, something the shell's own type system can represent. No special wire format. The menu request IS a Val.
> 
> ## 3. The Composition Problem and Handler Chains
> 
> Let me show you exactly what BHandler chains look like in Haiku's implementation. From `src/kits/app/Handler.cpp`, lines 267-282:
> 
> ```cpp
> if (fNextHandler) {
>     BHandler* target = Looper()-&gt;_HandlerFilter(message, fNextHandler);
>     if (target != NULL &amp;&amp; target != this) {
>         target-&gt;MessageReceived(message);
>     }
> } else if (message-&gt;what != B_MESSAGE_NOT_UNDERSTOOD
>     &amp;&amp; (message-&gt;WasDropped() || message-&gt;HasSpecifiers())) {
>     printf("BHandler %s: MessageReceived() couldn't understand the message:\n", Name());
>     message-&gt;PrintToStream();
>     message-&gt;SendReply(B_MESSAGE_NOT_UNDERSTOOD);
> }
> ```
> 
> The chain: if I don't handle it, call `fNextHandler-&gt;MessageReceived()`. If nobody handles it, send `B_MESSAGE_NOT_UNDERSTOOD`. Each handler either handles the message and returns, or falls through to `_inherited::MessageReceived()` (which walks to `fNextHandler`).
> 
> This is the advice chain pattern. It worked well for UI composition -- a custom view handles its own messages, passes unknown ones to its parent class, which passes them to the window, which passes them to the app. The chain was usually implicit (inheritance hierarchy matched the handler chain).
> 
> **Should psh's function table support handler chains?**
> 
> I'd argue: **not as an explicit mechanism, but the composition naturally emerges from discipline functions and the scope chain.**
> 
> Here's why. In Be, the handler chain solved a specific problem: multiple independent pieces of code needed to respond to the same message type in the same looper. A BWindow handled `B_ZOOM`, `B_MINIMIZE`, and a bunch of system messages. Your custom window subclass handled app-specific messages. The chain let both coexist.
> 
> In psh, the equivalent is: multiple plugins want to contribute to the same hook. Two plugins both want to provide completion, or both want to contribute to the prompt. The Be solution (chain) has a problem: **ordering is implicit and fragile**. Who goes first? `SetNextHandler()` was manual. In practice, the chain was usually just the class hierarchy, not arbitrary composition.
> 
> psh has a better primitive: **the scope chain already IS a handler chain**. Inner scopes shadow outer scopes. A plugin that sources another plugin and wraps its functions is doing chain composition:
> 
> ```
> # base-git.psh provides:
> fn git.prompt { echo '[' `{ git branch --show-current } ']' }
> 
> # enhanced-git.psh wraps it:
> . base-git.psh
> let _base_git_prompt = $fn:git.prompt    # save reference
> fn git.prompt {
>     $_base_git_prompt                     # call base
>     if ~ `{ git status --short } '?*' {
>         echo ' [dirty]'
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> This is explicit, visible, and debuggable. The Be handler chain was implicit and often surprising -- developers would forget that `_inherited::MessageReceived()` existed, swallowing messages that should have propagated.
> 
> For multi-plugin composition where multiple plugins contribute to the same list (like prompt segments), use a convention:
> 
> ```
> fn prompt.git { ... }
> fn prompt.venv { ... }
> fn prompt.k8s { ... }
> 
> # The main prompt function iterates over prompt.* by convention
> fn prompt {
>     for segment in `{ whatis -n prompt.* } {
>         $segment
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> This is discovery via naming convention (like `GetSupportedSuites()` but without the framework). `whatis -n prompt.*` is the query. The function table is the registry.
> 
> ## 4. The Host Trait
> 
> BApplication was the entry point, but the rendering contract was spread across BWindow, BView, and BScreen. The right analogy for the host trait is more like the app_server contract -- the set of operations the shell can request from its display environment.
> 
> Here's my proposal, grounded in Be's patterns but translated to psh's structured-request model.
> 
> ```rust
> /// What the host can render. Each method takes structured data
> /// and returns a result. The shell never describes how to render —
> /// only what to present.
> ///
> /// Analogy: this is psh's equivalent of the app_server protocol.
> /// The shell is the client; the host is the server.
> pub trait Host {
>     /// Present a selection and return the chosen item.
>     /// Be equivalent: BAlert::Go() + BMenu::Track()
>     ///
>     /// `style` is a hint, not a command. A terminal host may ignore
>     /// PopupAt and render as a numbered list. A Wayland host may
>     /// render a popup surface.
>     fn menu(&amp;mut self, req: MenuRequest) -&gt; Result&lt;Val, HostError&gt;;
> 
>     /// Request a line of input with a prompt.
>     /// Be equivalent: BTextControl in a modal window.
>     ///
>     /// The prompt is structured (segments with semantic roles),
>     /// not a flat string. The host renders segments using its
>     /// own style mapping.
>     fn prompt(&amp;mut self, req: PromptRequest) -&gt; Result&lt;String, HostError&gt;;
> 
>     /// Update the status line (persistent, not scrolling).
>     /// Be equivalent: BStatusBar / BStringView in a toolbar.
>     ///
>     /// `None` clears the status line. Not all hosts support this;
>     /// unsupported hosts return `Ok(())` and discard.
>     fn status(&amp;mut self, segments: Option&lt;&amp;[StyledSegment]&gt;) -&gt; Result&lt;(), HostError&gt;;
> 
>     /// Request tab completion candidates for a partial input.
>     /// Be equivalent: none directly — Be's Terminal didn't have
>     /// shell-aware completion. This is the one place psh goes
>     /// beyond what Be offered.
>     ///
>     /// The host may present candidates however it likes: inline,
>     /// popup, pager. The shell provides candidates; the host
>     /// presents them and returns the selection.
>     fn complete(&amp;mut self, req: CompleteRequest) -&gt; Result&lt;Option&lt;String&gt;, HostError&gt;;
> 
>     /// Query host capabilities.
>     /// Be equivalent: BScreen::GetMode(), get_mouse_type(),
>     /// ui_color() — querying the display environment.
>     ///
>     /// The shell asks "what can you do?" once at startup and
>     /// adapts. No progressive enhancement at runtime — the
>     /// capability set is fixed for the session.
>     fn capabilities(&amp;self) -&gt; HostCapabilities;
> 
>     /// Deliver a notification (non-blocking, no response expected).
>     /// Be equivalent: BHandler::SendNotices().
>     fn notify(&amp;mut self, note: Notification) -&gt; Result&lt;(), HostError&gt;;
> }
> ```
> 
> The supporting types:
> 
> ```rust
> pub struct MenuRequest {
>     pub title: Option&lt;String&gt;,
>     pub items: Vec&lt;MenuItem&gt;,
>     pub style: MenuStyle,
>     pub default: Option&lt;usize&gt;,
> }
> 
> pub struct MenuItem {
>     pub label: String,
>     pub value: Val,           // what the shell gets back
>     pub key: Option&lt;char&gt;,    // shortcut hint
>     pub enabled: bool,
>     pub group: Option&lt;String&gt;, // for radio-group behavior
> }
> 
> /// Layout hints, not commands. The host may ignore these.
> pub enum MenuStyle {
>     Popup,          // BPopUpMenu equivalent
>     Vertical,       // BMenu in column mode
>     Inline,         // present in-line (like fzf)
>     Transient,      // auto-dismiss (tooltip-like)
> }
> 
> pub struct PromptRequest {
>     pub segments: Vec&lt;StyledSegment&gt;,
>     pub history_key: Option&lt;String&gt;,  // which history file/namespace
>     pub multiline: bool,
> }
> 
> pub struct StyledSegment {
>     pub text: String,
>     pub role: SemanticRole,
> }
> 
> pub struct CompleteRequest {
>     pub input: String,
>     pub cursor: usize,
>     pub candidates: Vec&lt;Candidate&gt;,
> }
> 
> pub struct Candidate {
>     pub display: String,
>     pub replacement: String,
>     pub kind: CandidateKind,
> }
> 
> pub enum CandidateKind {
>     Command, Path, Variable, Function, Argument,
> }
> 
> pub struct HostCapabilities {
>     pub color: ColorSupport,
>     pub popup_menu: bool,
>     pub inline_completion: bool,
>     pub status_line: bool,
>     pub unicode: bool,
>     pub mouse: bool,
>     pub sixel: bool,     // image support
> }
> 
> pub enum ColorSupport {
>     None,
>     Ansi16,
>     Ansi256,
>     TrueColor,
> }
> 
> pub struct Notification {
>     pub severity: Severity,
>     pub message: String,
> }
> 
> pub enum Severity { Info, Warning, Error }
> ```
> 
> The critical design point from Be: **`HostCapabilities` is queried once, not negotiated per-request**. Be's `ui_color()` was a global function -- you called it and got the current system color. You didn't negotiate color support per window. The capability set defines the session, and the shell adapts its requests accordingly. A shell running in a dumb terminal won't emit menu requests with `PopupAt` style; it'll use `Vertical` and accept that the host renders as a numbered list.
> 
> ## 5. Syntax Highlighting
> 
> Pe (the BeOS editor) and BTextView's `text_run_array` are the right reference. From `headers/os/interface/TextView.h`:
> 
> ```c
> struct text_run {
>     int32       offset;
>     BFont       font;
>     rgb_color   color;
> };
> 
> struct text_run_array {
>     int32       count;
>     text_run    runs[1];
> };
> ```
> 
> A text run is: "from this offset forward, use this font and color." The editor computed runs and set them with `SetRunArray()`. The text view applied them during rendering. Pe ran a lexer on the visible buffer and generated run arrays. The lexer and the parser were independent -- Pe's lexer was fast, line-local, and wrong in edge cases (unclosed multi-line strings etc.), but it was responsive.
> 
> **Should psh do the same?** Yes, with a refinement.
> 
> The shell has two parsing layers:
> 1. The full parser (in `parse.rs`) -- correct, complete, slower.
> 2. A highlighting lexer -- fast, line-local, approximate.
> 
> For interactive highlighting, you want the lexer. The full parser is for execution. The highlighting lexer needs to handle:
> - Keywords (`if`, `else`, `for`, `while`, `match`, `try`, `fn`, `let`, `return`)
> - Variable references (`$x`, `$#x`, `$"x`, `${...}`)
> - Command substitution (`` `{...} ``)
> - Quoted strings (`'...'`)
> - Comments (`# ...`)
> - Operators (`|`, `&amp;&amp;`, `||`, `&gt;`, `&lt;`, `^`)
> - Redirections
> 
> And it needs to run on the visible line only, in under a millisecond.
> 
> The host trait already supports this. The shell runs its lexer, produces `StyledSegment` spans with `SemanticRole` tags, and the host maps roles to colors. The shell never sends color codes -- it sends semantic markup. This is the `text_run_array` pattern with semantic roles instead of raw `rgb_color`.
> 
> ```rust
> // The shell produces these:
> pub enum SemanticRole {
>     Keyword, Builtin, Command, Argument,
>     Variable, Substitution, String, Comment,
>     Operator, Redirect, Path, Number,
>     Error,     // unmatched quote, bad syntax
>     Plain,
> }
> 
> // The host maps them:
> impl TerminalHost {
>     fn role_to_color(&amp;self, role: SemanticRole) -&gt; Color {
>         match role {
>             SemanticRole::Keyword =&gt; self.theme.keyword,
>             SemanticRole::Error =&gt; self.theme.error,
>             // ...
>         }
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> **The key lesson from Pe:** the highlighting lexer is NOT the parser. Don't try to reuse the full parser for highlighting. Pe had a separate, simpler lexer that was wrong about some edge cases but always fast. psh should have a `highlight.rs` that is a separate, simpler state machine.
> 
> ## 6. Theme System: Semantic Colors
> 
> Be's `ui_color()` (`headers/os/interface/InterfaceDefs.h`) is exactly the right model. Look at the `color_which` enum:
> 
> ```c
> B_PANEL_BACKGROUND_COLOR, B_PANEL_TEXT_COLOR,
> B_DOCUMENT_BACKGROUND_COLOR, B_DOCUMENT_TEXT_COLOR,
> B_CONTROL_BACKGROUND_COLOR, B_CONTROL_TEXT_COLOR,
> B_MENU_BACKGROUND_COLOR, B_MENU_ITEM_TEXT_COLOR,
> B_SUCCESS_COLOR, B_FAILURE_COLOR,
> ```
> 
> These are semantic, not literal. An app never said "make this red." It said "make this `B_FAILURE_COLOR`." The system (or the user's theme) decided what failure looked like. When BeOS added dark mode support (Haiku extended this), all apps that used `ui_color()` worked automatically.
> 
> **psh should absolutely have named semantic colors.** This is the host's concern in terms of mapping, but the shell's concern in terms of vocabulary. The shell knows what something IS (an error, a path, a keyword); the host knows what that LOOKS LIKE.
> 
> ```rust
> /// Semantic color names. The shell uses these; the host resolves them.
> /// This is psh's `color_which`.
> pub enum SemanticColor {
>     // Shell-specific
>     Error,
>     Warning,
>     Success,
>     Path,
>     Keyword,
>     Builtin,
>     Variable,
>     String,
>     Comment,
>     Operator,
> 
>     // UI-level (from Be's ui_color)
>     PromptPrimary,
>     PromptSecondary,
>     StatusText,
>     StatusBackground,
>     SelectionBackground,
>     SelectionText,
>     MatchHighlight,      // for completion matching
> }
> ```
> 
> The host implements the mapping. A terminal host might have:
> 
> ```
> fn resolve_color(&amp;self, sc: SemanticColor) -&gt; TermColor {
>     match sc {
>         SemanticColor::Error =&gt; TermColor::Red,
>         SemanticColor::Path =&gt; TermColor::Cyan,
>         SemanticColor::Keyword =&gt; TermColor::Magenta,
>         // ...
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> A Wayland host reads the pane theme attributes. A headless host returns no-ops.
> 
> **The lesson from Be:** semantic colors worked because they were universal. Every app used them. The moment one app hardcoded "red" for errors, that app broke in any non-default theme. psh should make `SemanticRole`/`SemanticColor` the ONLY way to specify colors in the shell-to-host protocol. No escape hatch for raw ANSI codes in the protocol layer. Plugins that want custom colors must define new semantic names, and the host decides whether to honor them or fall back.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Summary Assessment
> 
> The thesis holds: **psh's plugin API is discipline functions + namespace + function conventions + menu protocol. No plugin framework.** This is the right call for a shell.
> 
> Where Be's experience strengthens this:
> - **Manifest convention** (from BTranslatorRoster's capability discovery): `fn plugin.provides` for lazy loading.
> - **Menu as structured request** (from BAlert/BMenu): the shell sends data, the host renders.
> - **Scope chain as handler chain** (from BHandler/fNextHandler): composition by wrapping, not by framework.
> - **Semantic colors** (from `ui_color()`): the shell names meaning, the host names appearance.
> - **Separate highlighting lexer** (from Pe/BTextView): fast and approximate beats slow and correct.
> 
> Where Be's experience warns:
> - The handler chain was often opaque. psh's explicit wrapping is better.
> - BMessage's self-describing format was expensive. psh's `Val` is already the right currency -- don't add a second serialization.
> - Be's add-on loading was directory-scan based and had no versioning. If psh ever adds lazy plugin loading, think about version compatibility from day one.</result>

> </task-notification>

### `2026-04-07T05:49:29.763Z`

> lets stabilize the API more before we do the next part. ok one thing before anything else, can we save a fleshed out spec reflecting the synthesis i'm citing below in docs/api.md?
> 
> --- Plugin Architecture Roundtable Synthesis
> 
> The thesis holds
> 
> All four agents confirm: discipline functions + namespace + function
> conventions + menu = the plugin API. No framework needed. The mechanisms
> already exist.
> 
> Consensus across all four agents
> 
> menu return type: Plan 9 says Maybe[T], Session type says three-tag Sum
> (selected | cancelled | err), Be's BAlert::Go() analogy supports the
> structured approach. The three-tag model is more expressive and costs nothing
> — Sum tags are free.
> 
> Syntax highlighting: All agents agree — separate fast lexer (highlight.rs),
> NOT the full parser. Be cites Pe's text_run_array pattern. Plan 9 proposes
> tokenize_partial() as a library function. Optics confirms highlighting is not
> an optic (it's a stateful transducer). The shell produces semantic roles; the
> host maps to colors.
> 
> Composition: rc model (last wins) with thunk capture for explicit chaining.
> No handler chains, no union semantics. Plan 9 and Be both endorse explicit
> wrapping over implicit chains.
> 
> Host trait: Be provides the concrete Rust trait design — menu(), prompt(),
> status(), complete(), capabilities(), notify(). All methods take structured
> data, return structured results. The shell never touches rendering.
> Capability query happens once at startup.
> 
> Semantic colors: Be's ui_color() pattern — the shell names meaning
> (SemanticRole::Error, SemanticRole::Path), the host names appearance. No raw
> ANSI codes in the protocol.
> 
> Key findings per agent
> 
> Plan 9:
> 
> - The model IS the Plan 9 model (namespace = extensibility surface)
>   - Discoverability gap: functions aren't in the namespace (can't get /fn/cd)
>   - ~/.psh/ convention: minimal — env + profile + lib/, explicit sourcing, no
>     auto-discovery
>   - Host trait should be designed for future 9P backing (remote shells)
>   - Acid test works with two gaps: list slicing/shift, menu return type
> 
>   Be:
>   - Manifest convention from BTranslatorRoster: fn plugin.provides for lazy
>     discovery
>   - Menu request IS a Val — don't add a second serialization format
>   - MenuItem should carry: label, value, key hint, enabled flag, group
>   - HostCapabilities queried once (like BScreen::GetMode()), not negotiated
>     per-request
>   - Separate highlight.rs lexer, semantic roles → StyledSegment, host maps to
>     colors
> 
>   Session type:
>   - .get readonly spec is inconsistent with live re-evaluation pattern — needs
>     resolution
>   - Function disciplines (.before/.after) are the principled answer to fn cd
>     collision
>   - menu protocol is degenerate session type (single request-response) —
>     correct and sufficient
>   - Value-position try + menu requires forked child to inherit host connection
>     fd
>   - Plugin loading is sequential context extension — session types don't help
>     here, it's a module system concern
> 
>   Optics:
>   - menu is MonadicPrism (effectful decomposition), not AffineTraversal
>   - Status line is Fold (read-only), not Traversal
>   - Cross-plugin accessor composition is sound — laws hold per-link
>   - Optics break at: syntax highlighting (stateful transducer), event streams
>     (session types), geometry-dependent layout (Glass optic — exotic)
> 
>   Three design decisions for you
>   1. menu return type: Maybe[T] (two-tag: ok $selection | none ()) or three-tag
>      (selected $v | cancelled () | err $e)? The three-tag version lets plugins
>      distinguish "user said no" from "host unavailable."
> 
>   2. Function disciplines (.before/.after): Should psh support fn cd.before { }
>      and fn cd.after { } for composable hooks? Session type says yes (the
>      protocols compose). Plan 9 and Be say no (explicit wrapping via thunks is
>      sufficient). This is expressiveness vs simplicity.
> 
>   3. .get readonly resolution: The live re-evaluation pattern (fn cursor.get {
>      cursor = ... }) contradicts the readonly scope claim. Should .get be
>      readonly-except-for-own-variable? The session type agent says yes, with
>      depth-2 reentrancy bound documented.
> 
>   These don't block the rebuild — they're post-core decisions. But worth noting
>   for when the core lands.

### `2026-04-07T05:51:42.090Z`

> can we apply the pane's technical writing standard across our api spec and other docs?

> 
> # psh Formal Audit
> 
> ## 1. Fidelity to Theoretical References
> 
> ### 1.1 CBPV Polarity Discipline: RunOutcome as F(A)
> 
> **What's correct.**
> - `RunOutcome { Status, Value }` faithfully implements the CBPV F(A) computation type (specification.md "The four sorts," roundtable decision #2). `return` produces `RunOutcome::Value(val)` and propagation is immediate: `run_cmds` at exec.rs:1053 checks `matches!(last, RunOutcome::Value(_))` after every command and short-circuits. This correctly models `return : A -&gt; F(A)`.
> - `return` without a value produces `Unit` (exec.rs:1041), matching the spec.
> 
> **What's wrong or missing.**
> - **`return` is not context-checked.** Specification.md (syntax.md:244, "Value-producing blocks") says `return` injects a value "from the command sort into the value sort." The spec implies `return` outside a value context (not in a block used as RHS of `let`, `match` arm, etc.) should be an error or at least semantically suspect. Currently, `return 42` at the top level produces `RunOutcome::Value(Val::Str("42"))` silently, and `Shell::run()` at exec.rs:1521 calls `.status()` which maps `Value(_)` to `Status::ok()` -- silently discarding the value. The spec says this is CBPV's `return`, but there's no diagnostic when a returned value goes nowhere. **This isn't a spec violation per se** (the spec says "Without `return`, the body produces `Unit`"), but spec sections on "value-producing blocks" imply the user should know when `return` is meaningful.
> - **Confidence:** High on correctness of the RunOutcome mechanism. Medium on the context-checking gap -- the spec is ambiguous about whether top-level `return` is an error.
> 
> ### 1.2 Duploid Structure: CBV/CBN Split
> 
> **What's correct.**
> - `eval_word` (exec.rs:176-261) completes all word evaluation before the consuming command runs. This is faithful to the spec's CBV subcategory claim (specification.md "CBV/CBN split").
> - Pipeline execution (`run_pipeline`, exec.rs:674-730) forks all stages concurrently with `pipe(2)` endpoints -- demand-driven co-Kleisli as specified.
> - The fork boundary correctly separates the two polarities: word expansion completes before `execvp` runs within each forked process.
> 
> **What's wrong or missing.**
> - Nothing structural. The CBV/CBN separation is correctly maintained at the call-graph level as spec claims.
> - **Confidence:** High.
> 
> ### 1.3 Profunctor Redirections
> 
> **What's correct.**
> - `Expr::Redirect(Box&lt;Expr&gt;, RedirectOp)` nests inner-to-outer (ast.rs:138). The parser at parse.rs:684-697 wraps each redirect around the accumulated expression, so `cmd &gt;file &gt;[2=1]` produces `Redirect(Redirect(Simple(cmd), Output{1}), Dup{2,1})`. The outermost redirect is the rightmost in source -- evaluating from outer to inner produces left-to-right evaluation. This matches specification.md "Wrapped redirections make evaluation order structural."
> - `run_redirect` (exec.rs:733-826) uses save/restore: `dup(fd)` to save, `dup2` to redirect, run inner, `dup2(saved, fd)` to restore, `close(saved)`. This is the correct profunctor bracket.
> 
> **What's wrong or missing.**
> - **dup save failure unchecked.** Every `dup()` call in `run_redirect` can return -1 (e.g., if fd is not open). exec.rs:753 does `let saved = unsafe { libc::dup(*fd as i32) }` without checking the return value. If `saved == -1`, the subsequent `dup2(saved, *fd)` in the restore path will silently fail, leaking the redirect. Same pattern at lines 769, 801, 810, 818. This is not a theoretical fidelity issue but breaks the save/restore roundtrip (PutGet lens law) when the initial fd is invalid.
> - **Confidence:** High.
> 
> ### 1.4 MonadicLens Discipline Functions
> 
> **What's correct.**
> - `.get` fires as a notification hook in a readonly scope (exec.rs:270-275). `push_readonly_scope()` creates a `Scope { readonly: true }`, and `set_value()` (env.rs:268-270) rejects mutations when `is_readonly()`. The value returned is always the stored value, not the body's output -- exec.rs:277 calls `self.env.get_value(name)` *after* the discipline runs, reading the stored value directly. This is spec-correct: ".get body cannot influence what $x evaluates to" (specification.md "Discipline functions as MonadicLens").
> - `.set` fires with `$1` bound to the new value (exec.rs:1168), in a pushed scope (exec.rs:1167), and the reentrancy guard (`active_disciplines: HashSet&lt;String&gt;`) prevents infinite recursion (exec.rs:1164).
> - The reentrancy guard is explicit tracking via `HashSet`, not a hidden flag bit -- spec-faithful (specification.md "Discipline function reentrancy").
> 
> **What's wrong or missing.**
> - **`.get` can actually mutate through the readonly scope.** The readonly check in `set_value` (env.rs:268-270) only checks the *topmost* scope. But `set_value` walks the scope chain from top to bottom (env.rs:277-294). If the topmost scope is readonly, it rejects the mutation. However, the `.get` body runs commands that call `run_cmd` -&gt; `run_binding` -&gt; `set_value`. The check `self.is_readonly()` at env.rs:269 looks at `self.scopes.last()`, which IS the readonly scope. So this is actually correct -- mutations are rejected. The readonly scope pushes on top, and `is_readonly()` checks that top scope. Confirmed correct.
> - **`.get` discipline can have side effects to stderr/stdout.** The spec allows this ("can observe, log, trigger side effects (to stderr)"). No issue.
> - **The `.get` discipline can modify global functions.** The readonly scope prevents variable mutation, but `define_fn` goes to a separate `HashMap&lt;String, Vec&lt;Command&gt;&gt;` not governed by the readonly flag. A `.get` body containing `fn something { body }` would succeed. This is technically a scope leak violating the "readonly" contract, though it's a subtle edge case.
> - **Confidence:** High on the main path. Medium on the function-definition leak.
> 
> ### 1.5 ExitCode Polarity
> 
> **What's correct.**
> - `ExitCode(0)` is true, `Int(0)` is false (value.rs:104 vs 101). The truthiness inversion is correctly implemented and tested.
> - `Val::infer()` never produces `ExitCode` (value.rs:232-262). `ExitCode` has no literal constructor. The doc comment says "Enters Val only through `try`" and `infer()` has no ExitCode path.
> - `validate_type` at env.rs:412 rejects `ExitCode` as `Int` (the `_ =&gt; Err(...)` catch-all at line 503). `ExitCode` matches `TypeAnnotation::ExitCode` at line 412 and widens to `Str` at line 489, but there is no `ExitCode -&gt; Int` path. Coercion is correctly prohibited.
> 
> **What's wrong or missing.**
> - **ExitCode can currently be constructed outside `try`** via `Val::Sum("err", Box::new(Val::ExitCode(n)))` from the `Value::Tagged` path. If a user writes `let x = err 42` in a context where tagged values work, the `eval_value` for `Tagged` (exec.rs:299-302) creates `Sum("err", ...)`, but the payload `42` will be inferred as `Int(42)` not `ExitCode(42)`. So there's actually no way for a user to create `ExitCode` directly from syntax. Confirmed correct.
> - **`try` in value position is not yet implemented.** exec.rs:115 has `#[allow(dead_code)]` on `CaptureResult::exit_code`, noting "Used by try-in-value (Phase 8)." The shared `capture_subprocess` primitive exists, but `try` in value position (producing `Result[T] = Sum("ok", T) | Sum("err", ExitCode(n))`) is not wired up. This is a known planned item -- syntax.md marks it `[planned]` in part.
> - **Confidence:** High.
> 
> ### 1.6 Sum/Prism Semantics (match)
> 
> **What's correct.**
> - Match with structural arms correctly decomposes Sum values (exec.rs:988-1000). When `Val::Sum(val_tag, payload)` matches a `Pattern::Structural { tag, binding }` where `val_tag == tag`, it pushes scope, binds the payload, runs the arm body, and pops scope. The binding is properly scoped.
> - Unmatched Sum produces `Status::err("no match")` (exec.rs:1008-1011) -- the roundtable decision #1 (Unit + nonzero status).
> 
> **What's wrong or missing.**
> - **Unmatched returns `RunOutcome::Status(Status::err("no match"))` but NOT `Val::Unit`.** The roundtable decision #1 says "produce `Val::Unit` and set nonzero `$status`" and "In value context, return Unit." The current code returns `Status::err("no match")`, which is correct for command context. But if `match` appears in value context (e.g., `let x = match ... { ... }`), the `RunOutcome::Status(...)` propagates up and `run()` converts to `Status` via `.status()`. There's no mechanism to return `Val::Unit` in value context on no-match -- a `return` is needed in each arm. This is consistent but worth noting: the spec's "produce `Val::Unit`" for value context relies on the user writing `return` in arm bodies. The `match` command itself doesn't auto-return Unit.
> - **Match evaluates against `match_str` (the `.to_string()` of the value) for glob/literal arms** (exec.rs:980-981). For `Val::Sum`, `to_string()` returns the payload only (tag stripped, per value.rs:327). This means `match $result { ok =&gt; echo yes }` where `$result = Sum("ok", Int(42))` will try to match `"42"` against the literal `"ok"`, which will fail. The user must use structural arms `ok $v =&gt;` for Sum values. This is spec-correct ("ok =&gt; { } (no $binding) is a glob match on the literal string 'ok', not structural decomposition" -- syntax.md:163-164). But it's a subtle user footgun with no diagnostic.
> - **Confidence:** High.
> 
> ### 1.7 Thunk/U(C) Semantics
> 
> **What's correct.**
> - Thunks are positive: `Val::Thunk` derives `Clone` and `PartialEq` (value.rs:28). They are inert data until forced.
> - Forcing pushes scope, binds named params, runs body, pops scope (exec.rs:522-548).
> - Thunk display is diagnostic `fn(params){...}` (value.rs:329-332).
> - Thunk truthiness is always true (value.rs:108).
> 
> **What's WRONG.**
> - **Capture-by-value is not implemented.** This is the most significant theoretical fidelity gap. Roundtable decision #4 explicitly specifies: "Lambdas capture free variables by value at construction time -- enables currying." The syntax.md section (lines 743-758) describes the mechanism in detail. But `eval_value` for `Value::Lambda` at exec.rs:292-298 creates the thunk with `captures: vec![]` and the comment says "No closure capture -- dynamic name resolution at force time (roundtable decision #4, CBPV convention)." **The comment misattributes the decision.** Decision #4 explicitly says capture-by-value for lambdas. This means currying (`let add = \x =&gt; \y =&gt; expr $x + $y; let add3 = \`{$add 3}; $add3 5`) will not work -- the inner thunk won't have `x` captured.
> - **Thunk forces do not restore captures.** The `force_thunk` method (exec.rs:522-548) binds named parameters and positional parameters, but never restores `captures` into the scope. Even if captures were populated, they'd be ignored. The spec says "At force time, captures are restored into the scope before the body runs" (specification.md, "Products, coproducts, and the optic hierarchy").
> - **Confidence:** Very high. This is a clear implementation gap against an unambiguous spec requirement.
> 
> ### 1.8 `\` vs `fn` Sort Split
> 
> **What's correct.**
> - The parser correctly produces `Value::Lambda` for `\params =&gt; body` (parse.rs:275-343), which is only reachable from `value_()` (parse.rs:346-361), which is only called from `let_binding`, `for_cmd`, `assignment`, and `match_arm` -- all value-position contexts. `fn` is parsed by `fn_def()` (parse.rs:1253-1270) producing `Command::Bind(Binding::Fn {...})`, only in command position.
> - The LL(1) disambiguation works: `line_cont()` (parse.rs:65-67) uses `attempt(ch('\\').with(ch('\n')))`, and `lambda()` starts with `ch('\\')` -- `\\` + newline is consumed in trivia before lambda is attempted in value position. In command position, `\\` is never tried (lambda is only in `value_()`).
> 
> **What's wrong or missing.**
> - **`fn` in value position is not explicitly rejected** -- it simply doesn't parse there because the grammar doesn't attempt it. If someone writes `let f = fn { body }`, the `value_()` parser will try list, lambda, and word. `fn` would be parsed as `Word::Literal("fn")` (since `f`, `n` are word chars), producing `Val::Str("fn")`. This is silent degradation, not an error. The spec says "let f = fn { body } is illegal" -- but the implementation makes it a valid assignment of the string "fn" to f.
> - **Confidence:** High. The parser behavior is deterministic but the error mode is not what the spec prescribes.
> 
> ### 1.9 `try` as ⊕ -&gt; ⅋ Converter
> 
> **What's correct.**
> - `try` block checks status after each command via `run_cmds_try` (exec.rs:1069-1082). Nonzero Status triggers `TryOutcome::Aborted`.
> - Boolean contexts are exempt: `in_boolean_context` flag is set for `if` conditions (exec.rs:936-937), `while` conditions (exec.rs:961-963), `&amp;&amp;` LHS (exec.rs:381-383), `||` LHS (exec.rs:392-394), and `!` (exec.rs:403-405).
> - Nesting works: `try_depth` is incremented/decremented (exec.rs:1018-1020), and `run_cmds` checks `self.try_depth &gt; 0` (exec.rs:1057).
> - `TryOutcome { Completed, Aborted }` matches the session type agent's recommendation from decision #2.
> 
> **What's wrong or missing.**
> - **Boolean context exemption leaks through nesting.** The `in_boolean_context` flag is a single bool on `Shell`. If `try { if cond { false } }` runs, `cond` sets `in_boolean_context = true`, runs the condition, restores. But the `false` inside the then-body runs with `in_boolean_context = false` (correctly). However, if `if cond &amp;&amp; false { ... }` runs inside try, the `&amp;&amp;` sets `in_boolean_context = true` for its LHS, but the RHS (`false`) runs with the restored value. The RHS of `&amp;&amp;` is NOT in a boolean context per the spec (only the LHS is exempt). Let me verify: exec.rs:383-388 saves `in_boolean_context`, sets true, runs LHS, restores, then runs RHS with the original saved value. If the saved value was `false` (not in boolean context) and we're in a try, the RHS failure would abort. This is correct -- only the LHS is exempt. Good.
> - **`run_cmds` try-abort check happens even outside `run_cmds_try`.** At exec.rs:1057-1063, `run_cmds` checks `self.try_depth &gt; 0` and aborts on failure. This is the mechanism for try-abort propagation -- commands within a try block that are run by `run_cmds` (not `run_cmds_try`) will still abort. But this means `run_cmds` returns a `RunOutcome::Status(failure)` which gets propagated up to `run_cmds_try` as a regular return, not through the `TryOutcome::Aborted` path. Actually, looking more carefully: `run_cmds_try` calls `run_cmd`, and `run_cmd` for `Command::Exec` calls `run_expr` which calls `run_simple` etc. These don't go through `run_cmds` -- they return Status directly. The `run_cmds_try` loop checks each `run_cmd` result at line 1074-1079. The `try_depth` check in `run_cmds` is for commands nested deeper (e.g., a block inside try that uses `run_cmds`). This is actually correct -- both levels check.
> - **Confidence:** High.
> 
> ### 1.10 `capture_subprocess` as Shared Primitive
> 
> **What's correct.**
> - `capture_subprocess` (exec.rs:313-371) returns `CaptureResult { stdout, exit_code }`.
> - Command substitution (exec.rs:200-211) projects `stdout` only, discarding `exit_code`. Status is not propagated.
> - The `exit_code` field has `#[allow(dead_code)]` -- it's available for try-in-value but not yet consumed.
> 
> **What's wrong or missing.**
> - **try-in-value position is not implemented.** The primitive is shared but only one consumer exists. This is a known gap (Phase 8 per the comment).
> - **Confidence:** High.
> 
> 
> ## 2. Test Robustness
> 
> ### 2.1 Coverage Analysis
> 
> **value.rs (48 tests): GOOD**
> - Truthiness: all 10 variants covered, including the critical ExitCode(0)=true vs Int(0)=false distinction. Tuple/Sum always-true.
> - Display: all variants covered.
> - Inference: Int, Bool, Path, Str, leading-zero rejection, empty string, ExitCode never inferred.
> - Concat: pairwise, broadcast left/right, mismatch, empty, typed coercion, ExitCode coercion, Tuple, Sum.
> - Count: scalars, list, tuple, sum.
> - Indexing: 1-based list, scalar self-index, tuple via both methods, ExitCode self-index, Sum self-index.
> - iter_elements: all cases.
> - From impls: all.
> - **Missing:** Path truthiness for empty Path (construct `Val::Path(PathBuf::from(""))` -- would this violate the "always non-empty by construction" claim? The Rust type allows it). Thunk truthiness test. Thunk display test. Thunk concat test (should coerce to display string). Negative leading-zero test for negative numbers (`-042`).
> 
> **env.rs (18 tests): ADEQUATE**
> - Scope resolution, new var in current scope, pid is set, discipline lookup, readonly scope rejects mutation, readonly var rejects mutation, nameref read/write/chain/depth-limit.
> - Type validation: ExitCode, ExitCode widens to Str, Tuple componentwise, Tuple length/element mismatch, Union matches any branch, Result ok/err/wrong-tag, Maybe ok/none/wrong-tag, Var error defaults None.
> - **Missing:** Immutable variable reassignment rejection test (a `let` without `mut`, then trying to reassign). Narrowing coercion tests (Str-&gt;Int parse success and failure). `let_value` creates in current scope (doesn't walk up). Type validation for List[T] with element mismatch. Interaction between `let` + readonly scope.
> 
> **parse.rs (46 tests): GOOD structure, but gaps**
> - L1: var_char/word_char predicates, empty program, whitespace, comment, line continuation.
> - L2: literal, quoted, quoted escape, var ref (simple, count, stringify, indexed, brace), tilde bare/path.
> - L3: free carets (var+literal, var+dot, explicit, quoted+var).
> - L4: pipeline (2 and 3 stage), and/or/not/background/block/subshell, all redirect forms (output, append, input, fd-specific, dup, close, here-string, read-write), coprocess, heredoc expanding/literal.
> - L5: if/else/else-if, for, while, match glob/structural/multiline, try/try-with-else, fn/fn-discipline/fn-arrow, let/let-mut-export/let-typed/let-result-type, ref, return/return-bare, assignment/assignment-list, lambda (single/multi/nullary/braced).
> - L6: multi-command newline/semicolons/mixed/trailing, command-sub, nested blocks.
> - **Missing:** Process substitution parse test (`&lt;{cmd}`). `~` in tilde path with deeper paths (`~/a/b/c`). Lambda with line continuation before `=&gt;`. Negative parse tests (the `parse_err` function exists but is `#[allow(dead_code)]` -- never called). Heredoc within pipeline. Multiple redirects on same command. `fn` in value position (should silently degrade to string, but no test documents this). Tagged value parsing test (`Value::Tagged`).
> 
> **exec.rs (28 tests): THIN**
> - Status/RunOutcome: basic constructors.
> - Simple commands: echo, true, false.
> - Variables: assignment+expansion, let inference (int, bool, quoted stays str), assignment walks scope.
> - Control flow: if true/false-else, for loop, while false.
> - Functions: def+call, with args.
> - Match: literal, no-match, glob.
> - Try: success, abort-to-else.
> - Return: produces value, bare produces Unit.
> - Lambda: creates thunk.
> - Tilde match: success, failure.
> - Discipline: set fires.
> - Redirections: output creates file.
> - Nameref: resolves.
> - Arrow body: if, fn.
> - And/Or/Not: basic.
> - Pipeline: basic.
> - Whatis: basic.
> - List: assignment, count.
> - **Missing critical tests:**
>   - Match structural arm decomposition (Sum value with tag match + binding)
>   - Match with wildcard `*` arm
>   - Try abort prevents subsequent commands (the spec's core claim)
>   - Try boolean context exemption (`try { if false { } }` should not abort)
>   - Try nesting (inner catches before outer)
>   - Thunk forcing with named params (not just "creates thunk")
>   - Capture-by-value (or absence thereof -- document the current behavior)
>   - Discipline `.get` fires on `$x` access
>   - Discipline reentrancy guard prevents recursion
>   - Readonly scope in `.get` discipline prevents mutation
>   - Redirect save/restore (fd is restored after redirect completes)
>   - Multiple redirect composition (inner-to-outer evaluation)
>   - For loop with typed values
>   - While loop runs body multiple times
>   - Block expression returns last status
>   - Subshell isolation (child mutations don't affect parent)
>   - Command substitution captures stdout
>   - Process substitution produces /dev/fd path
>   - Coprocess write then read
>   - Heredoc expansion of $var
>   - Heredoc literal (no expansion)
>   - Here-string
>   - `return` propagation stops subsequent commands
>   - ExitCode→Int coercion prohibition (runtime)
>   - `let` immutability enforcement at runtime
>   - `let` type annotation validation at runtime
>   - `let` narrowing coercion at runtime
> 
> **job.rs (8 tests): GOOD**
> - Job table tracking, reap updates status, pipeline reap, collect_done, slot reuse, find_by_pgid/pid, stopped status.
> 
> **signal.rs (0 tests): EXPECTED**
> - Signal handling is inherently difficult to unit-test. The implementation is thin (signal receipts + dispatch). No tests needed for the signal infrastructure itself, but integration tests for signal handler dispatch would be valuable.
> 
> 
> ### 2.2 Edge Cases
> 
> **Tested:**
> - Empty list truthiness, ExitCode(0) vs Int(0), leading-zero rejection, circular namerefs (depth limit), Tuple length mismatch in type validation.
> 
> **Missing:**
> - Empty Path truthiness (spec says "always non-empty by construction" but nothing enforces this).
> - Tuple with 0 or 1 elements (spec says "always &gt;=2 elements by construction" but `Val::Tuple(vec![])` is constructible).
> - Sum with empty tag.
> - List with single element vs scalar distinction.
> - Deeply nested try blocks.
> - Match with mixed glob and structural arms (should work since they're separate Pattern variants).
> - Concat of two lists with different lengths (returns Unit -- tested but edge case).
> - `for` loop over Unit (should iterate zero times).
> - `while` with body that `return`s.
> - Heredoc with empty body.
> - Redirect to non-existent directory.
> - Pipeline with single stage (optimization path, exec.rs:675-677).
> - `fn` with discipline name but no variable exists yet (exec.rs:1148-1150 creates it).
> 
> 
> ### 2.3 Negative Tests
> 
> **Almost completely absent.** The `parse_err` function in parse.rs is dead code. There are zero negative parse tests. On the exec side, there are only two implicit negative tests (match-no-match returns error, while-false returns non-success). Needed:
> - Unterminated quoted string
> - Unterminated command substitution
> - `let` with type mismatch
> - Readonly variable mutation
> - Immutable variable reassignment
> - Invalid redirect target
> - `ref` with nonexistent target (should it error?)
> 
> 
> ## 3. Invariant Soundness
> 
> ### 3.1 Tests That Pass For Wrong Reasons
> 
> **`exec::tests::redirect_output_creates_file` (line 1811-1819):** Currently FAILS. The test writes to a temp file and reads it back, but the file content includes test output from other parallel tests due to fd inheritance across forks. The test's `path` uses `temp_dir()` which is shared. The assertion `content.trim() == "hello"` fails because other test output bleeds in. This test is flaky by construction.
> 
> **`exec::tests::list_assignment` (line 1892-1896):** Tests that `x = (a b c)` produces `Val::Str("a b c")`. This is correct per rc heritage (bare assignment stores as Str), but the test doesn't verify the value is a *string* as opposed to a *list*. It asserts `Val::Str("a b c")` which does check the type -- good. But it should be contrasted with `let x = (a b c)` which would produce `Val::List`.
> 
> **`exec::tests::for_loop_runs` (line 1675-1681):** The test creates `let mut count = 0` but never uses it inside the loop or checks it afterwards. The test only verifies `run_status("for x in (a b c) { echo $x }")` succeeds. It doesn't verify the loop ran 3 times. This test claims to test "for loop runs" but only checks that the status was ok.
> 
> **`exec::tests::pipeline_works` (line 1875-1879):** Only checks status. Doesn't verify data flowed through the pipe.
> 
> ### 3.2 Tests That Don't Test What They Claim
> 
> **`exec::tests::lambda_creates_thunk` (line 1776-1780):** Tests that `let f = \x =&gt; echo $x` stores a `Val::Thunk { .. }`. This only tests parse + eval_value. It doesn't test forcing. There should be a test that forces the thunk with arguments and verifies the body runs correctly.
> 
> **`exec::tests::fn_with_args` (line 1700-1705):** Defines `fn say { echo $1 }` and calls `say world`, checking status. Doesn't verify `$1` was bound to "world".
> 
> **`exec::tests::set_discipline_fires` (line 1799-1806):** Good test -- verifies side effect of `.set`. But there's no corresponding `.get` discipline test.
> 
> ### 3.3 Vacuously True Assertions
> 
> **`exec::tests::if_true_branch` (line 1662-1665):** `run_status("if true { echo yes }")` checks `is_success()`. The `echo` builtin always succeeds, so this passes regardless of which branch was taken. Should verify via side effects that the then-branch (not else) actually ran.
> 
> **`exec::tests::if_false_else` (line 1669-1672):** Same issue -- `true` in the else always succeeds regardless of which branch ran.
> 
> 
> ## 4. Recommended Fixes
> 
> ### Critical (theoretical fidelity)
> 
> **F1. Implement capture-by-value for lambdas.** In `eval_value` for `Value::Lambda` (exec.rs:292-298), walk the body AST, collect free `$var` references, subtract the params, and snapshot current bindings into `captures`. In `force_thunk` (exec.rs:522-548), restore captures into the scope before binding params. This is the highest-priority fix -- it's the core of roundtable decision #4 and enables currying, psh's primary functional programming primitive.
> 
> **F2. Restore captures in `force_thunk`.** Add a loop before the param binding that does `self.env.set_value(name, val)` for each `(name, val)` in `captures`.
> 
> ### Important (spec fidelity)
> 
> **F3. Check dup() return value in `run_redirect`.** Before using `saved`, verify `saved != -1`. If dup fails, return an error status instead of silently corrupting fd state.
> 
> **F4. Prevent function definition inside `.get` readonly scope.** Either make `define_fn` check the readonly flag, or document this as a known limitation.
> 
> ### Test improvements (coverage)
> 
> **F5. Add structural match test.** Create a Sum value and match with structural arm, verifying the binding receives the payload.
> 
> **F6. Add try-abort-prevents-subsequent test.** `try { false; echo should_not_run }` -- verify echo never ran (via side effect on a variable).
> 
> **F7. Add try-boolean-exemption test.** `try { if false { } }` -- verify no abort.
> 
> **F8. Add .get discipline test.** Define `fn x.get { ... }`, access `$x`, verify discipline fired.
> 
> **F9. Add reentrancy guard test.** `fn x.set { x = $1 }; x = hello` -- verify no infinite recursion.
> 
> **F10. Add redirect save/restore test.** Redirect stdout to file, verify stdout is restored to terminal-equivalent after.
> 
> **F11. Add negative parse tests.** Use the existing `parse_err` function for: unterminated quote, unterminated `\`{`, missing `}` in block, `let` without `=`.
> 
> **F12. Fix `for_loop_runs` test.** Actually verify the loop body ran 3 times by accumulating into a variable.
> 
> **F13. Fix `redirect_output_creates_file` test.** Use a unique temp file name per test run (include pid or uuid), and use `tempfile` crate or unique naming to avoid collisions.
> 
> **F14. Add thunk forcing test.** Create thunk, force with args, verify body executed correctly.
> 
> **F15. Add immutability enforcement test.** `let x = 42; x = 99` should fail at runtime.
> 
> 
> ## Confidence Summary
> 
> | Finding | Confidence |
> |---------|-----------|
> | Capture-by-value not implemented (F1, F2) | Very high -- code explicitly says `captures: vec![]` |
> | RunOutcome mechanism correct | High |
> | CBV/CBN split correct | High |
> | Profunctor nesting correct | High |
> | dup() return unchecked (F3) | High |
> | .get readonly discipline correct | High |
> | ExitCode polarity correct | High |
> | try ⊕-&gt;⅋ mechanism correct | High |
> | fn-in-value silently degrades to Str | High |
> | Test coverage thin on exec.rs | High |
> | for_loop_runs vacuously true | High |
> | Function def in readonly scope leak (F4) | Medium |
> | return outside value context ambiguity | Medium |
> 
> ---
> 
> **The single most important finding: capture-by-value for lambdas is specified but not implemented.** The code at exec.rs:292-298 explicitly constructs thunks with empty captures and a misleading comment. This blocks the currying use case that is central to the thunk design. Fix F1+F2 first.</result>
